THE RED PAGE.
Q. NORA TIUS FL ACCU S DAY
TO-MORROW we shall celebrate the
1970th anniversary of the birth of
Horace (be the same more or less).
One trusts this notice will enable the
daily press to publish mendacious' memoirs
and misleading descriptive articles (as re-
cently for Nelson and Trafalgar). The
virile mothers 7r of our much-endurin°
school-children, also, may be asked to pro”
\ ide cakes to be eaten at the planting of
commemorative trees, while festive ginger-
beer can point suitable allusions to the
foils Bandusise.” A 1970th anniversary B
plainly a much more important occasion tnan
a mere 100th, and Horace is much more
useful to us than .Nelson. For lb trace's in-
spiration flows on from year to year, and the
deed of Irafalgar has now only naval signifi-
cance—or would have, if naval authorities
could agree precisely what its lessons are.
1 a ue, irafalgar altered history; but then
everything else that is past and gone altered
history. We are partly what we are to-day
because of Irafalgar, but also because of the
battle of Hastings, the fall of Babylon, the
evolution of anthropoid apes, and the shrink-
age in the sun ; and nobody thinks of follow-
ing a Hindu method of counting digits so
far as to set apart a period of time to be
foimally bored by these things. Too long a,
foolish human race has been wasting To-Day
in awe-stricken contemplation of Yesterday,
and worshipping its dead ancestors instead
of its living children.
One of the reasons why Horace may be
extolled on his 1970th birth-anniversary (and
at any other time) is this : he was among the
hrst to ' understand the significance of To-
Hon t waste it,” lie said in effect
bury Yesterday with it.” Horace realisec
fully that Yesterday had become an attrac-
tive subject for occasional poetry ; and
instead of looking back for famous anniver-
saries, he very sensibly looked forward for
his fame—not for a century, but in smcula
saeculorum ; and so he gets it, But he only
gets it because he deserves it—as much to-
day as yesterday, and no more to-day tha
to-morrow. The deeds of Nelson and Augu
tus are in the same limbo, and in process <
time one set will seem as musty as th
onoR 1 "' Trafalgar will be remembered i
as Actium is now—or not so mucl
unless a new Shakespear illuminate Nelso
and Emma as the old one has lit Antony anc
Cleopatra. But, short of a cataclysm, Horae
in 2905 will still console and inspire hi s
readers.
‘ ‘ And is there no inspiration in the heroi
virtue of Nelson ? ” To be sure there is
and precisely for that reason one ought no;
to keep it on a shelf that is dusted only ever
100 years. One should live with it an
absorb it and catch dishonest politicians wit i
it. (Just suppose N.S.W. Ministry chasin
Carliumentary thieves as energetically as Nel-
son chased a French fleet J) There was realiv
a great deal of sense in the savage idea tha
to eat an enemy was, to acquire his prowess
Nelson and every, other hero should
physically or symbolically, be eaten anc
digested and transformed into fresh force foi
jo-Da/s uses. What is the worth of waking
Australia up with a centenary alarum-clock
to admire Nelson, if next morning she ones
to sleep over naval defence and forgets al
about him? Heroes are like breakfast
foods : they may have all the splenetic
qualities that the advertisements allege, but
if t hey are to do you good you must swal-
low them and make them part of your flesh
and blood and the day’s work. Otherwise
tiie panegyric of the hero and the advertise-
ment of the food are on precisely the same
plane of unimportance, and to choose an
arbitrary day for hero-worship, at the end of
ten-lingers of years x ten-fingers of years, is
just as silly as to set apart merely the second
Monday after full moon for eating breakfast.
Religious anniversaries are in a similar
prehistoric pickle. Lent and Ramazan,
Christmas and Passover, the Time-Spirit
smiles mockingly at them all. They belong
to tne superstitious category of mystic
numbers and magic squares, syllables of
unction and words of power. Except, of
course, as they are convenient ways of
attaining a useful end ; except, maybe, as
hey are mnemonic devices in which weak
nimanity may And strength. Life is a
petrifying spring, and many people require the
consentaneous impulse of the crowd to force
diem for a moment to stand from under,
lhe work-habit, too, is more pernicious than
morphia : now and again we may see the
Mine-Spirit as healer, applying his calendar-
holidays as antidotes to the quick poison of
toil. And some festivals of the earth we as
earth-animals can never escape from. While
the Spring surges, there mp'st be a year
anniversary of rejoicing that the syste
of telluric hydrostatics can raise ev
the most bigoted vegetarian’s blooc
pressure. let the main argument is u
touched. A true symbol fully embodies t
substance; and saintly spirits once ce
prated, if the celebration were effectual
should recur no more than the bodies th
they figure. No self-respecting cannibal wi
devour the same corpse twice.
Then why be devoted to the spirit o
Horace ■ Well, one pleads only for i
moderate devotion, at a liberal shrine. It
a matter of valuations. We live so late
tue world, and so much knowledge has bee
stored for us, and so much more is beii
provided every day, that eclectic philosop]
is a matter of necessity as well as a mann
of wisdom. The banquet is splendid; b
people who try to partake of every cour
will meet resurrection-day with faces
yellow as the guests’ whom Horace pitiec
at a fashionable Roman dinner. Choos
then—what will do you good. If you don’
appreciate Horace, the argument sugges
that the fault is in you : for Horace ninetee
centuries are barracking. He has the pr
scriptive authority of tobacco, and womai
and liquor : all good medicine, comforting
Like these lie is close to the earth, and ver
human. Imagine him as another Sanch
I anza, a jolly little fat man, black-avisec
with rubicund cheeks and twinkling eyes
distilling the shrewd wisdom of peasant Soli
mons. But if that were all, one could absor
his message like Nelson’s, and return to hir
only for readjustments. It is becaus
Horace said his deed that he outlast
Nelson, and because of the way in which h
said it. His words make the deed liv
always with fresh lustre, always for To-day
His literary epithets amount to a perpetua
emission of Nelson-signals, and some are no
without their heroic message. For thong
Horace lived withdrawn, and seemed con
tent with simple pleasures, he had an ey
and an imagination to kindle for his country
The strongest condemnations of anti-social
conduct occur in his verses.
J*
The half has not been said, but this should
be said in safeguard : that from another
aspect Horatian retirement, the Horatian
philosophy, represent (like Thoreau’s) the
weak man’s excuses to himself and others
for declining combat. Horace was too wise
too young, because his pulse failed early
His literary decoration of the quiei
life is somewhat narcotic for the strenu-
ous business of the world. A com-
munity of Horaces in Australia would
lead a delightful existence doubtless—unti
the Japs came and blotted them out. Is tc
do better than to bo ?— remembering that to
be is to do. One could not argue it for the
individual, but the community is compelled
to dispense with argument. Life, to be
Life, must remain dynamic, with Turgenev’s
ni behind it, to “ fight on and damn it
all.” So, after all, we must stick to the
idea that Nelson and a thousand others
represent; and if the Nelson-label is the
most convenient for the idea, by all means
glorify Nelson. But not at a stated time
and season : at every time, all the seasons.

Geo. Bell and Sons (Lond.) give the desideratum
ot y ears a pocket text of Horace with a good
Lnghsh version on the page opposing. One
suspects that others also, tossed far from school-
day classics, may need for some portion of
Horace s work an interpreter or a dictionary :
not for the Odes well-conned, nor for the Art of
oetry, but for the Satires and Epistles with
virtue still to disclose. In this edition Coning-
ton s translation is taken almost throughout. One
thinks his Virgil was better ; yet the Horace is on
a good level, ail’d one can get critical interest in
attempts to improve it. Perhaps the worst of
Oonuigton is his habit of smoothing down the
coarse, vivid pictures that Horace sometimes
gives. In Odes 111/15, for example :
Illam cojit amor Nothi
Lasaivce similem liulere caprece ...
is spoilt by
No thus' love her bosom warms ;
She gambols like a fawn with silver feet.
Such malpractices are not numerous, yet still too
numerous. In some odes Conington is necessarily
surpassed by others. His version of IV/13, com-
mencing
The gods have heard, the gods have heard my prayer ;
Yes, Lijce ! you are grooving old, and still you struggle to
look fair...
compares ill with Calverlev’s
Byce, the gods have listened to my prayer,
Ihe gods have listened, Lyce. Thou art grey,
And still wmildst thou seem fair.
which, as Sendall suggests, hits the nail with
exactly the right force. Conington’s “Yes
Lyce ! with an exclamation, and his use of
‘you for ‘thou,” transpose the Latin into a
false key. Yet Conington has his triumphs too :
Vixi puellis nuper idoneus
et militavi non sine gloria:
nunc arma defunctumque hello
barbiton hie paries habebit.
(111/26) turns neatly into
For ladies’ love I late was fit,
And good success my warfare blest,
But now my arms, my lyre I quit,
And hang them up to rust or rest. ..
If we continue, Horace will appear characteristic-
ally. (“Links” means “torches.”)
Here, where arising from the sea
Stands Venus, lay the load at last,
Links, croivbars, and artillery,
Threatening all doors that dared he fast.
0 Goddess ! Cyprus mens thy sway,
And Memphis, far from Thracian snow :
Raise high thy lash, and deal me, pray,
That haughty Chloe just one blow !
As English verse that is of no account: its vir-
tue comes in fidelity to the Latin which it intro-
duces. If one relied upon the English alone, it
would be better to take one of the fragrant old-
fashioned paraphrases, which do translate the
spirit of Horace by mistranslating the letter.
Here is a 17th-century version of the close of 1/4 :
Since all the ivorld’s thus gay and free,
Why should not we ?
Let then accept our Mother Nature’s treat
And please ourselves with all that's meet:
Let ’s to th.e shady Rowers,
Where crown’d with gaudy Flowers
We ’ll drink and laugh away the gliding hours.
Trust me, Thyrsis, the grim Conqueror Death
With the same freedom snatches a King’s breath
And huddles the poor Slave
To's unknown Grave.
Tho’ we each day with cost repair
He mocks our greatest skill and utmost care,
Nor loves the Fair, nor fears the Strong,
And he that lives the longest dies but young;
And once depriv’d of light
We ’re wrapt in mists of endless Night.
Once come to those dark Cells of which ive 're told
So many strange Romanticlc tales of old,
No more shall Mirth and Wine
Our loves and wits refine.
No more shall you your Phyllis have,
Phyllis so long you’ve priz’d :
Nay, she too in the Grave
Shall lie like us despis'd.
Much of this is mere Horatian embroidery : yet
by force of losing his author the translator
gains him. Conington’s faithful rendering
is a means to understanding Horace in Latin, but
it is not Horace in English.
A hen Horace drops his jesting cloak and speaks
burning and prophetic words of the degradation
of Rome (111/G)
Non his juvmtus orta parentibus
Infedt cequor sanguine Punico...
Conington translates
Not such their birth, who stain'd for us
The sea with Punic carnage red . . .
Calverley :
Were those lads of such parents bred
Who dyed the seas until Punic blood ? . . .
but it is Dryden’s friend Roscommon who rises to
the height of Horace’s indignation :
’l’was not the Spawn of such as these
That Dy’d with Punick bloud the Conquer’d Seas 1
In this ode and elsewhere, by the way, the
edition under notice omits a passage—sometimes
the whole ode : a piece of foolishness. Horace is
not a writer to be expurgated, and the omissions
sensibly blot Conington’s work.
The book has not taken quite the right form. It is
bound in a good dark-red leather, with gilt top and rib-
bon. The gold stamping on the back, however, is unneces-
sarily descriptive: that the text is “complete with
Conington’s translation ” one learns from the title-page,
or for external advertisement the words can be printed on
? paper wrapper. The rule round “ Horace ”on the front
ns a decorative error. A worse fault is in the printing.
The type is neat, but has not been sufficiently inked, and
is grey throughout; the paper is too soft: a harder surface
would ha ve taken the ink better. The end papers are slightl v
too obtrusive ; and, except at the top for gilding, the
book’s edges should not have been cut. One stresses what
are conceived to be small faults, because this book is fit
to be a permanent treasure, and iiy that guise should fall
little short of perfection. \

A book of verse and prose by The Bulletin
contributor, Thos. Spencer (represented in The Bulletin
Reciter by “ How MeDougal Topped the Score ”) will be
published soon by N.S. W. Bookstall Co. A. C. Rowlandson
who stands for this Co. and the States Publishing Co., has
been responsible for the issue of quite a number of Austra-
lian publications this year. A packet of his view-books
and post-cards contains some creditable work. Choice
Views of Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide, Brisbane, Perth,
and Tasmania (65.) is the most comprehensive local ex-
ample of its class ; and is the kind of educational picture-
book that should he distributed widely over Britain. The
excellent views are sold in sections at a lesser price
Margaret Flockton’s Australian Wild Flowers (Is)
and Neville Cayley’s Australian Birds (Is.) rep-
resent our own art and industry, and for Xmas
despatch from Australia are preferable to anything of the
kind imported. Mr. Rowlandson, too, is rearing an in-
dustry in post-cards, of which he issues 64 series, all
giving employment to local designers and printers, and
some of them artistically true and worthv. The “ Bush
Life ” set, from pictures by J. Hutchins, and the “ Thea-
trical Celebrities,” are especially good, and in the former
there is real Australian value.
One of the protestants against N.S.W. Govt,
action in the Mitchell Library matter, E. W. O’Sullivan,
is the very last person who should protest. The Mitchell
Library should have been built years ago, and it is owing
to the action of E. \\. O’Sullivan and his colleagues in the
last N.S.W. Ministry that it was not built. In the last
hours of the 1901 session E. W. O’Sullivan rushed through
Parliament a Bill to provide 67,500?. to build a new
library for Sydney University; and according to the
evidence of John Perry, late Minister for Public
Instruction, before the Public Works Committee, it
was on account of this vote that the question’ of
erecting a building to accommodate the Mitchell Library
and the overcrowded Public Library was postponed.
Parliament would not stand two such votes. According
to the Govt. Architect, the estimated cost of the Univer-
sity Library has now risen from 67,500 1. to “about
75,000?.” This expenditure was ostensibly secured on
the ground of a bequest of “ about 30,000?."” by Thomas
Fisher. The University Senate originally had deter-
mined to spend 20,000?. of this on a building to be
called the Fisher Library ; but, nding that E. W. O’Sul-
livan had so little capacity for discerning the public
interest, it naturally took all the public money it could
get; and the Mitchell Library and the Public Library
were relegated by E. W. O’Sullivan to the background.
On Sydney University, by the way, N.S. Wales has now
®J'P° nd , ecl or guaranteed an expenditure of more thin
600 OOOf Apart from the University building and land
fej'dney University has now money and property invested’
and drawing' interest, approaching' a total of 1 000 0001 ■
and collects besides an income of some 800 of. a year in
students’ fees, and a yearly Govt, endowment of 50001.
Collins’s Graphic English Dictionary (Lond
Collins ; 3s. Gd seems to be put forward as a rival to
Chambers s and Annandale’s at the same price. One does
not admit the.rivalry. Good points in The Graphic are
the excellent printing and (for school use and quick
reference) the large type used for words. The coloured
illustrations, one was about to say, are neither here nor
there ; but giving- Collins the benefit of the quibble it mav
be admitted that they are there—that is beyond Australia'
1 ictures of British birds are practically of 'no use to us'
illustrations of foreign decorations of honour are of no
value to us. If Collins wants his dictionary to
sell m Australia by reason of the coloured pictures
he should insert Australian coloured pictures. The
large type has helped to make the book too bulky—not
too bulky if every line told, but too bulky considering
that a great part of the dictionary is rubbish. The charm
of Chambers’s or of Annandale’s dictionary is good edit-
ing. The business of directory compilation and definition
suits the Scotch nnnd, which distinguishes clearly and
labours thoroughly, Collins has a Scotch editor too but
he must have been changed at nurse. His arrangement
is bad One does not want half an inch devoted to
pallidly and pallidness, for example. Dependent words
in such a dictionary as this should come under the main
word and save space. Again, in such a dictionary as this
onedoesnot want words \\keshintiy ail, skint iq an and sliodi'
Die Graphic spells words incorrectly, e.g., trior for trier ■
tatooaga for tattooage. About half an inch in thickness
of the book is devoted to a list of proper names, nine-
tenths of which, for Australian use, might have been cut
out. The definitions here are not always good : e.g,:
Macquarie. Lachlan Macquarie, Governor 1809-21: A
New South Wales affluent of the Darling ; 350 m. long.
Unless you know, it is not clearly to be inferred from
this that Macquarie was Governor of N.S.W. All the
names of small English towns like Maidenhead and Morley
waste space for Australian use. Space is wasted also oil
Labe, an Arabian enchantress mentioned in The Arabian
flights; Mai, A hgelo, Italian antiquary and classical
scholar ; and hundreds of personages similarly obscure.
In the focal opinion the dictionary is- positively bad, and
comparatively very bad, for want of an editor witli brains
or a tree exercise of discretion.

Tiie suggestion 26 10/5) that the illustration of Ethel
Turner s books should be entrusted to Australian
designers moves one to ask whether Australians are
capable of drawing the adaptable children of the English
illustrator. For instance, the Australian girl and boy
(Lssm and Peter) of the cover-design and frontispiece to
Ethel Turner’s Little Mother Meg{ Lond., Ward, Lock ; no
date—? 1902) reappear as two American boys (Toddie and
t e ln f lwl Uspiece to the same publishers’ editions
of John Habberton’s Helen's Babies (Lond., 1905;
and 3903, in one vol. with Other People's Children).
In the Australian story the children have just had
a boat accident, and have swum out of a river fully
clothed. The illustrator indicates the moisture so in-
geniously that the same children can easily be accepted
as dry Americans. I don’t know whether they also figure
as boys of the Swiss Family Robinson or as Hans Ander-
sen’s lb and Christine, but the sex of one and the nation-
ality of both are so delightfully ambiguous that- there is
no particular reason why they' should not.
A selection of passages from The Rose-Garden
gr.Sa-.di is made by L. Cranmer-Byng in “ The
v\ lsdom of the East ’ series (Lond., Murray ; Is.
net). The translator seems to have little skill of
expression, but the rudeness of his versions adds a
certain salt of humour : they are plain, but whole-
some. Sa’di was borh in Shiraz about 1193, and
died 1291. Meanwhile, he was enslaved by Chris-
tians, and sent to work in the trenches at Tripoli.
There he was recognised by one of the chiefs of Aleppo,
who ransomed him for ten dinars and took him home and
married him to his daughter, with whom lie gave a dower
of a hundred dinars. The lady had a tongue of eloquence,
but not in the direction of poetry, and at length even the
gentle Sa’di turned upon her. Once, when reproaching
him, she said ; “Art thou not the man whom, my father
ransomed from the captivity of the Franks for ten
dinars?” He answered, “Yes, I am lie w’.io was re-
deemed for ten dinars and enslaved to thee for a hundred.’’
Sa’di’s poetry and wit shine even through the
English. This verse of Pantheism is sound doc-
trineto-day: Mis
The lovers by the Loved One all are slain ;
No voice can answer from the dead again.
Here is a pretty idea :
Green was the gay apparel of the woods,
Like festal robes on happy multitudes.
, A fragment suggesting non-interference in poli-
tics —bad doctrine : • j|
The king said ; “ Verily we are in need of one suf-
ficiently intelligent who is able to carry on the affairs of
the government.” He answered: “It is a sign of suf-
ficient intelligence not to meddle with such matters.”
“The red Gehenna of our dreams” may open
a vista. “Red Gehenna” is approved. So is
“ discordant blather.” " 3
None with an axe can mud from gravel part
As thy discordant blather breaks the heart.
Indeed, the more one looks at this little book,
the more one finds : it is worth the money. Here,
for example :
A fellow with a rasping voice happened to be reading
the Koran, when a holy man passing by asked him what
ho received a month for so doing.' He answered :
“Nothing.” Then added the holy one: “Why takest
thou this trouble?” He replied: “I am reading for
God’s sake.” Answered the holy man : “ Then for God’s
sake do not read ! ” 3
Or again, here is a source of some of Goethe’s
wisdom :
Dost want thy sire’s inheritance ?
Acquire his business ways,
Since all the gold that feeds thy glance
May melt within ten days.
And again :
A philosopher was teaching boys, and said to them :
“ O darlings of your fathers, learn a trade, since no re-
liance may be placed upon the possessions and riches of j
the world : for silver and gold are a source of peril, s lice
either a thief may steal them at once, or the owner waste j
them by degrees ; but a profession is a living spring and
wealth enduring. Although a professional man may lose
his fortune, he need not grieve, for his knowledge is
wealth of itself, and wherever he go he will he honoured,
and sit in .the upper seat; but he who has no calling will
glean the crumbs and suffer want.”
This should console the junior bar. And when
Sa’di had finished his book he affirmed big
originality thus :
The Book of the Gulistan has been finished, and the aid
of Allah besought. By the favour of the Almighty—may
His name be hallowed ! —throughout the work the custom
of writers to insert verses borrowed from the ancient
poets has not been followed.

Health! The Most Vital Interest.
LEARN FROM OTHERS’ EXPERIENCE.
The Case of
Mr. E. W. OAVIB.
(By a Sydney Keporter.)
It is safe lo say that the people
tvho have the most vital interest in
the following particulars arc those who
hnd themselves in a similar position
lo that occupied some seven years ago
by Mr. Edward Walter Davis, painter,
of Kenwick street. Marnckville, who
addressed these remarks to an inter-
viewer :
•‘lt was whilst 1 was living at
Leichhardt that I was so ill with
painters colic, and also rheumatism,
and 1 can give you my word that for
several months it was a doubtful mat-
ter liow things would go with me.
Both those complaints at once would
be enough to kill most men, and it
is no exaggeration to say that they
nearly caused my death. My digestive
organs were also in a terribly bad
way, so that, you see, I was suffering
from a serious complication of ail-
ments.’'
"bid you seek medical advice, Mr.
Davis ?’
Uh, yes ; 1 saw a doctor, and for
some time remained under his treat-
ment. which, unfortunately, proved to
be utterly u.-ckss. 1 took enough
medicines to do me good in thoso
days if they had been of the right
kind, but evidently they were not.
and the agony 1 was continually suf-
fering was simply intolerable. The
rheumatic pains in my legs had quite
a crippling effect upon me, for 1 had to
refrain from moving about as much as
possible because of any exertion ag-
gravating my alflictions. Nearly all
my joints were as stiff and sore as
could be, so you can imagine how dif-
ficult it was for me to do anything ;
but so far as doing anything was
concerned I hadn't the inclination, for
I felt as if the wholo world l had closed
its dioors against me. In my melan-
choly frame of mind i could see no-
thing excepting in the gloomiest of
colours, and all there seemed in this
life was to struggle hopelessly along
against overwh luring adversities. liad
1 been told that in a short while I
was going to die I’m sure it would
have been good news, for 1 was really
anxious to leave all my unhappiness
behind me.”
“It seems as if you had come to the
conclusion that you were not going
lo get better
•‘.lndeed, I could think nothing else,
because I could not get even a little
relief. My agonies had me worked up
to such a pitch of exasperation that
if anybody came and spoke to me I
could sva cely be civil to them. And
what fearful nights I put in week af-
ter wees. ! frequently no sleep from
tho time I went to bed till I got up
again next day, and I’d like to know
who could have got to sleep if they
had felt as I did, with the sensation
in my stomach as u everything was
being drawn up into a knot and then
screwed round and round. Combined
with these alflictions I had pains un-
der my heart, which caused me more
anxiety than enough, for what chance
had I of effecting a recovery if I had
heart disease as well, and the way
palpitation occurred made me think
that organ w a s diseased. This was
not all, though, as my chest had
heavy pains in it which’ affected my
breathing, and not a day passed but
what .1 was nearly distracted with a
headache. If 1 took a small quantit\
of food—and my appetite was too
poor to eat much —it simply turned
sour in the stomach and gave rise
to fits of belching, and a feeling as
if I wanted to be sick would come on.
Oh, there was no child’s play about
my illness, and I often say what a
lucky man 1 was ever to get rid of
“That is what I would like to hear
about. l)o you mind telling me ?”
‘‘Not at all. I am only too glad
for it was such a wonderful affair, as
most of the neighbours round where 1
lived could tell you. They knew Low
i suffered, and when they saw that I
was being pulled round they were very
curious to know what was the came
of it, and 1 told them Clements Tonic
was the medicine that was saving my
life, which was the truth. Besides
"hat I’ve already stated, I used to
get feelings of giddiness very often,
and my tongue was never clean, but
covered with slime, and in my mouth
the taste was like bile ; but when J
had taken a couple of bottles or so of
Clements Tonic I was not a bit like
that, and the old feelings of sickness
alter eating were gone. That was a
good deal to be grateful for, but no-
thing compared to what followed. J
used to dream a lot whenever 1 ob-
tained some sleep, and would wake
up in terror as if 1 had been dream-
ing of murder or something equally
bad, but Clements Tonic had such a
calming eflect upon me that when 1
slept 1 did so soundly, and felt as
fresh as paint when 1 woke up. My
nerves might have had a lot to do
with those restless nights, for before
using Clements Tonic they- used to
twitch dreadfully ; afterwards they
were firm and steady, and the little
involuntary jumps I used to give when
lying down were no longer in evi-
dence. 1 was not the same man at
all when I had been taking Clements
Tonic a few weeks. The acute rheu-
matic pains had been gradually be-
coming less and less .painful, and in
gxxxl time they died out altogether,
tiuch a thin<r might seem impossible,
but, on my honour, it is true, and
when 1 speak ol the colic pains I can
say exactly tho same thing—they were
done away with ny Clements Tonic
once and for all. Many of my work-
mates have reaped tho benefit of my
experience, for when I have told them
what cured me they got Clements
Tonic for themselves, and they havo
always spoken of the results in satis-
factory terms.”
“And how did your digestive organs
fare ?”
“Couldn’t possibly havo been made
more healthy than Clements Tonic
made them, which means all tho dif-
ference between a fife of joy and a
life of sorrow. It meant that my eyes
were no Jong'cr heavy and dull as
they had been, and it meant that I
could always cat heartily without fear
of being ill afterwards, for Clements
Tonic made my internal organs do
their work more perfectly than evor.
That 1. had got very thin could only
be expected, but under the treatment
of Clements Tonic 1 soon put on more
Ilcsli than J had boforo my illness, and
the additional strength 1 acquired was
amazing. My wife was also very ill
once, and Clements Tonic did her a
wonderful amount ol good, which
shows that it can be usod equally well
by either sex, to whom 1 unfailingly
recommend its use when circumstances
require.”
“And can we make use of this con-
versation ?”
“ Undoubtedly. You are welcome to
publish every sentence in any way you
prefer.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There Was a Cry.
From the sounding bass of a prophet’s tone
A treble of grief is leaping ;
Mangled, her. dying children moan—
’Tis the voice of Rachel weeping.
Under the stars in the listening night
An old world voice is crying,
Weird echoes still replying
From caves in G'armel’s mountains :
“ O ! that mine eyes were fountains
That I might weep for those that sleep
Outcast along the city :
The Bear has torn the folk forlorn
And there was none to pity.
The snow once white is foul to tread
Dyed with a life-tide gory ;
The dogs make free among the dead,
The sun is dark, the moon blood-red,
And ‘ Ichabod ’ our story.”
From the moving bass of a prophet’s tone
A note of woe is leaping
Old griefs to bitter mourning grown—
’Tis the voice of Rachel weeping.
Under the stars in the listening night
An old world voice is crying,
The Syrian oaks are sighing,
The grass of Ramah shivers :
“ Oh ! that mine eyes were rivers
That I might weep while others sleep,
When dawn gilds spire and steeple—
Weep for the slain on the Russian plain,
The daughters of my people.”
From the deepening bass of a prophet’s tone—
As a sword from the scabbard leaping—
A cry, of cries most poignant known,
’Tis the voice of Rachel weeping.
Maoriland.
ROSLYN.
A Scotch Song.
This morning, ah ! the summer skies they held no
hint of rain;
This morning, ah ! my happy heart was still un-
used to pain.
The cows were in the pasture land, the grass
caressed my feet,
And clink ! clink ! went the milking pails the
bonny kine to greet.
Did the fairies send a warning ? If so, ’twas all
in vain,
The lilting of that wee white bird that flew along
the lane.
This morning how his words of love went singing
through my heart !
The words he whispered me last night where we
two stood apart;
The others romped within the light, but I, the
crofter’s lass,
Stood by the great laird’s bonniest son and watched
the dancers pass.
Oh ! I am lowly born indeed, and he is braw and
proud,
Yet his should never be the hand to help me to
my shroud !
For well he pressed my roughened palms and
held them close again,
And begged me for another kiss (dear God, I tire
of pain !).
I want to be a child once more, as but a year ago,
Before I heard his horse’s feet come stealing
through the snow.
And yet this morning I was glad—how foolish and
how vain ;
I laughed to see that feckless bird go flying down
the lane!
Oh ! was it love that flew away, so winsome and
so white ?
I saw him as he passed me down the windy croft
to-night.
A lady riding by his side —he touched her finger-
tips
With just the reverence and the love he once had
for my lips !
And yet the folly of it all—for me, the crofter’s
lass —
The maid who only stands aside to curtsey as they
pass !
Oh ! will the Summer die at last, and Autumn
come again,
And Winter, with its piercing cold, to ease my
burning brain ?
To think I once was glad to live before I heard
him say,
“Yes, only a rough crofter’s lass, but pretty—in
her way.”
To know that he should sneer to her ! Yet I forgive
the sneer,
But not the way he bent and smiled, and whispered
in her ear.
This morning the clear Summer skies held never
hint of rain,
And yet the drops are pattering now, and racing
down the pane;
The cows are lowing in the byre, the pasture lands
are wet,
They tell me that he weds with her, and that I
should forget.
I think a sign was sent to me that true love is in
vain
When that wee bird went lilting by along the
dewy lane S
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

Sydney has evolved a tribe of arboreal police*
men. Last week two policemen, by concealing
themselves up a tree, managed (without Informa*
tlon received) to arrest two out of three persons
suspected of a park robbery. The third escaped.
Perhaps, if the police had been as ready with
their feet as with their heads, he too would
have been bagged.
Sydney Cky Council asks for authority to
resume ;and demolish slums, with a special eye
to Wexford=street. Bruce Smith is explaining
that the clean-up may create complications with
friendly As.atic races.
The arboreal policeman should make
things sure by Jumping down on his
victims.
Our ally from the window : “ Whaffor you
shlftee my dirt?”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy .” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
dlivays is.

dential.

MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions

destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for

desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 7, 1905.
JOE’S CUP.
All he could say, therefore, was this : “ If they want to
fill up the cup of iniquity to the brim, then let them do
it. Matters will, by conduct of this description, be
brought the more rapidly to a conclusion, and the situa-
tion ended.”— Premier Carruthers of New South Wales,
Last week a small incident happened in the
Senate. The vote for the maintenance of the
Governor-General’s residence at Sydney was cut
down—or rather the Senate suggested that it
sliould be cut down—as a hint towards its final
abolition. Hitherto Australia has been put to
the expense of maintaining Government House,
r, , .. a ~
Sydney, as an extra or superfluous residence
for the Governor-General. He doesn’t want it,
and he hardly ever has tune to use it.
When Parliament is sitting he requires to be
most of the tune in Melbourne, and when
N.S. Wales allows the Federal capital to be
fixed he will have to be at the Federal capital.
The rest of the time he wanders round a good
deal, and he has never shown the least desire to
be burdened with an extra household at Sydney.
But some Sydney politicians declared that the
poor old Ma State would be wronged and blighted
if the Governor-General didn’t have a residence
there, which was much of a piece with the same
crowd’s demand that the first Governor-General,
when on his way out here, should not be allowed
to stretch his legs ashore at Perth, or Albany, or
Adelaide, or Melbourne, because the poor old Ma
State would be grievously slighted and struck by
the left hook of opprobrium unless his Ex.’s boot-
sole first touched Australian soil in Sydney. No
other State demanded that the Governor-General
should have a costly and superfluous extra residence
on its territory, but the Cabbage-Tree Mob of Syd-
ney politicians makes N.S.W. quite unlike every
other State. So N.S.W. turned out its State
Governor to make room for the greater Excellency
who, as a rule, didn’t come, and both the
State and the Commonwealth paid a good deal
of money needlessly over the matter. At the
same time the present N.S.W. Government has
threatened more than once to resume posses-
sion of Government House, and to eject the
Governor-General who isn’t there, so ap-
parently it doesn’t really want him after all.
In fact, it has gone so far as to give notice
of ejection. At last the Senate grew tired and
proposed to drop the unsatisfactory arrangement.
It wasn’t a “Victorian conspiracy” this time;
the only "Victorian who voted gave his voice for
keeping on the payment for the empty house
rather than aggravate the poor old Ma State.
But a combination from the other four States
reckoned that it was time to save a little money.
On being appealed to later on not to aggravate
the poor old Ma State, the combination thought
better of it and withdrew its suggestion, and the
affair dropped.
* X * *
Out of this matter arose Joe Carruthers’ dark
observations about the cup of iniquity, and the
possibility of that drinking vessel becoming seri-
ously over-crowded, also his threat about some
“situation ” which won’t last much longer. Pre-
sumably the little man is again hinting at
secession, and this constant hinting at secession
on the part of a small, skimpy solicitor who
apparently hasn’t the beginning of an idea how to
secede grows wearisome. Carruthers’ favorite
attitude these times is that of the boy bushranger
who buys a cheap Belgian pistol after reading
“ Thunderbolt,” and goes out and scowls darkly
at a heifer and says “A time will come.”
He is a blowing and puffing political in-
fant, who looks as if he were almost liable
some day to run up a barricade of two
boot-trunks and a perambulator in his back-yard,
and throw off his allegiance where he is sure no
one will hear him throwing it. He is the mental
duplicate of the young man described by Dickens
who rose in the interior of the United States, and
said, in a deep voice, “ Produce your British
lion,” and laughed a deep bitter laugh when the
animal didn’t at once enter the hall. Yet Carru-
thers’ professed desire to throw off the yoke when
he can’t find any yoke ; and his attitude of Ajax
defying the lightning on a fine day, mingled with
that of Napoleon crossing a dead level range of
Alps; and his habit of letting down his back hair
and crying out that the jug is overflowing, or the
coal-scuttle is pretty full, or the cup will
presently slop over into the saucer—all
these things are not really worth the
price of admission. They make a poor show,
and they can all be heard any night at Bland
Holt’s theatre, where Albert Norman does them
a great deal better. Of course, no one dreams for
a moment that Carruthers’ tremendous remarks
about the “cup,” and the “situation,” and all the
rest of his baby bosh, really mean anything.
These observations only rank in popular estima-
tion with the ten-year-old school kid’s smudgy
but bloodthirsty essay on pirates, or the threaten-
ing attitude of Augustus Moddle, Dickens’
youngest boarder, when he rose up in Mrs.
Todgers’ hash-tower and threatened to demolish
the eldest boarder with firearms. He also said some-
thing to the effect that the time had nearly come,
and when it did arrive the corpse of a certain
fish salesman’s bookkeeper would accumulate on
the premises. But still it may be that the public
does Carruthers an injustice in taking this view,
for even the very youngest boarder’s gun goes off
at times, and the mildest solicitor, if he talks
secession long enough, may be driven at last either
to try to secede or to hide himself in the cellar till
his own remarks blow over.
■* * * *
This paper urges PremierCARRUTHERS toconsider
these possibilities and to do something definite—
either to shoot and be done with the matter, or to
stop talking a|M waving his gun in the ozone. If the
bath-tub of iniquity, or whatever the vessel may
be, isn’t yet quite full of iniquity let it either be
considered full or else let it be filled up with
water. Them some real business may commence.
The country wants to see its Joseph starting to
secede in dead earnest, so that it may stand round
and admire the circus—it hasn’t any real hope,
but it wants the circus all the same. It wants to
see Joseph build his barricade, or hoist his red
flag, or introduce his Secession Bill, or haul
Prime Minister Deakin before the High Court,
or throw road metal at Bill Lyne, or do anything
at all except whine in a corner and talk about the
injury done to the poor old Ma State. He has
found so main alleged injuries done to the poor
old Ma State that if he allows the State Parlia-
ment to close up without striking a blow at
the Hydra head of iniquity, or throwing a
ball of paper at the monster of oppression,
the public will be disappointed. If another session
passes in the same futile way, the public may even
be angry. And if Joseph goes on persuading the
people of Sydney that the poor old Ma State is
such a badly-trodden-on female they may—though
it seems hardly likely—become really serious about
secession, and then they will sack Joseph for not
knowing how to secede, and give his job to some-
body else. If New South Wales is so much in-
jured as Joseph says it is, then it is a mighty poor
Joseph who won’t do something more than whine
in its defence. At present the imaginary wrongs
of New South Wales are Joseph’s sole political
stock-in-trade. He lives on his imaginary valor
as the individual who confronts the imaginary lion
of Federal tyranny, and he excites a certain thrill
by his perpetual assurance that things are hasten-
ing to a crisis, and that the day of reckoning
is fast approaching, and that tlie “situation”
is near its end, and that the curtain is about to go
up, and the pop-gun is on the verge of going off.
But he can’t go on like that for ever, and there
are so many ridiculous possibilities in the busi-
ness that, unless he is really going to revo-
lushe some day soon, it would pay him better
to get a real State policy and live on that. If
the people don’t believe his tale of wrong, and
if he still goes on talking, they will end by
giving him the sack for being an irritating circus
ass, and an animal with much dreary repe-
tition in his bray, and a great excess of bray in
his repetition. And if they do believe him they
will demand some day that he go forth and tear
up the alleged lion of Federal oppression as if it
were so much wet blotting paper, and produce the
carcase of the animal as an evidence of good faith.
And no one has seen Joseph tear up a fly, let
alone a lion. Besides there isn’t any lion.
THE TENDER MERCIES OF ANOTHER
SHARK.
In connection with the split in the N.S.W. Rail-
way Commission, the right-thinking people and
papers are skilfully dodging the main issue. The
Railways wanted a large quantity of coal, chiefly
for the western lines. This fuel used to be sup-
plied at the pit at Lithgow for less than ss. per
ton. Years ago the Labor party proposed that
the department should run its own coal mines. It
pointed out that the Railways were by far the
largest consumers in the State, that their demand
for coal was constant, and-that^-even if they
did not sell an ounce of mineral, they could
easily keep a couple of large Western
pits going. The right-thinking people and
papers scoffed at the proposal, of course, as a gross
interference with Private Enterprise. That a
grosser interference with both Private and
Public Enterprise might happen in the
future didn’t occur to them; or, if it did
occur to them, they carefully stowed the danger
away in some corner where it wouldn’t be noticed.
Yet something of the sort began to happen
promptly. Competition, which the right-thinking
people were positive would ensure a fair deal
for the Railways, was rolled out almost flat under
the wheels of an association which the coal-sup-
pliers formed ; and the price of coal moved up
steadily. But there were still some mines that
did not come into the Combine, and these tendered
at something like the old price, whereupon the
Combine promptly set to work to knock them out.
* * * *
For close upon seven years it did its
work effectively. The Railways during nearly
the whole of this time were not paying,
and to make up the deficit the people had to be
taxed. Between June, 1901, and June, 1904, the
department showed a loss of £1,067,260, which
had to be made good out of either taxation or
loans. Meantime, the farmers in the country
were told that no more concessions could be made
to them, and the manufacturers in the cities were
oppressed by heavy freights. The State had kept
out of the business of mining coal for its own use,
on the plea that it would* interfere with Private
Enterprise ; and the resul£was that the State was
forced by the Combine into paying such a price
that, in turn, it had to interfere with Private
Enterprise in a hundred different ways, and even
then couldn’t come out square. At the beginning of
the financial .tear 1904-5, however, something hap-
pened. A couple of collieries not in the Combine
tendered for part of the supply for a year, and
one of them got a contract at something like 3s. a
ton less than the Combine’s price—a difference
on the year’s supply of £7OOO. That lasted for 12
months. But the combine wasn’t dead yet. It
next tendered to supply 130,000 tons a year at
7s. 3d. per ton, and 45,000 tons at 4s. 3d. per ton.
These two lots of coal—one at 7s. 3d., the other at
4s. 3d.—would come out of the same group of
mines ; the whole explanation of the remarkable
difference in price was that the four-and-three-
penny coal was “for depots at present supplied
by the Irondale colliery,” the Irondale colliery
being the mine that hadn’t joined the Combine,
and which had dared to supply the railways at the
old price of about 3s. less than the Combine’s
monopoly price. In other words, where there
was no competition the Combine charged 7s. 3d.
per ton; but where there was competition the Com-
bine charged only 4s. 3d.—until it could kill the
competition. As chairman Oliver, of the Rail-
way Commission, put it in a minute —
While competition was available the Lithgow associa-
tion supplied coal at a low rate some seven years ago, but
when that competition ceased, then prices went up to an
unreasonable figure, which we have had to pay during
recent years. The revival of competition last year by our
acceptance of the tender for Irondale coal had the effect
of bringing down the Lithgow association for next year
from 7s. 3d. to 4s. 3d. for 45,000 tons, with the clear in-
tention of cutting out the Iropdale, as indicated in their
tender, but for the further quantity of 130,000 tons, they
maintained their high price of~7s. 3d. per ton. The reduc-
tion in price was, therefore, solely due to the competition
which, in my judgment, it was desirable to continue.
# It * #
But Chairman Oliver was overruled ; on the
plea that the Irondale coal was not as good, a
three years’ contract was made with the Combine
for 175,000 tons per annum at a price which works
out at 6s. sd. per ton all round—2s. 2d. per ton,
that is, in excess of what the Combine was pre-
pared to supply coal for under competitive con-
ditions. Thus on the whole contract the State
will pay £57,000 more than the same quantity
would have cost under competitive conditions.
The acceptance of the contract means, no doubt,
that competition will be killed stone dead, so
that, if the State does not take steps to open up
its own mines in the next three years, the price
will go up to the old 7s. 3d. when the present con-
tract runs out. In fact, having killed competition
—having taught interloping Private Enterprise
a sharp lesson—the Combine will possibly
put up its price to Bs. 3d. And, hav-
ing helped to choke competition, the State
will have to pay what the Combine demands.
The Railways must have coal, and, having helped
to put the coal-supply securely into the hands of a
monopoly, they will have to pay just what the
monopoly calls for. Out of the claws of the octo-
pus which the State has itself helped to create there
is now only one sure way. It can obviously no
longer depend upon competition. It knows also
what happens when there is no competition—
Chairman Oliver’s report puts that plainly. In
that dilemma it must prepare to mine its own
coal. To have neglected that plan for so long is
a serious reflection upon the management of the
Railways—it was merely a concession to the stupid
party which has made a bogey of Socialism and a
fetish of Private Enterprise. But the question of
Socialism doesn’t come into this matter at all. The
ownership and control of the Railways is a well-
established feature of public policy, and the State’s
business now is to run them on business lines.
There is certainly no well-conducted business
establishment anywhere that, for one of its
essentials, would so completely put itself at the
mercy of a monopoly as the Railways have done
in this case.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Fading Memory of a Drunk.
“ The truth had better be out,” H. C. Hoover, a
member of the firm of Bewick, Moreing & Co.,
told Westralian Ministers and others last week —
The ore reserves to-day are only the remnants of the
rich ore bodies above the 1200 ft. level. Another three or
four years would see them depleted, and, if nothing came
forward to replace those mines, the gold output must
fall by at least 400,000qz. annually.
The Bulletin has been saying these things ever
since the situation called for the remark ; now,
on the eve of his departure from the country’,
the expert member of the firm which is sup’-
posed to know more about Westralian mining than
any other two put together, repeats the warn-
ing. This paper isn’t concerned for the moment
with the mining speculator; he, at least, is sup-
posed to know what a mine is. This paper is
concerned now simply with the State. What is it
going to do about the matter? It has been living
m a fools’ paradise. The other day it gave a
thumping majority to a Premier and Treasurer,
who said the prospect of a £323,000 deficit for the
current year did not alarm him in the least. For
the past three years the State has had a chronic
deficit, and every year the disease has grown
worse. Meantime, every shilling that Cohen
would lend has been borrowed—latterly on most
disastrous terms —to carry out works in the dis-
tricts which, according to expert Hoover, will
either peter out or shrivel up within a few years.
This year over £2,000,000 in dividends'.'will be dug
outof Westralian mines and put into the pockets of
mostly-foreign people. These people are rushing
the gold out of the country with all possible
speed ; in four or five years the cream will be
gone ; unless other discoveries are made, only the
skim-milk will be left. And instead of making
these people pay—these people who are rushing
out the most-easi! y-got wealth—they are permitted
to escape taxation to such an extent that deficits
are chronic. They are dragging the gold out as
fast as engines can do it for them ; and Westralia
is actually borrowing money to buy the engines
for them to do it with. Presently the bill will
come in—and the people who are left with the
skim-milk will have to pay it. If ever there was
a country in the world which ought to resolutely
set itself to pay its way, that country is West-
ralia. It is a country which has been simply roll-
ing in riches, and, instead of taking a fair propor-
tion of those riches and using them to develop its
resources, it has borrowed for the purpose, and
the present Ministry proposes to go on borrowing;
and the future generation, which will have to
meet the bill, will be a generation for whom the
riches will be only the memory of a drunk that
their spendthrift, shiftless fathers had.
Bent’s Unsettled Settlement.
The Bent Government is evidently backing-up
its Closer Settlement Board in an attempt to bluff
Victoria re the Overnewton Estate scandal. A
few weeks ago one of the Government valuers
(Mr. John M’Ewan) wrote to the Age stating
what he had done towards warning the Lands
Department against purchasing the Overnewton
property for closer settlement purposes. In his
report to the Minister he said: —
Unless the owners are willing to excise 5900 acres, and
allow the Government to purchase 3200, being the only
portion suitable for settlement, it would be better for the
Government not to purchase the property at all.
This was in July last year, four months after the
estate as a whole had been vainly offered at
auction. The Closer Settlement Board came
into being on January Ist. this year, and
early in May the Board bought all the
land—the bad along with the middling—
that M‘Ewan had reported on. Six months
elapsed ere this Overnewton country was divided
into lots and submitted for settlement, and then
the accuracy of the valuer’s judgment was proved
by the fact that 5400 acres, out of the 5900 which
he had condemned, were left on the Government’s
hands— i.e., the Board has purchased some £30,000
worth of land which doesn’t invite settlers. When
valuer M‘Ewan’s revelations were mentioned in
the Vic. Assembly they were met with an out-
rageous bluff. The Minister tried to discredit
the critic by alleging that his valuation had been
£3 per acre more than the Board paid for Over-
newton. And there were other incorrect com-
ments passed on other valuations, with a view to
taking the wind out of M ‘Ewan’s sails. Further
correspondence in the Age shows the valuer to
have been a thoroughly capable expert in all cases
where the Government employed him. As regards
the Overnewton estate he gives facts as follows: —
I valued 9143 acres at £5 4s. Bd. per acre, and as the
Board purchased at £5 10s. my valuation was ss. 4d. less
than the price actually paid, and not £3 per acre more
as stated in error by the Minister. The Closer Settlement
Board, or possibly the Minister, has possession of this
report, and the above fact can be promptly verified by
reference to it. I have only to add that I cannot see why
so much trouble should be taken to defend a purchase
which is admittedly a blunder. It is not a question of
price or valuation so much as the suitability for closer
settlement. The price paid for it is not an outside one
for one purpose — viz., light sheep-grazing, for which the
land has been used during the last 60 years. Oats or
wheat, or any other cereal, would have as much chance of
growing on the 5900 acres as they would have on the
metalled portion of the St. Kilda-road.
This is the story of a man who has nothing to
gain by telling unpleasant truths, whereas the
Bent Government is deeply interested in the
endeavor to hide any blunders that reflect failure
on the useless and incapable Closer Settlement
Act, and it would seem that the bluff is not con-
fined to the Government. The C.S. Board has
champions among the very people who were
expected to visit its notable sin upon the Govern-
ment’s head.

“ Lobbying.”
In view of the recent disclosures of the lobbying
done by importers in order to persuade the
Australian Parliament to destroy or discourage
native industries, The Bulletin again asks that
a Commission be appointed to find out what
inducements the importers held out in order to
secure votes. In the “Balata” belting, case it
was set forth in quite a series of letters that the
importers “worked” the two Houses of Parlia-
ment in order to secure a great reduction of
duties. Before that, in the matter of the spirit
duties, the importers’ agent, Aloysius Fox,
brazenly sued his employers, the importers, in an
Australian court for the price of his services in
persuading members to so vote as to completely
cripple the Australian spirit industry. The boast
that he had crippled it was made openly in Ins
own correspondence. It is certainly time Parlia-
ment held a searching investigation to find out
how these things are done. “ Lobbying” in the
United States is reputedly a bald, naked and
unashamed process of buying votes.. Now that
the importer has started to “lobby” in Australia,
it is worth while finding out how the thing is done
here. The Australian manufacturer is mostly too
poor to buy more than an impecunious member or
two, but it is only necessary to consider York and
Clarence and Pitt-streets in Sydney, and the
neighborhood of Flinders, Cfcllins and Bourke-
streets in Melbourne to see that the importer is
rich enough to buy a whole Parliament for cash, it
he wants to do it and if the Parliament is for sale.
And some of these rich men, by their own show-
ing, have sent agents to approach members ol
Parliament, and these agents, by their own
showing, have persuaded members of Par-
liament to change their views, and turn a more
favorable eye on the importers’ interests. It may
be that it is the eloquence, the sterling character,
and the commanding intellect of the agent that


does it —even though the agent may afterwards
have a squalid squabble with his employers in
Court about the price of his services in the way of
crippling an Australian industry. But if the
country hears much more about the successful
lobbying done by rich men’s agents to get damage
done to Australian industries, it will begin to
wear its tongue in its cheek and to talk plainly
and coarsely about members being bought. It is
impossible to quite believe that the importer who
pays an agent to undermine Australian industries
would stick at paying a politician. Parliament
would do well to inquire into the matter before
ribald accusations get fairly started.

The Apotheosis of the Jap,
[For The Bulletin.]
He’s the wonder of the nations,
Is the tricky little Jap,
Hailing from those eastern islands,
Marked in yellow on the map—
(Off shore from Mongol Asia on the map).
And the nations Trans-atlantic greet with adula-
tion frantic
Every circumstance romantic that’s connected
with the Jap.
He is brave and he is clever ;
He’s inscrutable and keen ;
He is all and more than ever
Any other race has been—
(Any other nation ever yet has been).
Man of virtues monumental is this splendid
Oriental.
He is brave, heroic, gentle, imperturbably serene.
He is temp’rate and forbearing;
He is honest, true, and just;
Given not to senseless swearing—
Given not to breaking trust —
(Save only and excepting when he must!).
And tho’ the subject be distressing, let us straight-
way be confessing,
That, by Buddha’s special blessing, he is wholly
free from lust!
In his private life ascetic,
In his public life sublime,
He is never apathetic,
He’s “ extended ” all the time !
(Striving ever for the highest all the time).
And to judge from words emphatic, penned by
journalists ecstatic,
Well, the oily Asiatic hasn’t very far to climb !
m.l. Sinclair S. Clark.

The Blessings of Asiatic Labor.
Even the Asiatic papers now admit the failure of
the Chinese' labor experiment in S’Africa. A
recent Englishman (Calcutta) comments: —
Considering how completely all the prognostications of
the advocates of Chinese labor in South Africa have been
falsified, it is not altogether astonishing that a certain
amount of resentment should be rising among that part
of the community which gains nothing from the importa-
tion, but which stands in greater danger from the out-
breaks which are constantly taking place than the mine-
owners themselves.
Salvation by Chinese labor—for S’Africa and Aus-
tralia—is now only preached by ignorant English-
men and worse than ignorant Australian Tories.
And even the Englishmen are beginning to find out
things. London Daily Mail, the other day,
printed from a special woman correspondent this
about the Reign of Terror which the Chow has
brought to S’Africa :
Everywhere it is “ the Chinese Terror.” In the town,
folks look as if they want to clap their bands for joy as
the Johannesburg mounted policeman—very smart and
well-groomed in his uniform, with a magnificent piece of
horseflesh under him —rides down towards the Law
Courts with a string of Chinese deserters in tow in blue
blouses, long black pig tails, and expressions on their
faces of supremest indifference to whatsoever their fate
may be. .. Where, outside the darkening- forests, lonely
Boer and other homesteads are dotted, all seem crazy
with “ Chinese terror.” Here the Boer farmer with his
young sons, a lowering expression of rage and hatred on
his bearded face, is digging holes all round his homestead
“ for the Chinese,” and in two he lias spring guns. A
savage dog stalks around, with dripping jaws and long
red tongue, and if a Celestial comes along that will be his
next meal. Inside, the vrow is standing on a tin hat-
box on a chair, nailing up a window which has not a
fastening. Last night someone tried it. Johann sits
sharpening an axe, occasionally swinging it fiercely
round his head and muttering “Yah!” For this
family knew poor farmer Jourert, just murdered by the
Chinese ; and the mother, who was on an adjacent farm
on the night of the murder, and saw Joiibert’s face after
death, speaks, covering her eyes, in low, shuddering
moans of the sight of the room. Though she tries to be
civil to you, still you are one of the Rooineks who have
brought this fresh trouble on the land once theirs ; and
far away on the sky-line are the Great White Mines whose
bowels of gold have done all the mischief from the war
up.
And only last week an Australian convention of
G. H. Reid’s Foreign-trade women called for the
introduction of Asiatic labor as a “ reform ” for
Australia !

The “Union Label.”
When accounts come to be made up, probably it
will be found that the most useful bit of legisla-
tive work of the present session of the Australian
Parliament is the legalisation of the much-abused
“ union label.” Quite outside of the merits of the
matter itself, its indirect consequences have been
fruitful of good, since, owing to it, the Opposition
was at last induced to face a fight in the
open, and thus clear the way for sensible Stand-
ing Orders of debate and a working programme.
But, leaving that out of consideration, the
“union label’’ promises great benefits. In
the first place, its incidence will be dis-
tinctly Protective. Only Australian goods
made under fair labor conditions can have the
benefit of the label. Foreign-made goods will be
debarred from its use. - Thus the public will have
a ready means of finding out Australian goods and
of giving a preference to our home manufactures.
Just as important will be the facilities the measure
gives to the public to distinguish between articles
which are made under fair conditions and the
products of sweating shops. It is to the growth
of a healthy public sentiment against “sweating ”
that a civilised community must look as one of
the valuable means of social reform. Legisla-
tion, wisely designed and firmly administered,
can do much to ensure fair wages and
reasonable hours for the worker; but the
reinforcement which social legislation may get
from a healthy opinion is of the utmost importance.
The general trend of human thought is, so far as
concerns the majority, towards good—when it
doesn’t involve too much trouble. Most men
would prefer to “do the square thing,” all
circumstances being equal; and most persons would
prefer, if allowed a free choice, to deal with the fair
and honest employer. The trade-union label will
enable the public to exercise that preference
since what articles have been manufactured under
trades-union conditions will palpably be 11 fair-
labor” goods. When it has been said that
the label will assist the patriotic Australian
to give preference to his own country’s goods
and the social reformer to give preference to
fair labor goods; nothing remains to be urged for—
or against —the measure. But the Foreign-trade
newspapers—which bitterly object to allowing
facilities for an Australian preference, and are
shocked at the idea of any check on the nefarious
tricks of their importing friends, and don’t wish
to see anything done to promote fair wage and
hour conditions in which, as Foreign-traders, they
do not believe—have contrived to cloud a fair
discussion of its merits with the usual
mass of lies and misrepresentations. The extent
to which these went may be best judged from
the impression they made. A very fair-minded
and capable man now visiting Australia, dis-
cussing the union label with a member
of the Australian Parliament, expressed sympathy
with the idea of allowing the public means to dis-
tinguish between fair-labor goods and others, but
was antagonistic to the union label clauses “be-
cause they went too far.” A little conversation
elicited the idea that he had formed of the union
label from the frenzied shrieks of the Foreign-trade
p ress —that it forbade any article to be exposed
for sale in Australia unless it bore a label showing
that it was made by union labor! He was in a
difficulty to discover how it was going to be applied
to coal or groceries or cabbages. Yet another, a
citizen of Sydney, an acute lawyer, and with some
personal experience to warn him of the unreliability
of the daily newspapers, had acquired the impres-
sion that the proposed law made it compulsory for
employers, in at least certain trades, to employ
none but union labor and to so brand their goods.
As a matter of cold fact, the Trade Marks Bill
only secures tO trades unions by express enact-
ment what they formerly had by Common Law—
the right to register a device showing that their
labor has been exclusively employed in the
making of certain goods. It is provided that
this device may be, or may not be as he himself
chooses, printed by the employer on his products.
If he uses the device, he can appeal for the support
of fair-wage ad vocates. If he thinks it better busi-
ness not to use it, he needn’t do so. The union
can’t force him to employ the label, but he
can force the union to let him use it if he observes
their conditions of labor. Further, if he observes
fair conditions but does not choose to employ
union labor, or to advertise by label the fact that
he does, he may, on proof to the Government of
fair conditions in his factory, obtain a Common-
wealth label guaranteeing his products as “fair-
labor ” ones. Nobody but the pirate and the
sweater —or, to put it shortly, nobody but a con-
firmed Foreign-trader—can object to this.
The N.S.W. North Coast Kailway.
The Carruthers Government, which fails to
decorate N.S.W. as a general thing, has at least
made one proposal which points towards honesty
and common sense. The North Coast railway is
wanted —urgently wanted. There is reason to
believe, however, that if it is built wholly with
borrowed money it won’t pay interest on its cost.
Therefore Premier Carruthers proposes to pay
half the cost out of revenue, so that the line will
only have to struggle with the interest on the
other half. It is a grievous pity that, while he
was at it, he didn’t propose to pay the whole cost
out of revenue, but in a land of ghastly dishonesty
so far as State finance is concerned even a small
instalment of honesty and decency is something.
Significantly enough, N.S.W.’s blight and evil
genius, the Sydney Telegraph, which has backed
Carruthers cordially in many a muddy political
transaction and many a murky dodge and wile
and shift, turns dog on him just here—or at least,
if its proceedings are not strong enough to be
called dog, it turns pup on him—and holds forth
thus: —
The latest intention of the Government regarding- the
North Coast railway, as explained by Mr. Carruthers
yesterday, is “to put it in the category of a reproductive
work, and attach to it a condition that the cost should
come equally out of revenue and out of loans.” But how,
by any mere book-keeping operation of this kind, a work
can be made reproductive that otherwise would not be
so, the public are left to find out for themselves. If this
railway will earn interest on the cost of its construction,
it would stand in the category of reproductive works, no
matter where the money was procured from. Failing
that condition, it must go in the other category, and the
difference between the market value of the capital spent
on it and the annual interest return remain a dead loss to
the people who have to make it good.. . The line
should wait till it comes into the category of reproductive
works.
Put in a condensed form, the Telegraph idea is
evidently that we should measure everything by
the length of Cohen’s nose, and that we should
invest in none of the adjuncts of civilisation
unless they will directly pay interest on the full
amount of their cost in borrowed money,
whether the money is borrowed or not.
The idea is worthy of a paper which
imports its financial expert from England,
and fills its money column so full of remarks about
the state of the Bank of England that it hasn’t
had time to discover the existence of Australia
yet. The civilisation of Australia, or of any other
country, isn’t a thing to be wholly measured by
the price at which money can be borrowed in Eng-
land. Australia needs military and naval
defences, schools, roads, bridges, harbor works,
and hundreds of other works which won’t pay
per cent, on their cost—which, in fact, return
nothing per cent, directly, and often call for a
heavy outlay for maintenance as well. And it
needs many railways which won’t pay per
cent, directly on their cost either, but which may
pay 2 per cent, in cash and make up the other
per cent, indirectly by contributing to the progress
and civilisation of the country. If the country
went in for nothing which at the outset
left a gap between what the Telegraph calls
“ the market value of the capital spent
on it and the annual interest return ”it
would still be as purely aboriginal a land as
Captain Cook found it. No country which isn’t
ready to spend many hundreds of millions on
enterprises which pay a great deal less, directly,
than 3i per cent.—which often pay directly nothing
at all per cent. —is likely to come to much. And
just at present, with many millions of gold lying
idle in the banks and earning nothing at all, it
would be a payable spec, to invest a good deal of
our own money in a work that would return 2 per
cent, in cash at once, and perhaps much more later
on, and add an indefinite amount to the aggregate
value of the country. The absurdity lies in the old
practice of building 2 per cent, works, or less than
2 per cent, works, with money borrowed at 2>\ or
4 per cent., and in thinking that we can borrow a
civilisation at the pawnshop, and in mortgaging
our progress and our prospects and our infant to
the foreign Jew. We want a civilisation of our
own, paid for with our own money. But if we
wait for art and science and education and de-
velopment till we are quite sure they will pay
per cent, from the start, we will wait a long,
lingering time. Even Christianity didn’t pay 3|
per cent, at the beginning, and couldn t have
floated a loan at Antioch to save its life. Yet
Christianity and the North Coast railway are both
progress and both worth paying for.

More N.S.W. Lands Muddle.
There are already in N.S.W. 27 ways, apart
from sheer stealing, in which it is possible to
occupy State lands—auction purchase, after-
auction purchase, conditional purchase, home-
stead selection, conditional lease, pastoral lease,
ordinary occupation license, preferential occupa-
tion license, homestead lease, settlement lease,
improvement lease, annual lease, scrub lease, snow
land lease, special lease, inferior land lease, arte-
sian well lease, western land lease, residential lease
on mineral fields, improvement purchase, and
mineral or auriferous lease; also, it is possible to
acquire a pastoral lease under the Church and
School Lands Act, likewise a mining lease, agri-
cultural lease and building lease; and when none of
these fits the case there is always the “permissive
occupancy ” to fall back upon. Now at the tail end
of the session, State Premier Carrutiiers has
devised still another Lands Bill, and proposes to in-
vent a “conditional purchase lease”—a 40 years
lease with appraisement every 10 years and right
of purchase at any time. In addition to this
the Bill seems intended to patch the already
much-patched and amended land laws in
almost every direction, and it also provides for
“the temporary administration by Commissioners
of the Department of Lands.” The Commissioner
idea, which is greatly in favor just now, is a bitter
satire on the present system of Party Government
or Government by Contradiction. In Switzerland
Ministers are elected by Parliament, presumably
on account of their fitness, and their appointment
is for a fixed term of years. Therefore they really
are Commissioners. In Australia, following the
foolish English system, they are appointed for a
wholly unfixed period which may be a week or
may be some years, and nothing is said about their
fitness; the man who moved the amendment
which upset the last Government is Premier, and
he distributes the portfolios among his friends, or
his creditors, or his best supporters, or the enemies
he is most afraid of. This scheme has worked so
badly that, first of all, the railways were put
under permanent Commissioners—the dazzling
succession of political rulers who might last three
days or a year, and who might or might not
know an axle from a signal-box, grew too exciting.
Next the control of the Civil Service was taken
out of the hands of Ministers and put under Com-
missioners. The alleged Sinking Fund and the
resumed area of Sydney are under Commissioners.
The Lands now go the same way ; as Parliament
has not the sense to give up the party system and
elect its most competent member as Lands Minis-
ter for a fixed term so that lie may be the Com-
missioner, it is proposed to give some kind of per-
manency to the lands policy by putting the public
estate under the control of outside Commissioners.
It is only necessary for the Works Department
and the Treasury to go also into the hands of
Commissioners, and then the present temporary
and accidental method of Government, whereby
Brayhard, M.L.A., becomes for a fleeting mo-
ment Minister for Anything That Happens To
Be Lying About, will be revealed as the huge joke
it is. Either Ministers must be their own Com-
missioners—elected because of their presumed
fitness by Parliament to run certain departments,
subject to Parliamentary control, for a fixed term
of years—or apparently a time will yet arrive in
N.S.W. when the Government will have nothing
to do except invent grievances against the Federal
authority and watch Commissioners doing its
work.
A Moan About the Mines.
London Daily News on Johannesburg :
Johannesburg is an enchanted city, and nothing
seems able to break the enchantment. At first it was
said that if Krugehism were banished and a British rule
established, all the land would flow with milk and honey.
Well, the British rule came. Then the cry wafc for limit-
less labor, cheap labor, yellow labor. It was‘gratified-
still no change. Then it was said that a huge output re-
vealing the potentialities of the mines—“ potentialities
beyond the dreams of avarice’’—would make Johannes-
burg-leap into life. And the new “ record ” comes, but
it does not remove the enchanter’s wand. Hotels, banks,
vast offices, are springing up everywhere in anticipation
of the golden time that is so long overdue ; but the sun
still rises and sets on the Enchanted City—a city where
trade is stagnant, where labor is negligible, where poverty
and hunger haunt th§ streets, and where only the fabu-
lously rich grow fabulously richer.
We should prefer to say that it is a Disenchanted City,
not an Enchanted City. It is a city whose foundation is
lies, greed, and disloyalty. It is the product of Stock
Exchange finance, and of cosmopolitan adventurers, who
are none the less cosmopolitan because they are given
English titles. Johannesburg flung itself at the feet of
the German Jew financiers. It believed that if the
British could banish the Boer from his own land all the
plunder of the Rand would be its own. The war over and
“ the corrupt Boer oligarchy ” a thing of history, Johan-
nesburg has discovered that it has helped England to pull
the chestnuts out of the fire for the Wernhers, the Beits
and the Barnatos. The white man, who alone works the
inferior mines of West Australia, and works them at a
profit, is found too costly a servant for the Hamburg
diamond merchants who now rule the destinies of the
Transvaal. Nor is that all. He is too dangerous a ser-
vant, as Mr. Lionel Phillips has frankly admitted, for he
would demand a vote—that very vote which was, indeed,
the prime object of the war. And so the mines are filled
with yellow slaves, and the earnings of the laborers vanish
to China.
Nor is that all. Johannesburg awoke after the war to
find itself “ A Tied House ” of the Randlords. It was not
only the mines that they possessed. It was its commerce
that they controlled. The middle man who supplied the
necessities of the mines has been largely displaced. The
great houses provide themselves with their lightest needs,
just as the great brewery supplies the public with its own
matches and its tobacco. And above and beyond all this
there is the disillusionment of the home investor. He
has passed through years of bitter enlightenment. He
understands to-day as he did not understand when, in
the language of his poet, he “killed Kruger with his
mouth,” the sinister figures and sinister influences
behind this tragedy of the Rand. He knows, especially
if he chances to have had any dealings with Rhodesia,
how he was deluded by the brilliant fiction of the pros-
pectuses, and how worthless mines were floated on a tide
of mendacity. He, in a word, like Johannesburg, is dis-
enchanted. He has buttoned up his pocket, and not even
the attractions of cheap and limitless Chinese labor and of
“record” outputs have restored his confidence in aland
where gold is abundant, but where its only fruits, so far,
have been blood, confusion, and slavery.
