The Asiatic Briton’s Opinion.
Dear Bulletin, —I was in Shanghai when the
result of the peace negotiations came to hand,
and was astonished to find the joy with which the
Japanese back-down was received. They know
the Jap better in Eastern Asia than anywhere
else, just as the gentleman who wears it knows
where the shoe pinches. The orthodox outside
idea, is that the Japs are a mild, picturesque, toy
nation with picturesque ugly women, prettily
dressed, whose expressionless, inane faces are the
index of womanly submission, good nature, and
domestic subserviency; and of polite men, who
bend double in excessive self-depreciation when
they meet you. It does not need a long sojourn in
Eastern Asia to ascertain that the Japs are uni-
versally hated there, and since their rapid military
and naval rise, are feared as well. The Japanese
ii-..un j.auts in places like Singapore, Hongkong,
Shanghai and Tientsin, can generally be divided
into three classes —the barber, the bagnio geisha,
and the merchant. The two former are in the
way of rapidly becoming monopolists, and they
carry on their callings at least faithfully and con-
scientiously, according to their lights. The latter
is known commercially as a rogue, a shuffler, and
a repudiator. The Chinaman rejoiced more than
anyone else in the Jap diplomatic defeat. He
has had some experience—more, perhaps, than
anyone else—of the Jap’s propensity, for back-
ing out of a bargain which turns out disadvanta-
geously. Also, the Jap can argue the leg off an
iron pot when he is trying to wriggle out 'of a
trade agreement. The Chinaman is not argu-
mentative. The wonder is that the peace envoy
of “ Mdtsuhito the Magnanimous” (vide British
press) did not prolong the duel with Witte until
the Russian was talked or bored till death. An
American who has lived in Japan accounts for
the commercial probity of the Chinaman and the
equally notorious roguery of the Japanese in this
way: In old Japan the soldier (Samurai) class
was the national hero and ideal. Trade (as in all
early feudal societies) was looked upon with con-
tempt, and got into the hands of a pariah class,
which existed in Old Japan and exists in the New.
In China, on the contrary, the soldier was (and is
to-day) drawn from the pariah classes, and was
despised; while peaceful trade and commerce
were the national ideals, and the merchant classes
were esteemed and admired. That, says the
American, is why the Japanese merchant imitates
trade marks, and repudiates contracts, and why
the Japanese courts legalise, or illegalise, his
repudiation ; while the Chinese merchant’s word
is his bond, and a parole contract with a Chow is
as much respected in Asia as a contract under seal
would be with a Jap. The military prowess and
success of the Jap—and I do not gainsay or
depreciate it—has only thrown into bolder
relief the qualities which have made him odious.
Whilst at ’Ome, and in some circles in Australia,
the Briton swells out his chest with pride at the
Jap alliance —as who should say “one English-
man and a Jap are equal to three Frenchmen ;
while a British alleged poet (a patriotic poet!) can
be found so abject in his adulation of the Jap
successes that he prays God in his goodness and
favor to graciously permit the Briton to become
some day as good a man as the Jap (vide Mr.
Richard Straciian Rowe, in the Monthly
Review) —the Briton in Eastern Asia loathes
the Jap and abhors the alliance, which he con-
siders in the last degree degrading to the white'
races ; which he knows has lowered his prestige in
the eyes of the Chinese, among whom he has to
live ; and which he considers a standing procla-
mation of Britain’s Imperial decline. It may
safely be said that the whole body of international
traders in Asia who have had experience of the
Jap rejoiced with the Far Eastern Briton at
the back-down of the victorious Jap before the
press-defeated Russian. A prolonged contact
with the Japs appears to have the same effect upon
everyone—it excites a feeling of dislike and
repulsion even in those who have, from a distance,
admired their wonderful art, their undeniable
progressiveness and successful self-development,
and their marvellous military organisation and
personal courage. Foreign opinion, often ex-
pressed in Eastern Asia, is that the Japo-British
alliance is a betrayal of the interests of Western
civilisation, which will sooner or later bring its
own punishment; and that opinion is shared and
voiced by the expatriated Briton, who makes
no secret of his dissatisfaction. As to the idea
that the Chinaman is waiting in his childlike and
bland manner for the superior Jap to come along
and make a soldier of him in millions, in order to
fight the West, it is absurd. John is not built
that way ; but if he did desire to rise in the mili-
tary scale (and the Peking crowd has such ideas)
the great majority of him would rather have the
devil than the Jap as a military master—Yours,
Locust.

A Matter of Weeds.
The utter futility of leaving important matters,
where any expense is necessary, to municipal
councils composed of landlords, is well exemplified
by what is happening in Victoria in connection
with the eradication of noxious weeds. The Local
Government Act requires the councils to see that
all noxious weeds aie eradicated before they get a
firm footing ; but in practically every instance
this important provision is a dead letter. The
Victorian Government entomologist, Mr. French,
has several times drawn attention to the spread of
the deadly imported weed, St. John’s wort, in the
north-eastern district, but to no effect. He
says :
A few years ago I pointed out to the Rutherglen
Borough Council what they might expect from the Weed
if they did not take stern measures to check it, and to-
day they are doubtless sorry they did not take the advice
given.
Probably they are not actively sorry ; but, even if
they are, all the sorrow in Rutherglen woijß eradi-
cate one root of the weed. In fact, if the sorrow
is expressed close to the weed it will refresh it.
Mr. French continues :
Should the weed get a footing in the rich lands of
Gippsland, it will be a bad job for the rural prospects of
Victoria . . . The administration of the Noxious Weeds
Act must be at once removed from 1 all local bodies, and
placed in the hands of the Government.
And when the Government tackles the jcfb, as it
must inevitably do sooner or later, it will be a
very big job indeed. The inert municipal coun-
cillors, who could once have dealt with it by
expending a few pounds, wasted their time and
their money in organising anti-socialist leagues
and opposing the union label and other foreign
matters. At the present time, Victoria is the
paradise of weeds, most of which were imported.
Just now, the Western District is a blaze of
yellow, the Cape weed pest being in flower. This
weed has owned the roads for years, and is now
steadily occupying the paddocks to-the extermina-
tion of the rye-grass, clover and cocksfoot. Also,
armies of men are now busy trying to cut out the
so-called Scotch thistle; and it is impossible to
travel far in the most fertile parts of Victoria
without coming across the star and other thistles,
the furze, the blackberry, the sweetbriar, and, a
number of kindred vegetable pests, which will,
unless drastic measures are taken, eventually
occupy the whole of the country. A good pre-
liminary measure would be to pull the councillors
backwards through all the furze and briar found
in their territory.
The Victorian Cold Tea Crusade.
For the past few years the Victorian Cold Tea
party has been in a condition of unwonted
quiescence, and the banner of Bung has floated
triumphant, with an average of only one decayed
egg per twelve months thrown at it. Several
years ago, after much work, the temperance crowd
got a Local Option Act; but as it provided for
cash compensation for dispossessed publicans, the
alleged reform was soon discovered to be a sham
of the first degree. Local option polls were held,
usually with fine large results—in fact, the results
at last became so splendid that “ the cause ” was
rapidly being ruined—by prosperity, so to speak.

GALL-AHER UP BELFAST- N? TRUST OR COMBINE
The North Melbourne poll made such a hole in
Bent’s finances that the awful spectacle of a
Premier asking the various publicans to knock a
little off the Compensation Court’s award, as a
discount for cash, was witnessed. This was the
temperance party's low-water mark. A few
months ago a Maoriland temperance apostle
named Judkins shifted to Melbourne, where, by
Eractising the vigorous methods of Seddon, he
as carried fire and sword into the camp of
Bung, and is raising Cain generally. Lemmon,
the secretary of the whisky association, made
one or two attempts to stem the torrent; but the
froth and foam of Judkins have pretty well
smothered him. Judkins said that drink was
causing the death of hundreds of infants every
year, to which Lemmon foolishly and feebly
retorted (according to Judkins) that the infants
were better dead. Lemmon, when last heard of,
was denying bitterly that he ever said such a
thing ; but he is evidently no match for the fiery
Maorilander. J udkins also attacked Bent’s Chief
Secretary, Gillott, for being interested in hotels
while he was the official head of the police ; and
the only reply has been to call Judkins “sus-
picious” and “evil-minded.” Judkins has been
speaking at large all over Melbourne, and pouring
scorn on the Temperance crowd for being so far
behind Maoriland ; and the recent advance in
N.S.W. liquor legislation has given him another
point. So that everything points to a new Licens-
ing Act next session, with a provision for “no
license,” the abolition of barmaids, and the repeal
of the compensation provision. When this Bill
comes on the boards, the antics of Judkins should
be something out of the common ; and if Lemmon
is well-advised he will change his name to
Lemonade, and get a billet as secretary of a Sons
of Temperance lodge.
A Voice from Outside.
An interview on board the s.s. Moeraki with a
Canadian harvester man :
Yes, I’m going- back to Buenos Ayres. I’m representing
a Canadian harvester manufacturer, and I’m bringing
along eighteen Australians with me to fit up machines
and set them working. Australians are thought a great
deal of there, more than Englishmen and Americans, and
lots of them are making a pile of money. You see, we
can’t sell the ordinary machines in Canada ; country is too
wet, and the machines won’t thrash, as wet penetrates
the ears. Buenos Ayres is more like Australia, and the
Australians understand it exactly. And they know their
business. There are four representatives of Australian
firms on board also on the same job. Their agents are
selling a lot of machines, and they do good work. We
get £l4O for a machine down there. It’s a good profit,
but the risks are greater, and the law is so loose that it is
hard to recover without palm-greasing. Feel sorry, but
Australians will let the trade slip away. I sold 50
machines last year. This year I’ve placed 500.
We don’t mind the extra valuation for duty in Aus-
tralia. We know it was pointed at one firm, and we are
satisfied to get £BS for a machine. You Australians don’t
understand patriotism. If we didn’t protect heavily we
would never have got a start. We can’t get a machine
into the States, but they came over and built on our
territory. Still we can hold our own and better. Pro-
tection is strong in Canada. Recently they had a big fire
in Toronto which burnt out the largest undertakers’ fur-
nishers there, and while the place was flooded with
American goods people refused to be buried. This is a
joke, but it’s nearly true. They wouldn’t be planted in
foreign coffins.
We pay big wages and have little or no boy labor.
Cheap labor doesn’t pay, and Americans pay higher than
we do. All our men are specialists, and don’t work as
hard as Australians. The machinery and system make
the work quick, but they don’t age as early as people
seem to think.
Buenos Ayres is the greatest country in the world for
agricultural machinery at present. Knew one agent who
sold 1000 reapers and binders, and 300 thrashing machines
in one year. Australia doesn’t send any. Country is very
corrupt and there are always tinpot revolutions, which
are exaggerated abroad. Just before I came over thirty
men were killed in a melee in my street and they destroyed
the court house ; but it fizzled out. Foreigners keep out of
the way and attend to business. If they took a hand in
politics they could improve things, but what’s the good?
The State doesn’t own any land, it being given away to
military swells. It doubled its value last year. The
country is covered with rank grass which sheep and horses
won’t eat. Used previously to put mares, which the
natives won’t ride, on to it to eat or starve, and they
frequently starved.
p* Most of my men will return to Australia, which is a
great country, and when you cut up the land and know
enough to make your own goods, there will be a boom
that will settle all your troubles right away. I expect to
be back for more men next year. They are just the thing
and it’s only their own country that doesn’t appreciate
them.
In an article on Defence, Melb. Argus tells Aus-
tralia that it is a poor worm, and that it is no use
trying to ever be anything more than a worm :
If the British navy were beaten, Australians could
under no conceivable system of defence hope to hold the
continent against a military power with the sea open to
its transports. . . . Major-General Hutton realised the
folly of talking about preparations against a regular inva-
sion. ... We must eschew, therefore, dreams of an Aus-
tralian navy in our time and of land forces numerous enough
to meet a hostile army such as Britain sent into the South
African republics.
The little S’African republics, with but a
tenth of Australia’s white population or
wealth or resources, put up a good and by
no means despairing fight, for three years, against
the greatest empire on earth ; and it seems safe
to say that, had the Boers possessed a seaboard,
they would have built and maintained a navy (of
their own, not hired) that would have argued the
point considerably with the enemy before he
landed—that is, if their prowess on land is any
criterion. But Australia, the Argus says, is a
caterpillar that must not think of resistance.
Still, after all, it isn’t the first time the Argus
has mistaken itself for Australia.
A league has been formed in Melbourne for the
purpose of boycotting union-labelled goods when
they arrive. But like many other threatened
combinations of the same sort, the league will be
found non-existent when the time comes. The
men who are running it will, owing to the
extent of their operations, be the first to adopt
the label. The man who will really feel the
effects of the Act, and who may be
crushed out of existence by it, is the small,
insignificant sweater who competes
with the big firms, and, while
making a starvation pittance him-
self, employs operatives who have
to supplement their wages by crime
or prostitution to get three meals
a day. This has been the result of
the Yictorian Factories Act. It
has closed up many sweat shops
and really done the decent em-
ployer more good than harm in re-
moving his most serious competi-
tors in the struggle which leads
to the survival of the cheapest.
Most of the Vic. employers are
ready to admit this ; and it is only
the few who used to profit by sell-
ing goods bought from the same
sweat shops who really regret the
passing of the Factories Act.
When the union label comes,
manufacturers will be found tumb-
ling over themselves to get it
attached to their articles, and the
only person who will view it with
genuine alarm will be Fatta Matin
and Abdul Khyhab and the other
sturdy producers of Little Lonsdale-
street.

By reason of the reluctance of the
affable Prime Minister Deakin to
use the closure now that he has
got it, things are not as yet
very much improved in the Aus-
tralian Parliament. The stone-
walling, and the interminable monotony of the
“ Squealing Dozen,” and the all-night sittings are
still in evidence. There is something curiously
bald and primitive about the Parliamentary
manoeuvres of Cook. His favorite device is to
offer to. let so many clauses go through in a certain
time—the smallest number of clauses he can ven-
ture to offer in the longest time he thinks he can
suggest without getting his offer thrown back at
his frequently-opened head—and if the Govern-
ment wants more business done he and Kelly
and Johnson will talk to the last gasp and
try to prevent anything at all being done.
His other favorite device, after he and the other
dry obstacles have talked about nothing for a long,
long time, is to offer that, if they are allowed
to continue talking about the same nothing
for two or three days longer—without giving
any guarantee that they will make a single new
remark concerning it—they will then consent to a
division being taken on the nothing. Cook knows
perfectly well that the majority is against him.
He knows that all his speeches won t change a
single vote or turn the majority into a minority.
He knows that in every Democratic country the
majority must rule, even when it isn’t Joe Cook’s
majority. The whole policy of the venomous
ex-coal-trucker is to obstruct, to be disagreeable,
to prevent majority rule, to bring the Parliament
into contempt, and to be a cantankerous outrage
generally. And Deakin is already falling back
into his famous affability, and allowing a free run
to the little gang of verbal interminables.

bourne, chiefly because it is made of fruit, instead
of tomatoes, turnips, and various berries ; but that
the Victorian factories are being wiped out by the
operation of the minimum wage law is pure
Ananias. The said law came into operation in
’99, and the Vic. Statist says that the jam factories

R. S. Smythe, the Much-Travelled, is the latest
to speak on the S’African horrors, to London
Telegraph
When the coolies were engaged in China it was never
hinted to them that they would have to work under-
ground. They get tired after a week or two, and do not
hesitate to commit some offence, as they prefer being
prisoners above ground to being slaves underground.
When I was at the Cape, about 1500 prisoners had
escaped, and the police were scouring for them in all
directions. To prevent outrages, arms had been served out
to the Boers with permission to shoot the Chinese on sight.
Travelling allowances of 6s. per mile were being paid by the
mineowners to Boers who brought in escaped Chinese. The
coolies imported include a lot of criminals whom the
Chinese authorities are doubtless very glad to get rid of.
The overseers are magistrates and can make almost
anything an offence. . . They are judges as well as execu-
tioners. It is said that prisoners are hung up by the
heels and mercilessly sjamboked. . . The gigantic men
from the north of China act as policemen and often inflict
brutal punishment. Chinese and Kaffirs have always to
be kept in different compounds. . . The special corres-
pondents sent out by the English newspapers will reveal
a state of affairs quite as bad as anything depicted in the
Southern States of America in the slavery days.

* * + *
Played for the first time in Australia at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday night, “Leah
Kleschna ’ ’ is excellent melodrama. Melodrama
it is essentially, and when you see it you
find how good melodrama can be and generally
isn’t. But just because it aims . higher than
most, it is inclined to be a bit heavy
during a couple of the five acts. Paul
Sylvaine’s theories are so startling that the
playwright evidently felt he had to let the theorist
do a good deal of rather prosy talking in order to
develop his ideas, so that they shouldn’t seem too
absurd and kill the piece with ridicule. And,
unfortunately, the stiff, prosy matter is in the
hands of a stiff, prosy actor like Douglas Gerrard.
From the little he did in “ Sunday,” one hoped
for better things than he didin “Merely Mary
Ann”; and one doesn’t get them. But, apart
from this rather wearisome dialogue,''for which, as
said just now, there is some excuse, “Leah
Kleschna” is deeply interesting, and holds at
least two tensely dramatic situations —the meeting
between Sylvaine and his uninvited guest, and the
subsequent meeting between the girl and her
father, when she announces that the old life is no
longer possible. The latter is the only point at
which Roy Redgrave’s very fine character study of
old Kleschna seems weak. A strong character
is Kleschna, as noted for some remarkable acts of
bravery as for his remarkable acts of burglary.
Old Flambeau, of “L’Aiglon,” gave Redgrave
many hints in the new part, and the two must be
coupled and put in the front row. Only when he
ceases for a few moments to be the cool, plotting
thief-manager, and is a father hit hard by the
thought of losing a daughter whom, in his curious
way, he loves, does Redgrave strike a false note. As
for Leah, Miss Brune’s uneven work rises as high as
any she has done, and in a few flashes even higher
(the last stormy scene with her father is conspicu-
ous). One can’t, however, believe that the scene
with the despicable cad, Raoul Berton (Gaston
Mervale), is managed to the end as Leah would
have managed it. Albert Gran’s Berton pere is
better than passable—his make-up deserves a line
to itself. Atholwood, in the Gecko-like character
of Schram, plays an extremely difficult minor part
with all this actor’s consistent attention to detail,
and Oily Deering adds a fleeting touch of low
comedy to the final act, which is seemingly
dragged in because melodrama prescribes a wed-
ding as the reward of all virtue. Add that “Leah
Kleschna” is finely mounted (artist John Gordon
received the rare honor of a double call on Satur-
day when the fifth-act setting was unfolded), that
every “curtain” leaves a striking situation to
supply food for interval-talk, and you have the
story of a sterling success.
** * *
“Princess Ida,” revived at Sydney Cri-
terion after so long an interval that it is now a
novelty to most playgoers, is in the earlier manner
of Gilbert and Sullivan. It has not the same fan-
tastic originality of idea t.s later pieces, nor so
much wit. Only here and there does the whim-
sical topsy-turvydom of Gilbert’s mind peep out.
But nevertheless the fun is etched in with bold,
free lines and prompts perhaps more hearty laughter
than some of the more ingenious quips of “The
Mikado” or “lolanthe.” “Princess Ida” shows
the transition stage between opera bouffe and that
highly original school of comic opera which is
associated with the names of Gilbert and Sullivan,
and which no one but Gilbert seemed able to
devise, for the “Rose of Persia” was a solitary
effort, and the man who wrote it produced nothing
more of its kind. “ Princess Ida” calls for much
more gorgeous mounting than the partnership’s
later efforts, and J. O. Williamson has done it
full justice, the scenery being exquisite, and the
dresses brilliant, while the armor shines like a
new moon or a boom in silver shares.
* * * *
Standing out from a performance which
is, on the whole, open to very little criticism, are
the dainty acting and singing of Miss Aggie
Thorne, who enters thoroughly into the spirit of-
this class of work; the equally attractive pour-
trayal of Lady Psyche by Miss Yinia De Loitte ;
and the fine rendering of the Bacchic tenor num-
ber “Kiss Me” by Kenningham. Obviously so
as to give the best effect to this number, Kenning-
ham takes the subordinate part of Cyril, and he
sings and acts the winey rhapsody with artistic
abandon. Miss Dolly Castles has a very difficult
r6le as Princess Ida; it calls for very high
singing and acting ability, and the little
lady is somewhat overweighted in a part
which even taxed Miss Nellie Stewart’s powers
in her best days though as Nellie Stewart’s
days have all been best days, or very nearly so.
this way of putting it may seem invidious. Miss
Celia Ghiloni is an impressive Lady Blanche, and
the work of Frank Wilson and Ernest Fitts is also
commendable. Other principals are fair to mid-
dling, and the chorus and orchestra, as usual, are
excellent. For the concluding week of the season
there will be a series of revivals, “The Mikado,”
“ Patience,” and “ The Pirates of Penzance.”
** * *
“Through the Divorce Court,” staged at
Sydney Royal on Saturday, is a rather sane and
reasonable melodrama, as melodramas go, with a
strong story carried out on robust lines. The
heroine, Dolores, has a natural brother (she is a
“LEAH KLESCHNA.”
“ In a matter of business the thing to do
Is to grit your teeth and see it through.
There isn’t spare room for sentiment
In the heart of man on business bent.
Dream no dreams, be sparing of speech,
And take whatever your hands can reach.”
This was the lesson that Kleschna taught
To Leah his daughter—who set it at nought.
His motherless daughter (the good) had died ;
Now Leah was daughter and pupil beside.
She could “crack a crib” with the best in the
trade—•
Swift to think and do was the Kleschna maid.
Paul Sylvaine, of the Deputies,
Pondered on things in his home of ease.
Deep, he said, in the heart of man
Clear and pure the waters ran.
Foul though the surface, yet underneath
Was a Soul unscathed in its battered sheath.
The time shall come, he said in his glee,
When never a prison in France need be ;
When all who live in the dark of night
Shall open their eyes and see the light;
When the gentle touch shall make men whole,
And the scum shall be skimmed from every soul.
Sylvaine is rich, and his jewels rare
To-morrow shall pass to his bride most fair—
Diamonds and rubies and pearls which invite
Kleschna and Leah to work to-night...
A step and a stir, and face to face
The soul-physician has met a “ case.”
Give the doctor his due; he wasn’t dismayed
As he set to work on the Soul of the maid.
“Be seated,” he said. The thief was a-stare,
As he asked her the why, and the when and where.
And he found, ’neath the vice she wore as a hood,
A soul unscathed, and the seed of good.
“ A thief came seeking for gems,” she said ;
“ She found the light of her life instead.
A thief crept in by the casement door ;
A woman walks out who will rob no more.
The weeds by the hand of man were sown ;
Now the seed of truth shall spring alone.
“ Back I go to the fields of my youth,
Where a simple people live lives of truth.
Coarse the work that my hands shall do ;
But —Kleschna’s daughter will see it through.”
Messieurs, Madame Sylvaine ! Yes, she’s married
her Paul,
So the Sylvaine jewels are hers, after all.

as he conceals all the proofs, including the register
of birth-marks and the certificate that ma and pa
were not married, virtue is downcast in the dust,

arms, and wickedness gloats. Until close to
curtain fall, in accordance with immemorial tradi-

then, on Christmas Eve, with the snow crowning
the battlements of Ballyboodle Towers, the che-ild
lisps that she wants Santa Claus to bring back
mama as a Christmas gift for her stocking, and
mama promptly arrives with the proofs of her
innocence. The decree nisi has been made
absolute, and lady and lord are as effectively
unmarried as could be, but melodrama is too unso-
phisticated to notice trifles of that sor t and domestic
happiness is restored right there without further
ado. The work of Mr. Anderson’s co. in this
divorce piece is throughout good and even. Miss
Eugenie Duggan gives an effective picture of the
injured wife ; but why a golden wig for a Spanish
part? Miss Guildford, as a lady who is no better
than necessary, is another bright item ; and the
cq. has unearthed another comedian in Temple
Harrison, whose picture of a young English
johnnie is distinctly good. Probably “Through
the Divorce Court ” will occupy the jury until the
Christmas pantomime is reached. For the panto.,
a handsome principal boy and other massive fur-
nishings have been imported.
X* * *
Most interesting of the newcomers at the
Sydney Tivoli is Boy Glen, a youngster with a
voice, and a droll way of using it, and a budget of
good stories which he tells as though he had just
invented them. Arthur Albert, a recently-arrived
descriptive vocalist from the London ’alls, makes
some deplorable remarks as an alleged Australian
digger who is surrounded by a vast wealth of
limelight. Albert, it transpires, has been a bad
lot at ’Ome; but he puts on the top-boots and
riding-pants that every Australian digger wears,
and says he would give all the world if he could
go back ’Ome. And just as he realises the utter
impossibility of it, a boy comes in from the wings
and hands him a brief letter, which rhymes with
the last remarks he had made. Then he gasps
that he is forgiven, and he thanks God that now
he can turn his back on the land where he has
made his pile. The Mephisto triplet—Leroy,
Talma, and Bosco—are still playing weird tricks
with poultry and other things; the Jacksons are
playing on nearly everything, from a violin to a
side-drum ; and the bill is so full of large items
that the girl who generally fills in the first part
with some nasal observations, made in a short
frock, has been crowded out to give room for
something better.
** * *
All the Melbourne theatres are changing
their programmes again. At the Princess’s there
is a brief interregnum of silence, Nellie Stewart
having already concluded five extra grand per-
formances of “ Sweet Nell,” which bore the season
along to Wednesday of this week. A while ago
Melbourne wa@ exhorted to see the last of its
Nellie in her favorite character “perhaps for
ever,” but a managerial advt. is entitled to a lot
of poetic license, and when “The Bishop’s Car-
riage ” began to go very slowly the voice of good
old General Desire cried aloud for a further revival
of the Ellen who sold oranges in Drury Lane. All
things considered, it was better for the departing
actress to farewell in King Charles’s palace than
to say a few words to the audience subsequent to
her Pot and Pipkin fable in the other drama.
Melbourne would have been sorry to identify her
with a Pipkin in the hour of her send-off.
** * *
An amateur production of “Rip Van
Winkle” was to have happened at the Princess’s
next Saturday (9th), but the arrangement appears
to be “off.”
*■ * * *
The last nights of “Veronique” have
come with an unexpected rush at Her Majesty’s
(Melbourne), and the admirers of that charming
novelty are lamenting its present inability to
score a big success. The duty of catching on to
“ Veronique ” at the outset, and holding on for a
long period, will presently devolve upon the
Sydney public. Melbourne is letting it go in the
fourth week. For the last fortnight of the season
“The Girl from Kay’s” will be staged, with
Lauri as Hoggenheimer, and the full-toned
songstresses of J.C.W.’sCo. in the places of the
Geo. Edwardes Co’s ladies, whose tones fell short
of ordinary Australian requirements.
”** * »
Bland Holt likewise is changing the bill on
Saturday. “ With Flying Colors,” one of the best
dramas in Bland's valuable stock, would probably
be more popular under another title. The present
title suggests battle scenes, and repels a vast
number of people who have wearied of the Flag
and lost their appetite for Rule Britannia. It is
a misleading title, but the persons who don’t
trouble to discover their mistake are not blameable
in this matter. If the drama were called “The
Tune of Death ” it might run for three months,
and the appellation would be fully,. accounted for,
seeing that the first man who dies in the drama is
shot down whilst pounding away at a piano. “In
Sight of St. Paul’s” will return to notice on
Saturday.

At the Opera House a happy amphitheatre
sang its familiar hymn to the Bull who went down
to a Bush last time the present scribe looked in.
Florence Challis was reappearing ; also several
other “turns,” some of them undoubtedly good,
but none of them proudly pre-eminent. The Oor-
ricks, described as “Marvellous,” have ceased to
bid for amazement at the Athenaeum Hall,
where the Aquarium continues to show how
English trout are hatched, and the attendance at
Princes Court is eminently satisfactory. There is
a marvellous person at the Court who slides
through space by his teeth and closes his career
every evening in the Chute Lake.
THE ENGLISH UNEMPLOYED PROBLEM.
Replying to a deputation representing the London unemployed, the Prime Minister gave his reasons for re-
fusing State aid and for not summoning a winter session of Parliament. One of Mr. Balfour’s arguments was
that State aid was calculated to destroy the enterprise of capitalists and the energy and fibre of the workmen.—
Cable.
THE PROBLEM (who has pawned his last shirt): “Sate us, we're starving!"
PRIME MINISTER BALFOUR: “No, no—I could not think of destroying the energy and fibre
of the nation.”
Charles Holloway has closed up at
Adelaide Royal with “The Shadows of a Great
City,” and moved on —to Sydney, it is said. Yet
another local young musical genius has been fare-
welled at the Town Hall—Miss Maud Puddy.
She also moves on—to Europe. There has been
a casual mandolin concert; and now a dull depres-
sion has settled down, like the pall of death
covering the remains of yesterday’s lunch.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ Clubb ” : The comedy, “In the Bishop’s Carriage,” is
an example of the way an author and a few well-dressed
actors can pull the moral leg of an audience. Right
through the play the sympathy of the house is levelled
on Latimer, the gentlemanly golfing person who reclaims
Nance from her burglarious surroundings and makes her
an honest woman, while Dorgan, Nance’s former lover, is
made to appear the villain of the piece. _ Yet, of the two,
Dorian is far and away the more admirable character.
He is a burglar, but a burglar of qkill, daring, and, within
the limits of his profession, honor. He saved Nance from a
blackguard who was tempting her to a life of diamonds and
champagne, forebore to take advantage of her gratitude
himself, found her a poor thief and made her a smart one ;
and, when the time came, loved her so deeply that he
killed a warder to break gaol and see her. Latimer, on
the other hand, starts by jilting the girl he was engaged
to. He professes to take up Nance for reclamation pur-
poses, but shows the hollowness of his professions by send-
in<>- Dorgan to gaol for seven years at the same instant,
though Dorgan is just as fit a subject for his missionary
zeal as Nance. An audience, whose sense of honor was
not distorted by the sophistry of the author and the neat-
fitting trousers of Latimer, would, have hooted the golfing
lingerer and wept over the picture of Nance in Latimer’s
arms for the tragedy it was.
It is something to blow hard and long into a brass tube.
The Boulder City (W.A.) brass band, which recently won
a competition at Ballarat, went home to be met at the
station by the Kalgoorlie brass band, which played “ See
the Conquering Hero Comes.” The mayor and councillors
bouoht the deafeners beer and sandwiches, and then took
them on a special tramcar to Boulder, where more beer
and German sausage were provided. Australia has an
eccentric sense of what deserves praise. When a man
manufactures things he is sentenced to contumely, and
when he splits the peaceful firmament with the heathenish
blare of a big shiny trumpet the Mayor of the City takes
him on a special car to the free beer.
“Alix” : Can anyone tell me what constitutes a com-
plimentary concert? Always thought it was an affair
arrano-ed by a fellow’s friends and admirers and enthusi-
astic fellow'citizens on his departure for somewhere, and
that he had nothing to do but gather in the shekels when
it was all over. But t’other day I had a letter from a pal
who is going to the Cold, Boggy-Smoke to study. He
asked me to make an ornamental noise at his compli-
mentary concert and dispose of 27 tickets. As everybody
I know also has 27 tickets to sell, it seems I have been let
in for something. Still this is not so bad a case as one I
knew of, when a very well-dressed young gentleman was
arrested at his own farewell banquet, paid for by himself.
He would have been away a fortnight before the discovery
of the shortage in his cash if he hadn’t waited to organise
his own farewell. He was just responding to his own
health, when he was torn from the infested board, and he
got two years.
“ Melb.” : Nellie Stewart trundles on every night in
the pair-horse “ Bishop’s Carriage.” It’s not Archbishop
Clarke’s carriage, nor Archbishop Carr’s excellent pair-
horse equipage. The other night, at the Diocesan Festival
in the Town Hall, there were lantern views. One of the
Archbishop’s carriage was shown. Archbishop Clarke
afterwards said, “ Mind, that is only a carriage lent by a
friend. I have none.” Whereupon churchmen are
querying querulously : “ Why has he none ? Do we give
an archbishop £4O a week to go without a carriage ? Is
he generously expending the money in charity, or only
hoarding it up ? Anyway, we object. Let the prelate
keep up his dignity, and ours.”
“Wyoming”: “If, as “J.A.C.” writes (B. 23/11/05),
Duse played Juliet when 14 years of age and Marion
Dunn when 144, then they, probably, are the only
actresses whose years have approximated those of the
heroine. Shakspeare fixes Juliet’s age, as per the nurse
(act !., scene III.), at 14 or a little under: ‘ Come
Lammas-eve at night, shall she be fourteen. Lingi del
Porta from whose romance Shakspeare wrote his play,
says J uliet was approaching 18. It has become a stage
tradition that no actress can play Juliet until she is too
old to look the part. Hence Shakspeare’s lines are
altered to approach somewhere near the performer’s
apparent age. In the Brune version Juliet is represented
as being 18. Janet Waldorf, am incomparably better
Juliet than Tittell Brune, also made the age 18. I forget
what age the Brown-Potter adopted.
E. W. Mackney, one of the earliest fathers of nigger
minstrelsy in Britain, has been getting into print again
conCernm"- the London stage as he knew it sixty-eight
years it«o. These reminders of the virility of old has-
beens, suctf as Mackney, go to explain why the antique
artists wer© great institutions in their daj. Mackney
merely blacked his face, twanged a banjo and sang in a
commonplace! voice stuff that would be reckoned intoler-
ably soft and silly if anyone tried to revive it now. But
the singer, in his prime, had another fifty years of life in
him. He was a man of force, imparting his uncommon
personality to his doggerel ditties. Mackney, too, had a
new axiom to assert, and when he threw it off his chest it
struck everybody. “ We’re all bound to go the whole
hog or none,” was Mackney’s contribution to the world s
wisdom.

“Stunt” is the American term for such parts of classic
drama as the “seven ages” speech and Mercutio’s
“ Oueen Mab ” description, which most actors and
audiences seem to think should be given as a sort of bas-
relief on the general flat surface of a play. It is related of
one actor-manager playing Romeo that he couldn t bear
to let Mercutio have the Queen Mab stunt. He
annexed it himself, making some slight changes.
•Representatives of the Yankee commercial spirit have
lately been imploring the American public to ‘ Drop a
nickel in the slot and hear the ravings of John M Cul-
louah ” Trao-edian M’Cullough lost his reason a few
vears’before his death, and those who patronise the
phonograph are regaled with broken scraps from his
parts interspersed with a “dreadful laugh, half-insane,
half idiotic ” It is not known whether the unspeakable
sensation-vendor induced M’Cullough or an imitator to
rave into the phonograph, but the actor’s disgusted
friends admit that the instrument gives a lifelike repro-
duction of his rambling utterances, and are aching for an
opportunity to lock the phonograph man in a room with
a few dozen lunatics.
Verdi’s tuneful old operas, with their luridly tragic
stories and orchestration to match, continue to hold their
popularity beside the works of the French and modern
Italian schools. The programme for the first week of the
present season at Covent Garden (London) was “La
Boheme,” “ Un Ballo in Maschera,” “ La Boheme ” again,
“II Trovatore,” “ Manon Lescaut,” and “La Tosca.” The
birth dates of the Verdi operas, “ II Trovatore ” and “ IJn
Ballo,” are 1853 and 1858, and the libretto in both cases is
of the “ penny dreadful ” order. But the melodies !
A wholly unexpected hit was made with an amateur
dramatic performance in a Victorian provincial town
somewhat recently. A young man and a young
woman sat in a conspicuous place in the stalls. Every
time the tall, picturesque hero on the stage embraced the
small, pert heroine, the young man in the front stalls
embraced the girl alongside him and kissed her with a
noise like the report of a Royal Commission. Some
people recognised the young man in the stalls as the
husband of the pert heroine on the boards, and imparted
their information to their neighbors with the result that
the whole audience learned the facts, and the manner in
which the pair in the stalls went two better thari the
couple on the stage provoked thunders of and
took all the stiffening out of the drama. The pert
heroine broke down in the most, affecting love scene in
the third act as a consequence of the nefarious goings-on
of her husband. The latter, it seems, strongly objected
to his wife’s weakness for amateur acting, and this was
his protest.
Some of the Australian singing crowd who have adopted
the names of their native places for platform purposes in
England are beginningto see that the practice isnotagood
one, and there is talk of the fantastic nomenclature being
dropped by at least one of the number. England has
seen among others, Melba, S.vdna, Ballara, Benda, D’Argo,
Yarra, and Bathurst (Patrick’s right name is Kenna).
There were, of course, heaps of places still to be selected
for musical celebrity. “ Croajinga” would have been ex-
cellent, but it would have been run hard by “ War-
rackna” and “ Upotipotpa.” Also YVooHoomoola would
have supplied a deficiency, and Signorina Lilly Bucka
should have raked in plenty of engagements. It is really
a pity that the demand for such names has run out, for
the supply is practically unlimited.
Mux,sky's tells of Nat Goodwin and Maxine Elliot, that
Nat had completed all his arrangements in ’Frisco for
Australia, with Blanche Walsh as leading lady. Then
Maxine came along, and he fell madly in love with her.
He was willing to pay the expenses of two leading ladies,
but Maxine was under professional engagement to
Frawley. Frawley was persuaded to allow Maxine to
follow Goodwin to Australia in one month, Nat promising
in return to appear for him when he came hack, and
making him a present of the right to play “A Gold
Mine.”

Sandow, strong man and showman, recently returned
to England after an eighteen months’ sojourn in various
corners of the universe. A London interviewer was made
the receptacle of a stiff yarn about a gluttonous Parsee
Grcesus who was reputed to eat and drink enough daily to
supply a small zoo, and, as a consequence, developed
elephantiasis to an alarming extent. European doctors
failed to effect a cure, but the modern Samson brought
him down to normal in six months, and received a fee of
£lO,OOO and princely trimmings in the shape of yachts,
carriages, motor-cars, and such like. An offer to remain
and continue the good work for three years longer for
£30,000 was refused. And thereupon the digestive organs
of the interviewer’s readers promptly refused to act—
allee same the Parsee gent.
“L.”: Nellie Stewart told an Argus interviewer that
her intention of making a few farewell appearances in
Melbourne in comic opera had to be abandoned because
“ there were no people we could find capable of filling
the parts.” Then Manag'er Musgrove let loose a “good
old times” lament: “That’s what’s wrong with comic
opera now. It isn’t that there are no more Gilbert and
Sullivan pieces ; the trouble is that there are no people to
produce them if they were written. There’s not a comic
opera prima donna in the English-speaking world—a
woman who possesses the personal charm and vivacity
that’s necessary.” If the last statement is anywhere
near correctness (which is open to considerable doubt), is
it not dearth of demand that causes the small supply ?
Mr. Musgrove has not given much encouragement to
comic opera recently. Surely he did not expect a" full
company of qualified actors to be waiting on the door-
mat till he was ready to vouchsafe them a three nights’
engagement. Considering how short a time it has been
founded, the Williamson Gilbert and Sullivan , Co. has
been wonderfully well supplied with capable Australian
girls, several of whom need only time and opportunity to
develop all the qualities mentioned.
“ Cockaigne ” : This year’s may be the last pantomime
at London’s Drury Lane. There were even doubts about
producing it. Last year’s realised the poorest dividend
known. This was attributed to the bad book, and
Burnand writes up “ Cinderella ” this Christmas. How-
ever, it is only galvanising a habit that used to be
bursting with joyous life, when all the West End houses
had their pantos.
Nellie Stewart does not expect to act again in Australia
after this farewell. She hopes that “ Sweet Nell of Old
Drury ” will secure a two or three years’ run in America.
Strange to say, it has never been acted there, though
written by an American. Miss Stewart has never acted in
America since 1 1880, when she was there with the “ Rain-
bow Revels.” She was through America in the St. Louis
Exposition year. Musgrove takes 70 tons of parapher-
nalia. The opening is at the Majestic Theatre, in San
Francisco. f
“ Dip ” : Touching “ Melb.’s ” recent remark about the
fearful originality of the phrase “Unhand me villain ! ” I
suggest tp the bellowdramatists tOTelieve the tension
with the occasional variant “ Lemme go beast!”
Gilbert’s Pooh-Bah was anticipated by Baron Factotum,
a character in Planches burlesque, “ The- Sleeping
Beauty ” (1840). Factotum, who is Great-Grand-Lord-
Everything, soliloqiiises thus :
I shall go crazy. Ye who sigh for place,
Behold and profit by my piteous case.
As Lord High Chamberlain I slumber never;
As Lord High Steward in a stew I’m ever !
As Lord Hig h Constable I watch all day ;
As Lord High Treasurer I have the deuce to pay ;
As Great Grand Cupbearer I’m handled queerly ;
As Great Grand Carver I’m cut up severely.
Iq other States the honors are divided,
But here they’re one and all to me confided.
They’ve buckled Fortune on my back—until
I really feel particularly ill !
Young' man, avoid the cares from State that spring',
And don’t you be a Great Grand anything 1
Another Pooh-Bah ieature occurs in A. C. Benson’s recent
life of “Omar” Fitz Gerald. If Fitz Gerald by chance
spoke sharply to the lad employed to read to him, he
would apologise or offer him a money present, which he
called “ insulting the boy in a pecuniary way.”
Some years ago mummer Marius, who was in Australia
vrith Mrs. Bernard Beere’s Co., was giving evidence in an
English court. “What are you?” he was asked. “An
actor,” he replied. Someone in the body of the court
promptly shouted “ Perjury 1 ’’
“ Kodak ” : Sometimes I go to a students’ concert and
sit on a hard chair. The hall is filled with 2000 women and
several men. The ushers get through their work quickly
—they know what is coming. Women rush in holding on
th ir clothes and towing men—men who’ve been drugged
and bullied and threatened and shadowed for days. When
everyone is seated —on the hard chairs—the orchestra
plays something descriptive of ghosts dancing in a fog, in
order to tone things up a bit and give the girls who are
going to tackle arias time to straighten their fringes.
After the orchestra has finished there is a pause. Then
a girl in evening dress zig-zags through the chairs and
bows frigidly to the people, who applaud wildly
why, Lord only knows! The girl’s accomplice
the pianiste—hurries on to the platform, plumps
down on the stool, and has a word or two with
the piano. A bald-headed man stands by with a flute.
General dead silence. Then the pianiste strokes the key-
board for its whole length twice, like a wrestler feeling
for a hold, which she eventually finds somewhere near its
waist. There is a fierce interchange, in the middle of
which the bass distinctly says ‘“Damn!” The
accompaniste sits up straight and drops her hand. The
lady standing up, gazes at the roof as if imploring mercy
and says “ ’Tis the lark ! ’Tis the lark ! ’Tis the Wow-Wow-
(Foie-Wow !! ” The bald-headed man performs a libel on
the lark. Again the singing woman asserts. “ Yes, yes, I
know ’tis the IFoio-Wow-WOW !” The flautist upends
his instrument in a thirsty way, and the pianiste
hits the piano below the belt when it isn’t looking.
Above the riot the singer tells the bald-headed
one—“Oh, yes, tiiou art the Wow! I know—l know
the Wow-Wow-Wow. Oh-h yes ! ” The bald one
tootles some doubts. “Oh, yes, the Wow thou art!”
(Evidently she knoics the Wow and the sort of noise it
makes when it’s bothered.) Then the plot develops—
accusations and denials —with the piano raging in the
background, and eventually the man with the flute gives
in and retires convicted of being either the Lark or the
Wow. When the applause has subsided (everything is
applauded) another girl sings a frantic aria with great
expression. It is jn Cherokee, and nobody understands
it or knows what she is talking about. There is,
however, a great quantity of “Wo.v ” in it, and as a
Cherokee screech is just as intelligible as an English one,
she gets an ovation after her final “ Wow,” and is per-
suaded to come back and do the most excited part all
over again. Most of the high-class arias are nothing but
slices of tombstoney music separating isolated yelps of
“ Wow ! ’’—that is in the slow, impressive parts, when the
policeman is supposed to be walking round the tote. In
the frantic parts it is merely an otherwise nice girl glaring
wildly at the Girls of the Old Brigade while she howls
something that sounds like “ Gerfushen !—gerfushen !
What-o ! tut-tut! Wow —wow !! ” If I had a passion for
sitting on hard chairs and hearing women calling out
“ Wow ! ” I would go oftener than I do to the students’
concerts.
The anti-Home Rule petition, prepared by
the Orange lodges in Melbourne, is being largely
signed by knickerbockered infants, dragged in by
their masters and pastors to help on the bad cause
with a labored and smudgy autograph. This al-
ways happens when religious bodies start on pe-
tition work. It is regarded as good work to shove,
a pen into the bhubby fist of the newest baby and
let its father scrawl its name to the tail of a
lengthy piece of paper, and pass the thing off as
the mature opinion of an adult voter. When
carefully scrutinised, the sense of honor possessed
by the average religious body in political matters
seems several shades below that of the gentleman
who operates with three peas at the pony races.
The sum of £5OO a year each has been
voted by N.S.W. Assembly to the families of the
late Frederick Bridges and Alexander Oliver.
The reason assigned was that each deceased had
rendered long and faithful service to the State.
Yet the idea of rewarding similar services in the
cases of poorly paid public servants never occurs
to Parliament. If Alexander Oliver as a Land
Appeal Court Judge on £2OOO a year, and F.
Bridges as Under-Secretary at £lOOO, could not
leave their descendants well enough off to save
them accepting charity from the State, what
chance has, say, the public school teacher who,
after 30 years’ service, is rewarded with about £4
a week ?
The Australian National Defence League
meeting, held in Melbourne, showed the same
pathetic lack of enthusiasm which characterises
all defence proposals in Australia. The audience
numbered only about 700, and although Prime
Minister Deakin was beautifully eloquent, he said
nothing. He didn’t commit himself or his Go-
vernment, directly or indirectly, to constructing a
fleet, however small, to adopting any definite
system to furnish a really good ..military defence,
to setting up an arms and ammunition factory,
to building the transcontinental railways which
we must have if the outlying parts of the country
are to be made defensible, to an immigration
scheme in order to supply the country with more
defenders ; in fact, he skimmed gracefully over
everything—just like a yacht. Then he had a
lovely peroration j ust like a red and gold sunset —
even among the summer hats in David Jones’s
window there is nothing more lovely or more
nicely trimmed than that peroration—and after
that Deakin sat down beautifully. Then State
Premier Bent rose and said gruffly, among other
things, that he had been, a sergeant once. Bent
seems to have been everything once.
The trial of Lionel Terry for killing one
Chinaman, Joe Kum Yung, in the interests of
racial purity at Wellington.(M.L.), lasted only one
hour, but during that time the tall ex-cavalryman,
faultlessly dressed, dignified in bearing, and
speaking with easy fluency, made a lot of people
about the court feel decidedly uncomfortable.
He mentioned that the ideas of Asiatics on the
subject of giving evidence in court supplied only one
of many reasons why they should be excluded
from the country. He pointed to the presence of
a Jew Crown prosecutor and other Hebrew officials
about the court as proof of the necessity for
taking drastic steps to rouse the people to
a perception of what a hold the Asiatic was getting
on the country. The Jew and the Chinaman, he
argued, were both Asiatics, and should both be ex-
cluded as undesirable. And the Crown Prosecutor
and other Hebrew officials felt glad that Terry met
Joe Kum Yung first that particular Sunday
evening. Also, judging by the nature ,of the
correspondence in the papers for a few days
following the trial, it became evident that Terry’s
remarks about the Jews had soaked into quite s.
lot of people. It really looks as though it would
be necessary for a proportion of the Jews in each
community to devote themselves to hod-carrying
and road-mending, just to supply the race with a
scrap of reputation for genuine hard work.
The one item knocked right off the N.S.W.
estimates th'is year was the £525 for members of
the Public Service Tender Board. This item has
a history. A few years ago the tender business
was placed under the supervision of a Board con-
sisting of the most highly-paid officers of the Public
Service. These people sat during office hours,
and each time they met they were paid £1 Is. An
amazed Assembly spoke to Premier See about it,
and the item only passed that year on the under-
standing that thereafter each member was limited
to £5O a year and must earn the money after office
hours. Ever since then the Board has met at
4.31 p.m., it never being convenient to meet
during business hours (when there was no fee).
This year Carruthers refused to justify the vote,
and left the Chamber to show that he washed his
hands of all responsibility. So the item was fired
out. Hereafter the Tender Board will find time
to meet inside office hours or bust.

“ Ed.” : On the Broadineadows (Vic.)
plains all the trunks of the trees lean to the north,
the sweeping . south winds bending them in that
direction ; they are to a great extent sheltered by
the Dividing Range from the full force of the
north winds. On this bleak, wind-swept, low-
hyinf? plain Bent has established his sanatorium
for consumptives. During the summer the sick
ones will be parched and frizzled like peas in a
pan ; during the winter there will not be a corner
where one can get out of the ever-howling gale.
* * * *
The motor-car is one more boon to the
medical profession ; in fact, two more boons. It
enables the prosperous, expensive doctor to cover
more ground and make a greater number of calls
than he could make in his discarded kerndge.
And'then there are the splendid cases that it
creates. The other day, in one of Australia’s
largest cities, a motor ran over an elderly man
and damaged him pretty severely. The catas-
trophe happened near the brass plate of a well-
known surgeon, who promptly came upon the
scene and ordered the victim to be taken into liis
private hospital. The man didn’t die—he was
able to return home in a week. But the motorist
had a “funeral.” His carelessness cost him £GS in
compensation to the damaged one, £5O for the
surgeon’s fee, and a big sum for board, lodging
and nursing at the private hospital.
A measure now in preparation will give
the Sydney City Council large powers to obliterate
its slums. It is proposed to begin by wiping out
the awful regions of Wexford-street and Athlone-
place—the latter a mud-hole which has 4ft. of
water in it when there is a little more rain than
usual. A great new street is to run from Darling-
hurst to Owe’Sullivan’s fresh and unpaid-for rail-
way station, and after this work is done Woolloo-
mooloo is to be torn up by the roots and re-
modelled. These are wise proceedings, but while
the public is glad to hear of them it badly wants
some more information. Are the owners of the
compulsorily-resumed properties to be paid the
ordinary valuation, with 10 per cent, added as
usual for disturbance —10 per cent, for being so
dirty and so much of a public evil that it
is necessary to shift them at public expense
for the health of the community ? In The
opinion the law should rather lay it
down that the owners of such places should only
get half the assessed, values of their rookeries ; it
believes in 50 per cent, fine for being a premedi-
tated disease—not 10 per cent. bonus. Otherwise
the new powers sought by the Council will pro-
bably tend to make owners who are already dis-
reputable try if they can’t, by a little extra dirt
and a little less paint and repair, become just
dirty enough to be resumed at 10 per cent, above
their value. When a slum is so near the limit at
which it gets 10 per cent, added to its price, that
a few more Chinamen and broken railings will do
the trick, there will be a great tendency to get
them. If the slum were instead approaching the
limit at which it might any day be confiscated at
half its value there might rather be a feverish
tendency to clean up.

Apropos of Sydney’s proposed new public
library. Could some one find out what was the
area of the space available to the public in Sydney
Domain when it was at its largest, in what year it
was at its largest, and how much has been gnawed
off since the process of diminution set in. It is
such an easy thing for the tired Treasurer, who
can’t be troubled finding the money for a site for
some new public building, just to “ put it in the
Domain,” that a very steady gnawing process goes
on almost unnoticed.

A new significance is given to the recent
decision of the Melbourne Queen Victoria statue
authorities to use imported instead of Australian
marble, by the discovery that the Carrara quarries
in Italy are owned by an enterprising Yankee,
who also controls the only quarries for statuary
marble in the United States —thus cornering the
market m the commodity. Considering that Aus-
tralia has dozens of likely spots for fine-grained
marbles of apparently lasting qualities—Caloola is
the best known, but Bulumwaal (Gippsland) is
just as promising—it would appear that Australia
should not only provide marble enough for its
own statues and higher-class buildings, but should
be able to create a big export trade in white
“ gibber.”

face to that of Justice G. B. Simpson, of N.S.W.)

The dangerousness of the average Melb.
policeman is so small that the criminal disregards
it altogether. This contempt for the bobby
became so marked some years ago that it was pro-
posed to arm him with a revolver, and now every
constable carries a “ gun.” No provision has ever
been made for teaching him to use the “ gun.” A
couple of months ago an excited policeman pulled
out his howitzer in the lanes round Little Lons-
dale-street, and poured lead after a runaway
assault-and-robbery man, whose health remained
quite unimpaired. Week before last another
“cop,” after a fierce encounter with three un-
armed burglars, rose from behind a heap of stones
over which he had tripped, and emptied his can-
non at the group. The range was short, but again
the offenders departed in good health and spirits.
Some of the police couldn’t shoot Fink’s Build-
ings at ten yards.
TWO GOOD REASONS.
PATER FAMILIAS : “I think you are still very young to ask for the hand of my daughter .”
SUITOR : “ That may he, sir, hut I am bald, and I have rheumatism.”
Bendigo has, in proportion to its white
population, an enormous number of Chinamen.
They herd together in a big camp
over on one side of the city. This
camp, from which a most dilapi-
dated leper was recently taken, is
full of smellful Asiatics, frowsy
women, mangy dogs, dirty half-breed
children, all mixed up with that vile
acrid stink that hangs about the
habitation of the Chow. The camp
covers about a square mile in area.
Its tracks and lanes are littered with
old tins, dirty rags, filthy paper, and
odds and ends of garbage. The
places in which the Chows and their
harems aha families live are low, un-
ventilated, overcrowded, reeking
hovels. The place would be a hot-
bed of disease at the South Pole,
and Bendigo in summer is nearly
tropical. Still it’s nobody’s business
to make Asia . clean up the place.
The Chinaman is held up for ad-
miration by the fat Bendigo mining
men. He makes a living—or pre-
tends to —out of tailings that white
men won’t work. Won’t work,
notice, not can’t work ; and there-
fore he is regarded as ‘‘an important
economic factor.” As a matter of
fact, the Chow on Bendigo lives in
large part on gold purloined from
the mines and amalgam stolen from
the batteries. He shovels over a few
tons of tailings and says he gets the
gold out of them. That is in his
spare time. His real business is fan-
tan, pak-a-pu, dominoes, opium, and
the running of bagnios.
The good old faith-healing and massage
business appears to be becoming a popular side
line with parsons engaged in the religious racket.
At Auckland (M.L.), t’other day, the Rev. M.
Walker (said to be a registered minister in
N.S.W.), sued a man for £4 for the treat-
ment of latter’s wife by massage, electrical
applications, &c. The reverend, besides giving
her internal and external treatment, earnestly
exhorted the lady to have faith in him and the
Almighty. But apparently she had too little
faith—which is the usual explanation—and she
wasn’t cured worth a cent. The Court nonsuited
the parson on the ground that he couldn’t demand
payment, as he wasn’t a registered medical practi-
tioner. It’s a cruel world.
Victorian Royal Commission to inquire
into the alleged criminal idiosyncrasies of the
police force has pretty well fizzled out. Except
that a number of beat-wallopers have been given
an opportunity of expressing their free, unfettered
opinions of their superior officers, nothing has
been gained. These same constables have told
the Commission their ideas of how promotion
should be arranged, and how criminals should be
caught, and how the force should be administered.
This is not the system Thomas Bent adopted with
the dissatisfied railway men, whose intelligence is
of a much higher order than that of the common,
kitchen-garden constable. Thomas apparently
was trying the other extreme of treatment,
and it has resulted in ghastly failure. The chief
abuse-finder in connection with the affair was
one Cornelius Crowe, an ex-constable who runs a
night patrol in the city of Melbourne in opposi-
tion to the State police system. Cornelius has
levelled charge after charge of corruption against
the Police Department, the detectives, and the
Chief Commissioner. Only one detective of the
whole lot has escaped Crowe’s attention. All the
other charges have been diligently inquired into
by the Commission, and shown to be baseless. On
the other hand, Jack Wren and his Fidus Achates,
Paddy Boardman, have taken a hand in the game.
Crowe accused Wren and his friends of squaring
the police. Boardman and Wren have produced
affidavits declaring that Crowe himself was bribed
to give away police information to the betting-
shops. The man who comes out of the affair
next worst to Crowe is David Gaunson, Victoria’s
political firebrand. David was put into Parliament
for the civil service under the Separate Represen-
tation Act. It was the police vote that got him the
seat. He was no sooner in the House, however,
than he became a bosom friend of Thomas Bent,
and his regular postal address was on Bent’s
“ weskit.’’ He also became the avowed enemy of
the Labor party. Crowe filled Gaunson up with
his bogey stories about the police, and it was
Gaunson’s voice always at Tommy’s hairy ear that
induced Bent to grant the Royal Commission.
The finishing touch was put to his cocktailery when
Gaunson appeared before the Commission and
“as his employer” commanded his confidential
clerk not to give evidence. Gaunson will possibly
try to pose before the police next elections as the
man who saved the situation by preventing his
clerk telling things that would have damned the
whole Force.

Sydney Sunday Times prints the pathetic
case of a family of nine, which, already on the
verge of destitution, was recently enlarged by
triplets. The father earns a meagre crust by
hawking, two grown-up sons are out of work, and
a third is onl /on half-time. The poor doddering
sheet heads the case “ The Penalty of Parentage,”
and asks, “ When are we going to have the large
family pension ? ” It can suggest nothing for filling
these empty stumicks better than charity in the
form of “ the large family pension.” If we made
things instead of importing them, there would be
work enough to profitably employ the hawking
father and the three big sons, and enough
wages to support the triplets, but that fact hasn’t
reached the Sunday rag’s intelligence yet.
IF I WERE ROCKEFELLER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The income of Mr. John D. Rockefeller, the American
millionaire, is estimated at £8,000,000 a year.”— Cable
message.
If I were Rockefeller, blessed
With such a pile of money,
This earth would flow at my behest
With cow-juice and with honey.
A willing push I’d get my way,
As soon as I had bought a
Big, brown-stone house at Mosman’s Bay,
Close down upon the water.
I’d have a splendid garden there,
A boathouse and a cellar,
And be a boshter millionaire,
If I were Rockefeller.
The opera girls I’d fetch along
And shout them gowns from Paris,
And hear them sing the latest song
Composed by Charles K. Harris.
The damsels, innocent of pads,
Whose glowing fascinations
Distinguish Davy Jones’s ads.,
Would all get invitations.
And all the jolly girls I knew—
Each bright-eyed fairy-teller—
Could come and spend the summer there,
If I were Rockefeller.
I’d get a crowd of decent pugs.,
Some poets, not addicted
To biting everybody’s lugs ;
Some artist chaps evicted.
We’d spend the mornings going strong—
The sunlit cove we’d swim in,
And bask upon the rocks along
With ripping, dripping woman.
We’d skip or give the ball a punch,
Spar lightly for a spell, or
Throw quoits, before the gong for lunch,
If I were Rockefeller.
When lunch was o’er, I’d rest ; or, if
The sea was blue and pleasing,
I’d scull to Manly in my skiff ;
Or, if the girls were teasing,
I’d forge my motor launch ahead
With cargo plump and pietty,
And leave the ferries all for dea 1,
And land them in the city.
My word ! that motor launch would fly—
I’d make its old propeller
Spin round a hurricane, if I
Were Rockefeller.
At night, the Boulder ban 1 would play
Us tunes that take some licking.
A ballet with May Moore Duprez
I’d have for lofty kicking.
We’d watch them pirouette and whirl,
Until our lively fancies
Would make us seize the nearest girl
And join her in the dances ;
And when she sought the moonlight calm
I’d squeeze her close and tell her
Long tales of love beneath the palm,
If I were Rockefeller.
The city kids by special train
Would travel to the mountains ;
I’d cheer the dead-beats’ dry Domain
By beer in all the fountains.
I’d lend my cash to each old mate,
Twould be my special hobby,
And pay their fines if some late night
They’d chanced to cheek a bobby.
If I’d the stuff, no florid rhyme
Could tell of doings sweller,
And it’s a gross injustice I’mj
Not Rockefeller.
vie.
A.B.
SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hail to the Spring-! The lark, on high, inanely
Carols away until compelled to stop
By want of breath ; and lodgers four insanely
Glare —as they glare on rafts—at one small chop.
Then comes the m.lkman —charioteer erratic—
Like the tornado raging in the lane,
And on the ink-pot’s brim, high in his attic,
The poet howls—and plunges in again.

On the Divorce Court Air :
Every big- divorce suit represents a Grand Parade of
hypocrisy. To the attention of an elderly or middle-aged
gentleman in a wig several gentlemen, not usually quite
so old, adduce evidence and arguments to show that Bad,
in the course of his life, has not been a strict monogamist,
and that Lad hasn’t been quite a Sir Galahad. It is per-
haps mentioned in an awed whisper that Lad has a lady
for mistress instead of for wife, and that Bad neglects
his spouse to pay indiscreet attentions to Miss Gay.
Whereupon the Judge is profoundly shocked, and oounsel
suffer from emotion too deep for tears, and the jury,
if there is a jury, blinks its eyes in blank wonder-
ment that such things should be. Even the court-crier
looks sad and solemn. And nary a person winks. To
wink would be to spoil the game, and this Divorce Court
game is played with the deadly seriousness of a test
cricket match. It is British Respectability’s solemn
testimonial to itself. The proceedings declare clamantly :
“This is unusual; quite unusual. We never do such
things as-a general rule in our community. Observe our
air of shock at the painful revelations that all are not like
us. When we married we were innocent young men ;
and ever since we have been very good indeed, and have
never, never wandered from the matrimonial fold.”
In other places, which are not so British in sentiment,
the Divorce Court is quite different. Monsieur, who has
been found out with Mam’selle Fi-fi, takes an obvious
pride in the fact that Fi-fi is something of a peach. Or
Madame, against whom the case is going, shows a modest
disconcertment certainly at such things being talked
about before strangers, but it is a disconcertment very
much modified by her air of “The poor man loved me
so. What could Ido but console him ? ”
In the U.S.A.—where a flourishing Divorce Court busi-
ness exists—they might, ere this, have found a match for
the British Divorce Court Air, but, unhappily, in the land
of the wooden ham, Divorce has sunk to the level of a
trifle. Phineas D. cuts the bond because Columbia serves
the coffee cold in the morning, and Lycia shunts Cyrus Q
because of his gauche taste in brown boots. There is thus
seldom a chance for anything to happen to put on Airs
about. The British Divorce Court Air of sodden and
astounded wonderment that such things should be is
indigenous to the British Isles and is peculiar to them and
to Australia, to which spot it has been transplanted.
Possibly it is one of the secrets of England’s greatness. A
nation that could play poker with such an air would bluff
tiie Universe down to its last chip.

“ Terence ” writes :
The Women’s Branch of the Australian Liberal Union
carried Mrs. Vandeleur Kelly’s motion “ That no midwife
should be allowed to act as such unless possessing a cer-
tificate from a recognised midwifery school.” This may
be all very well if confined to cities or large centres, but
thousands of bush children are born with very little “cer-
tificated ” assistance. The most gruesome bush memory
I am oppressed with has to do with a confinement in a
bush hamlet, where the sole medical man had scared the
fairly competent but uncertificated old midwife with the
terrors of a then proposed midwifery law. The dis-
grace to Esculapius backed against the rickety bed
with one hand clenched and a glass of whisky
in the other, vowing he would have the old
woman gaoled or hanged if she lent a hand. “ It’s my
job,” he yelled, “ and I must have £lO for it.” The owners
of the hut hadn’t a penny, but a couple of people volun-
teered to advance £3. “Go to blazes with your £3,”
yelled the Disgrace. “ Think a gentleman like me ’ll
work for £3? I must have the money in my fist, or she
can die, and if that old harridan puts a finger on her I’ll
hang her.” A total of five pounds was made up, and
then seven and a fresh bottle of whisky was reached, and
the Disgrace was left to his professional duties —which, it
is fair to add, he successfully performed.

Drinking is a dangerous vice for doctors.
Sometimes they poison themselves, which is
painful. Sometimes they kill their patients,
which is sad for the patients. Sometimes they
just miss cases and fees. But Dr. Herbert Watson
Brownrigg struck something worse than any of
these catastrophes. He went on a howling drunk at
Clermont (Q.), and, according toliis own story, woke
up to find himself married to a lady he did not
want. He said he had a hazy recollection of
seeing a woman in a rather striking red dress, and
also remembered being surprised at meeting that
woman, or some other woman, in a nightdress.
His companion swore that the lady carried the
doctor off to his room by physical force, and
locked herself in with him all day, and took him
out in the evening and married him. The lady
swore that the doctor was quite sober, and made
violent love to her, offering to settle as much
as £lOOO on her if she would marry him out
of hand. She said it was not a matter of money,
but he insisted, and she had to let him have his
own way. The parson had a very distinct recol-
lection of the affair—it was the second of two
cases in which he had been paid £5 for doing the
trick, and £5 marriages were rare. The doctor
was, he said, perfectly sober, and acted and spoke
like a polished gentleman of the world. He was
pained and shocked when he received a letter
from the doctor asking him what was the name of
the lady he had been married to, and he didn’t
answer the letter. The jury gave a verdict in
favor of the lady in the striking red dress, and the
Judge remarked that there were some “lovely
liars ” on one side or the other.

Melbourne Argus interviews many Mel-
bourne employers to demonstrate the impropriety
and impertinence of under-paid and over-worked
clerks in seeking longer salaries and shorter shifts.
Most of the bosses are indignant at the want of
ambition in their clerks, and explanations reveal
the fact that “ambition,” in this case, means an
ardent desire to work longer, oftener, and more
(jheaply for the beloved boss. One boss exclaims
bitterly : “The cleik will not settle down seriously
to the business of life. After office hours and on
every holiday he is off after pleasure.” This is
very, very wrong. The clerk should employ his
time on holidays and after hours, sitting with ice
on his head, thinking up things that will benefit
his master’s business and help him to be a great
Lord Mayor, and eat with Governors, and even-
tually get a K.C.M.G.-ship all to himself.
A CASE FOR DYNAMITE.
ENTHUSIASTIC AMATEUR: “ Who is he? Why,
that’s ‘ Ironbark’ Bill Biggins, the champion
heavyweight, a glutton for punishment. You
couldn’t kill him with an axe!"
HIS FRIEND: “ What a pity!”
Two members of the great Brown family
have caused the law considerable anxiety. Last
August, Mr. Brown aspersed Mrs. Brown’s char-
acter, in consequence of which Mrs. Brown had
the law on him, and obtained an order to make
him pay ss. a week for a child for whose existence
he had declined to accept responsibility. Mr.
Brown thereupon appealed to a Judge in cham-
bers, who quashed the order. Mrs. Brown
took the matter to the Full Court, which
quashed Mr. Brown on the irrelevant ground
that a relieving clerk had signed certain
depositions, and of the existence of the
relieving clerk the Full Court was not legally
aware. Brown, who had had nothing to do w r ith
the relieving clerk, and did not know him from a
sheet of bark, appealed to the High Court. The
High Court took the matter home to bed, slept on
it, looked up aged precedent, and finally decided
that the truth of the depositions was not cavilled
at, and that Brown was not responsible for the
relieving clerk, so it squelched the Full Court. It
is a horribly expensive way to get satisfaction, but
when you do get it it’s all there.

The brutality-loving honorarium again ! In
a case of wife-beating at the Carlton (Melb.) court
lately, the arresting constable swore he found the
victim “covered with blood ; her hair was down,
and there was a gash on her left ear. There was
also an abrasion on the nose, and the front of her
dress was saturated with blood. The accused was
lying on the bed, and there were pools of blood
on the floor. He said he wanted to get rid of his
wife.” Then the horrible J.P. spoke to him
quite firmly, and fined him £3 —a whole £3—for
what looked like a fairly good attempt at getting
rid of the lady per morgue. If there was a
reasonable supply of healthy public opinion in the
country that kind of J.P. would be cut dead by
every decent citizen. But the law has its splashes
of vigor. The South Melbourne Court has just
handed out “12 months’ hard” to a “Yarra
Pirate ” —an aged derelict who for years had
meandered round the wharves picking up uncon-
sidered trifles and doing a bit of smuggling, or
butter agency, or something of the kind.
* * * *
The Chow, although he practically domi-
nates two big industries in Melbourne—furniture-
making and cabbage-culture—is a diminishing
agony. In the middle sixties there were quite
70,000 Chinamen in Bent’s State. To-day a good
authority—Moy Ling, the Methodist Yellow
missionary—estimates Bent’s Chow subjects at
7000. Some have gone to China in the flesh, but
the vast majority have gone back as bones. The
Rev. Moy invites goodly Australians to lament
the loss, as it diminishes the missionaries’ oppor-
tunities of saving the Chinese. Good Australians,
being more desirous of saving Australia, will bear
up, however.
