The Dailey divorce suit was a case of pay-
ing a greal deal of money for a very small quan-
tity of information. After a vast expenditure of
costs the only question to which the jury gave an
unhesitating answer was the one which asked if
the parties were married, and that was the only
point on which the parties themselves were fully
agreed before the trouble started.
“Pelican ” : Easterners scoff at Adelaide’s
trams and tabernacles, but there are good points
about the village. It is the only town in
Australia where they press Aus-
tralian goods on you. I
went into a draper’s
shop to buy sing-
lets. The man
behind the
counter pro-
duced S. A.
flannel at
once. “The
best we
keep,” he
said ; “ it’s
all wool.” I
went into
another shop
and asked for
Stephen s’
ink. “We
don’t keep
it. You’ll
find our own
just as good.
The only dif-
ference is
that the im-
ported ink
costs a shil-
ling, and we
sell ours for
sixpence.
You see.
ours doesn’t
have to pay
duty.” And, sure
enough, it was just
as good. This is
some of it. I asked
a publican for brandy.
He handed out S. A. brandy, as a
matter of course. That clinched it.
Evidently I was in a country that
believed in itself. Add that Ade-
laide is the cleanest, best laid-out,
and almost the most beautiful city
in Australia, and that it has in the
Advertiser one of the few dailies
that a good Australian can read without
disgust, and you will See that it is not the
sort of town that Sydney—where the Stink-
ing Fish come from—can turn up its nose at.
The Guy Fawkes lunacy has blown up at
last and landed two Whangamata (M.L.) men
in hospital. Thinking to afford the kiddies some
amusement they poured two gallons of naphtha into
the harbor from a boat, and after the naphtha had
spread over the water one of them threw a match
overboard. A great explosion followed, the flames
enveloping the boat and ascending some ten feet
high till the affair looked like a volcano. Their
next address was Waihi infirmary for scorched
lunatics.
It may interest
some people, now
that the Orange body
has decided to raise
sectarian strife and
the Irish Home
Rule issue at the
next Common-
wealth elections,
to note the change
in the spirit of the
times in Ireland
itself. On Queen’s
birthdays and during
royal visits the eques-
trian statue of King
William 111. on College
Green, Dublin, was always
decorated until within a few
years ago. Now the statues
of Grattan, Moore, O'Con-
nell, &c., &c., are decorated
on State days, and “Billy”
is left unadorned. Till early
in the 19th century the statue
was regularly festooned with
flags and flowers on public
occasions, when the Lord
Lieutenant, followed by the
Lord Chancellor, the Judges,
and the Lord Mayor, with the
“Rotten Corporation,” paraded in state
round the image, but all this is pretty
well forgotten. Seeing that Edward VII. owes
his throne to his Stuart descent, any mixing up of
reverence for. the present reigning family with
worship of the Dutchman who chased the Stuarts
from the throne is rather confusing.

When a dog’s conscience is troubling him
he barks all night and keeps the neighborhood
awake, and the human article seems just as
illogical. Last week Sydney was shaken up sud-
denly by a lady who repented. Her story is that
the will—made in her favor—of a wealthy old sea-
captain who lately died in Maoriland was a forgery.
The will was being contested by a nephew ; and
the beneficiary, the two women who witnessed the
document, a Sydney solicitor, and a man who
seems to have been giving a general support, were
to leave for Wellington to fight the case.
They were also to leave with some money
borrowed from another Sydney solicitor. They
didn’t get away because the beneficiary was
ill in bed with her conscience or something. A
nurse aggravated the disease by reading the Scrip-
ture to her, and she suddenly sat up in bed, and
repented bitterly, and began to bark. Logically, of
course, a woman in an abnormal state of repentance
should simply have gone to the police station,
given herself up, and asked to be put on short
rations in the most uncomfortable cell available.
It was her own repentance, and she should have
made certain of her own cell. Instead of
this she seems to have gone forth and
given so much alleged information to the
other solicitor—the one who lent the money
—that four people who hadn’t repented at all were
run in. Supposing her story is proved true, these
other people will fall in for trying to help her to
a great deal of money. Presumably, though, the
lady who repented will simply be used as a wit-
ness, so she won’t fall in, unless she imprisons
herself in her own coal cellar after the case is over,
or spanks herself for her share of the sin. The
other people, whether the case is proved or not,
have, quite naturally, only a limited opinion of
this kind of repentance.
* * * *
Had Miss Cross, the Randwick girl whose
body was found at the foot of the Coogee cliffs the
other day, lived in the time of Theocritus or some
of the other polished and imaginative Greek poets,
she would probably have been immortalised as a
victim of the jealousy of Venus. She was a
respectable and unusually hard-working girl who
toiled by day for an employer, and spent her even
ings making dresses for friends and neighbors.
Her knowledge of the other sex seems to have
been restricted to one young man, who called
round in the evenings, and talked to her while she
toiled on the collective frock of the neighborhood.
Nothing could have been more eminently respect-
able and dully prosaic. Then one day the girl
confided to her sister and father that she had
fallen a “ victim ” to some man whose name she
would not disclose. Apparently she had previ-
ously confided in the young man who used to
cheer up the dressmaking in the evenings, for he
had sorrowfully packed up and departed to Vic-
toria. She left a letter deploring her “ fall ” and
the fact that it had brought ruin and disgrace on
her father and his family. And then, when the
doctor examined the body, he found that the fall
and the unknown man were mere myths—illusions
of a disordered imagination.

“ Viator ” : Sydney and Melbourne are
sadly in need of national portrait galleries. What
a ramshackle lot there is in Melbourne, the basis
being an assumption that the Australian nation
has been made by its imported Governors. Other-
wise the most conspicuous portraits are those of
Snooks and Bloggins, contributed either by them-
selves or by their admirers. Yet the Melbourne
Corporation has quite an ideal gallery, in its
limited outlook. How much more rational than
Westminster Abbey is the London National Por-
trait Gallery!


The WILD CAT COLUMN
So long as State Treasurers borrow, and pay for
ordinary works with the borrowed money, and steal the
proceeds of public-land sales, and pay for ordinary services
with the stolen money, the monthly State revenue
returns are to a great extent a delusion and a snare.
They are also a very imperfect delusion and a snare,
because hardly any two Treasurers furnish the same
details, and several of them don’t furnish any details to
speak of; and a fool public is apparently not sufficiently
interested in its own money-matters to kick about it. But
as far as the figures go, here are some comparisons built
upon them: —

For the large increase shown by N.S.W. a good season
and a record railway traffic are mainly responsible. The
railway revenue shows an increase of £213,722. The
Commonwealth, which Carruthers says is filling N.S.W.’s
cup of iniquity, has also poured in £82,362 more this year
than it did last; and £82,362 is a brand of iniquity which
deserves to be received with something more than'a snarl.
The Queensland increase is due to the same two causes—
Commonwealth refunds are £54,756 better, and £75,744
more has come from the railways. The Vic. returns show
a heavy decrease for the month alone ; but in Nov., 1904,
there was a very large payment from the sugar bonus
trust acp.ount. Westralia continues to drift steadily on
to the rocks, and Prem.er Rason rushes to save the situa-
tion with a tax on the totalisator, or on periwinkles, or
something. Westralia hasn’t even the ghost of a land-tax
yet in force ; and Rason lets that obvious plan of raising
something substantial stand over until be has achieved
the quarter-million deficit that he looks forward to with-
out any alarm whatever.

Victoria and Tasmania furnish no figures whatever on
this subject. Bringing revenue and expenditure together
we get these results
N.S. Wales.—The present deficit is £252,566. At this
time last year the State had a deficit of £574,439, and the
year finished with a surplus (how realised is another
story) of £141,843. As so far this year an improvement of
£321,873 is shown, there should be enough available at
the end of the year to start a Public Works B'und. In
fact, there isn’t any reason why the Public Works Fund
shouldn’t be started now.
Queensland.—At the end of Nov., 1903 (Philp and
Cribb were then in office) the State had £336,630 saved up
towards the current half-year’s interest-bill ; at Nov., 1904
(soma time after Morgan and Kidston had got to work), it
had £590,653 in hand ', it has now £680,491 available.
Compared with last year it is £89,838 better off ; com-
pared with 1903, it is £343,861 better off.
Westralia.— The present deficit is £124,109, of which
£77,587 has been piled up during the past five months and
£46,522 was brought forward from last year. And
Westralia is the State which will this year pay to (mostlv
absentee) mine-owners over £2,000,000 in dividends ; and
a mining dividend is only made by digging up wealth and
carting it away. In this case it is being carted away
where it can’t be reached by any future Westralian
taxation.
S. Australia. —For the first quarter of last financial
year the deficit was £49,369 ; this year it is £59,516.
Last year the State wound up with a surplus which you
could see if you looked hard at it with a microscope ; this
year the position is £10,147 worse. Yet the Tory Leg.
Council has refused Price and his Treasurer Peake some
small increase in the Land Tax, on the ground that it isn't
wanted.
So far "this year N.S.W. has spent £622,845 of
borrowed money, which is at the rate of just upon
£1,500,000 a year. This means an annual addition
of, say, £55,000 to the interest bill. The State is
having a season of unusual prosperity. Money is more
abundant than it has been for many years. The wool
clip is very good, and the price very high. The harvest
prospects are excellent. The State’s metals are at boom
prices. And still Carruthers the Reformer borrows at the
rate of £1,500,000 a year. When will N.S.W. be prosper-
ous enough, in the Reformers’opinion, to pay its way?
Queensland has so far this year spent £90,101 of loans,
as against £85,668 to Nov. 30, 1905—a small offence, but a
growing one. The Queenslander ought to know what it
feels like to be in pawn, and what getting deeply into
pawn means for his State. Cohen calls for £4l of every'
£lOO raised by taxation, or collected in fares or freights,
or received from any other services in Queensland. And
with that knowledge, the Queenslander should be on his
guard against the drift that even now seems to have
started—just as it started in Maoriland after that State’s
historical cleaning-up under Bailance.
♦ * * *
Bam ford* has a motion on the business paper of
the House of Reps.—“ That the Government should forth-
with introduce legislation for the better protection of
the interests of Australian policy-holders in foreign life-
assurance co.’s.” What is the matter with legislation for
the better protection of Australian policy-holders in all
offices? Policy-holders in American offices are, at any
rate, protected to this extent, that there is an Insurance
Department in New York, which makes an annual inves-
tigation of the affairs of all offices in that State,
and that is some guarantee of their solvency ;
hut even that much protection is not accorded to
policy - holders in Australian offices. No doubt
policy-holders in the best Australian offices do not
at present need any more protection than their excellent
management affords ; but Australian offices are not all
best. As to. Foreign offices, there seems no reason why,
especially in view of-the recent scandals, Australia should
not follow European precedents, and prescribe that
foreign offices should maintain in Australia assets (to be
subject to valuation by the Australian insurance depart-
ment) equal to the value of their policy liabilities.
♦ * * *
In the report of (Queensland Railway Commis-
sioner Thallon on the Cloncurry railway it is stated that
there are 3800 tons of rails and fastenings (equal to about
50 miles of line) lying at Croydon (N.Q.). This material
was imported in 1890 for the Normanton-Clonourry line,
and present cost of same, plus interest, must be easily
£50,000. This is a good sample of how Australia, in its
borrowing stupidity, is burning the candle at both ends.
Fifty thousand pounds worth of railway material gradu-
ally growing in cost per the interest bill, and just as
-gradually vanishing to nothing per the rust. It was
originally stated that Thallon would recommend these
rails for use in the Richmond-Cloncurry extension, hut
he has not done so. All circumstances considered, per-
haps he cannot be blamed in this particular case, but the
sooner those rails are put in use or honorably written off
as a bad debt the better.
Apparently it doesn’t take much in the way of
accounts to satisfy N.S.VY. Parliament. Sydney Harbor
Trust laid its figures before the Assembly, the other day.
and shoved that for the past year (to June 30) the
balance available to meet interest, &c., was £175,736.
About a dozen officers certified that the accounts were
correct, or that “ the writing above is a true copy of the
original statement whereof it purports to be a copy,” and
so ne more initials checked it and found it correct, and
somebody else initialled the other initials as a guarantee
of good faith. But nobody troubled to say what the
capital expenditure had been, or how much the
interest bill was, or whether the money available
to pay the “interest, &c.,” was sufficient for the
purpose—whether, in short, this concern in which
over £4,000,000 of loan money has been sunk, is
paying its way, or whether it is another of the permanent
and reproductive undertakings that have to be subsidised
oat of taxation. Apparently, however, according to the
Auditor-General (Annual Report for 1904-5), the capital
expenditure has been £5,187,044 ; and, taking the
average interest payable on the public debt at 3'582 p.o.
(with an allowance for some portion of the capital not
spent during the early part of the year), the interest was
£184,970. As the net earnings were £175,736, the deficit,
on this basis, was £9234. And the statutory sinking fund
contribution has simply been allowed to drop through a
crack and become lost to sight. Parliament said there
should be a sinking fund, and provided for it
in the Harbor Trust Act of 1901 ; but the
Commissioners later on reported that sinking
funds were a humbug-, or words to that effect, and, so far
as can be seen, they have made no attempt to create the
fund which Parliament said should be created. Anyhow,
there is no sinking fund. Parliament started off by
deciding that in this matter the State should be honest
and pay off its debt; it knew that the wharf, and the
resumed slum building-, or the new building for that
matter, couldn’t last for ever, and it decided to wipe out
the capital cost by instalments. But when its instruc-
tions in the matter are put away in a bole, Par-
liament doesn’t trouble. It, apparently, wasn’t in
earnest about the matter ; if a sinking fund could be
dredged up out of the harbor along with the decaj'ed dog
and the cat that wasn’t wanted, well and good ; but if it
had to be worked for, and paid for—well, Parliament
would rather be dishonest. All the same, it borrowed
£4,000,000 or so from Cohen on the express statement
that there was a sinking fund in the Bill under which the
money was to be spent ; and if Cohen tells the N.S. Wales
Parliament that it is a fraud, he will seem to have pretty
good grounds for his remarks.
* * * *
The farmer who believes that Freetrade lowers
the prices of the articles he uses is just now getting
another lesson on the folly of that pet theory. Bran bags
and cornsacks —which are on the free list—are selling-
to-day at ss. 6d. and 7s. 3d. respectively in Melbourne,
the Calcutta quotations thereof being 4s. 3d. and ss. 9d.
delivered free on Melbourne wharf ; so the farmer is
paying the importer about 30 per cent, of the value of the
article for the trouble of carting it from the wharf to the
store. These bags have advanced 2s. per dozen in the
last twelve months solely through the importers woefully
under-estimating the consumption. Hessian bags, though '
made of the same material, have only risen 2d. per dozen
in the same time (this being the actual advance on the
raw material). The difference is solely owing to the fact
that there is a 10 per cent, duty on hessians, and they are
consequently made in Australia.

The profits have again been seriously affected by heavy
discounts paid on debentures sold during the year. The
practice of the Savings Bank (of which institution this is
the advances department) is to regard as a liability the
full amount of the debt which will have to be paid on
maturity, reckoning the discount on the other side as a
temporary asset, and gradually wiping it off out of
profits. In all the discounts have amounted to £22,345,
of which £5162 has been written off, leaving £17,153 yet
to be dealt with. In the foregoing table, however, as
this £17,183 is purely a paper asset, it has been written
off at once, and only the apparently solid reserves are
shown. The effect of issuing, debentures at a discount is
to make a saving in the interest bill ; so that, if there
were not this loss on debenture-issues, there would be
less profit shown on current operations. The main
difference is that the loss is charged at once instead of
slightly heavier interest affecting the earnings to the end
of the chapter. The discount for the past year shown
above (£8241) was in respect to £200,000 worth of
debentures—half issued in August, 1904, and the other
half in March, this year. These issues were the dearest
money yet raised by the bank ; the early lot (3j per cent,
at a minimum of £95 10s.) costs £4 2s. Id. per cent, on
the minimum, the later lot costs £4 os. 6d. per cent. As
the bank’s expenses amount to over j per cent., it would
have been a losing business bad the old maximum rate of
4£ per cent, for advances been maintained ; at 4J per
cent., which the Commissioners have been permitted to
charge since the beginning of the year, a bare margin of
profit—certainly not sufficient to cover risks—is shown
on the dearest money. Fortunately, however, for the
reserves, it isn’t all dearest 'money ; £lOO,OOO raised in
1901 is costing only £3 7s. per cent.

There is nothing to explain the jump in expenses. But
in the previous year, when the expenses were below the
average, only one lot of debentures was sold ; this past
year, two lots were pushed off, and of this money there
remained in hand at the end of the year something like
£175,000 to go on with, as against £47,000 twelve months
before. Most likely this accounts for some at least of the
rise in the expense rate ; had the bank kept off the market
for a couple of months more (as it might have done,
only money' seemed very plentiful and cheap at the
moment) the expenses might have been kept at least
under 13s. The S. Australian rate, for-the same service,
is 13s. Bd. ; the Maoriland rate 4s. lid. But Maoriland
has more than twice as much money out. An extremely
satisfactory feature is that, though in all £1,890,299 has
been advanced in Victoria, the total of properties in
possession is only £2561, whijh is less even than the total
of 12 months ago ; while of this year’s interest (£60,595)
only £ll is in arrear, and of the repayments of principal
(£27,151) only £8 has not been paid. And yet the stupid
party is always telling us that once the State touches
banking and starts making advances, every politician will
unload his dead-beats on to the department, with
disastrous consequences. The most disastrous conse-
quence visible here is that “ strug-gling farmers ” have
this year been able to borrow at 4f p.c., on easiest terms
as to repayment, though it has actually been costing the
State over 4 p.o. to raise the money ; and still the busi-
ness has been directly profitable to the State.

Deposits at interest (£1,378,338) are £186,800 more than

more rapidly than advances ; still the disproportion isn t
so marked as in connection with most Australian banks.
And the E.S. & A. has had other good use for the money
too ; the last of the old 4 per cent, terminable deposits
fell due during t.he year and were got out of the way.
But notwithstanding that heavy repayment, the liquid
assets still represent as much as 60 per cent, of those lia-
bilities to the public not covered by the scheme of recon-
struction.

Eighteen months ago, or thereabouts, this co., which has
had many vicissitudes, passed into almost entirely new
control, and great changes were promised. The year
shows that some progress has been made, though nothing
of a sensational sort. The expense rate, though still
exceedingly high, has, however, been materially re-
duced :
Outgoings (apart from Claims) Compared with Income.
1897 .. £6O 13 2 per cent. 1902 .. £lO7 14 3 per cent.
1898 .. 69’ 3 9 „ „ 1903.. 80 10 6 „ „
1899 .. 70 7 0 „ „ 1904.. 76 19 1 „ „
1900 .. 63 4 5 „ „ 1905.. 66 18 11 „ „
1901 .. 59 8 7 „ „
As the business in the industrial department is three times
as great as that in the ordinary department, nothing in
the way of low figures is possible. Yet, as these figures
include ordinary as well as industrial business, and as
ordinary business alone ought never to cost more than 25
per cent., the ratio is still far too high. It is undesirable
that the two classes of business should be lumped together
in this way. They stand on an entirely different footing ;
moreover, until they are separated, no proper comparison
between what this office can offer and what other life
offices can offer is possible. It is not possible to tell,
while the present methods lasts, what real progress the
co. is making in the matter of reducing expenses ; because
a reduction might result from easing-off on the always-
costly industrial business. It is a clumsy way of doing
things, and the new management should end it with all
possible speed.
a • • ( •
In addition to the paper value which has been
added to the buildings, the balance sheet shows as an
asset “ deferred instalments of annpal premiums, £1269”.
This is a wholly unsatisfactory item, which the reputable
office does not long keep on the premises. It is a dubious
asset in any case ; it is unusually dubious in an office
where forfeitures and surrenders are painful and frequent;
and it earns no interest anywhere. As to forfeitures
nothing can be said definitely. But “6968 policies
assuring £163,099 were written in the industrial depart-
ment, and in the ordinary department 727 policies
assuring £52,600, yielding an annual premium of £2588,
were issued.” Policies whicli should have added, say,
£BOOO or £lO,OOO to the income were thus issued al-
together ; yet the premium income was only £315 more
than it was in 1904. And as the claims and surrenders
only amounted to £3665, it looks as though for every
four policies that come in, three drop ojut without
waiting to acquire a surrender value. To send it forth
that during the year so many new policies have been
issued, with never a remark about .the number that have
been thrown up, after probably only a year’s premiums
have been paid, is ingenious, but it might be fairer to the
public to tell the whole tale. It is bad enough to have so
high an expense rate ; it is worse still if a vast proportion
of the business which costs so much to get lasts only -for
a year or so ; for new business posts approximately 10
times more than renewal business. As to the assets, it
was remarked 12 months ago that, on the average
of assurance fund and shareholders’^ capital, interest
and rents showed a return of £4 7s. 4a. per cent, ; and
that looked pretty good. But, on reconsideration,
that presents the matter in much too favorable a light.
The buildings carry a mortgage of £lO,OOO, and' the
revenue account is debited with £4Ol interest on loans.
Interest, &c., received amount to £1209. The net return
is therefore £BOB, which is at the rate of £2 18s. 6d. per
cent. It is clear enough from this, either that the co.
does not charge itself enough for its own office accommo-
dation, or that the assets are much overvalued. If the
fdrmer, then the true expense rate is even higher than it
is represented to be ; if the latter, then the assets ought
to be written down.

This co., which sells land mostly on time-payment, has
had what looks like a very good year ; though until the
last payment is completed it is impossible to tell
how good it really was. The profit disclosed is to
some extent an estimate ; the real profit may be
more, it may be less. All depends on the future. Appa-
rently something in the shape of an inner reserve is
accumulated to meet possible defaults in payments and
losses on that account; the amount of borrowers, and
purchasers’ balances, after deducting this suspense account,
is £75,149, or £13,878 more than it was a year ago. As
against that properties now stand at £26,153 instead of
£37,431 —a reduction of £11,268, so evidently the co. isn’t
making the mistake of buying, in the present state of the
market, for subdivision. Another satisfactory feature is
that debts to the public (including deposits) have been
reduced during the year from £28,463 to £26,219. Evi-
dently this land co., at any rate, is keeping its head so
far—it is selling land, and not buying it ; and it is using
a substantial part of the money it is receiving to pay off
its debts.

Five years ago the shareholders’ capital represented 51
per cent, of the assets ; to-day it only represents 26 per
cent. The capital has decreased ; the deposits have in-
creased enormously. This, of course, means that the
shareholders’ risks have also increased enormously. The
extra £120,000 or so of the public’s money has been lent
out on mortgage ; if the securities slump, it won’t be the
depositors who must stand the racket—it must be the
shareholders. Meantime, no doubt, it is also the share-
holders who get the profits. Five years ago they were at
the rate of £5 10s. per cent, on capital; now they are at
the rate of £lO Is. per cent. It is a situation which calls
for very substantial additions to reserves.

Broken Hill Junction Mining Co.
Losses. Written Off. Deficit.

Mar., 1904 .. 1910 1600 27,014

Mar., 1905 .. 3708 1381 85,481
Sept., 1905 .. 2458 1313 39,252
This is three years’ record of the most unsatisfactory
mine at Broken Hill. The greater part of the time little
work was done, and that only to develop the property,
which had been previously worked to a standstill. The
mine contains bunches of very good ore ; but when a
bunch has been struck the market has always bounded up,
and the directors have allowed themselves to be forced or
cajoled into paying dividends which would show a
fair return on the excessive price, without spending
a fair amount on prospecting. Although the losses shown
above haves been made mainly during a development
period, so little ore was opened up that when, a few
months ago, the mill was re-started it had to be stopped
again after about 8000 tons had been put through. In-
cluding a small quantity of rich friable ore which was
sold to smelters, the profit claimed to have been made
was at the rate of 3s. per ton. But there was no real
profit. While this ore was being broken no development
work was done, and the cost of developing this particular
ore was not charged against it. After a few' weeks pros-
pecting the mill has been started again ; and, unless by a
stroke of luck-the co. happens to break into another patch,
another failure will soon be reported. Certainly it
has some-tailings which at present-high prices of metals
may possibly pay to re-treat, just as the South formerly
treated a large quantity at some profit; but not much is
to be hoped from this, because the most easily an
cheaply-won “crop” has already been taken out of tne
material. The mine, being so patchy, is hardly suitable
for separate control; it ought to be amalgamated mtn
some other which could help it through between tune .
At present it is a gambling counter, and under exisling
control onlv to be recommended as such.

CONCENTRATING MACHINERY.
EXPLOSIVES and MINING REQUISITES generally.
CAn-UOM/1-o u " u v Melbourne Manager: C. A. Lempriere.

The Cathcart Proprietary Gold Mine, Ararat,
was the first mine in Victoria to adopt electricity as motive
power for pumping, hoisting and puddling. This plant
commenced work in September, 1904, and has been in
constant and successful operation ever since. It consists
of two return tube boilers, 160 horse-power engine, 110
kilowatt dynamo, switchboard, an electric hoist capable
of lifting 800 tons in 24 hours, an electric three-throw
pump raising 25,000 gallons of water per hour and a 85
horse power motor driving the puddlers. The dynamo
also provides electricity for 40 lights by means of which
the surface and underground workings are lighted.
Experience obtained on this mine during one year’s
service proves that: (1) electricity makes an actual saving
in fuel amounting to about £350 per annum ; (2) the
dynamo and motors cost practically nothing to maintain ;
(3) the cost of lubrication is reduced to a minimum ; (4)
electric motors require much less attention than
steam engines; and finally (5) electricity is much
more flexible than steam power, permitting ready exten-
sion or change as mining conditions frequently require.
The contractors for this installation were the Austral Otis
Engineering Compaq' Limited, corner of Hanna and
Kavanagh-streets, South Melbourne, Sole Agents for Ganz
and Company, Budapest, Hungary.* [1
Financial Times (London), under date of 29th
July, 1905, in reviewing the annual report of The Colonial
Mutual Life Assurance Society Limited, wrote : —“The
annual report of this society for last year indicates an in-
crease in its funds, new premiums, premium income and
income from interest, and also an increase in the average
rate of interest earned by its assets.”*

The old plan of making a friend of the family
the trustee and executor under the head of the family s
will is gradually disappearing ; the new plan is to get the
work done by a trustee company. Such a co. has officers
specially trained for the work, it is always available, its
fidelity is guaranteed by its capital. The Perpetual
Trustee Co., of Spring-street, Sydney, offers all these and
many more advantages.*
* * * *
A trustee co. has only one business the
management of estates committed to its charge. The
inevitable result is that, being expert in a business to
which it gives its constant and undivided attention, it
makes few or none of those mistakes which, when com-
mitted by amateur trustees, are often so costly. Nobody
knows what administration by a private or amateur trustee
will cost ; on the other hand, the Perpetual Trustee Co.,
of Spring-street, Sydney, established under special Act of
Parliament, has its fixed scale of charges, and the testator
knows what the cost will be. The charges are low, and
the faithful performance of the trust is guaranteed by the
co.’s capital, its reserves, and its good name.*
The Perpetual Trustee Co., of Sydney, has
made remarkable progress in securing the confidence of
the public of N.S. Wales. In 1895 the total value of the
trust estates in its control was £2,424,413. In 1905 •
within 10 years—the amount had reached £5,589,194.
This striking success is the best proof of the value of the
services it has rendered to its The company s
address is 2, Spring-street, Sydney ; it undertakes all
business of a li luciary character, and its wide experience
is placed at the disposal of testators, absentees, and
others, at extremely moderate charges.*

A set of Three-Throw Pumps, capable of
delivering 3,000,000 gallons of water per day against a
head of 450 ft. is being built for the Victoria Deep Leads
G M Co., Moolert, Victoria, under the management of
Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and Co. The pumps are plunger
geared, externally packed, and fitted with machine-cut
speer and pinion wheels and a grooved pulley 14ft.
diameter for a rope drive. There are 3ft. valves of the
double flap type, strainers, and air vessels attached to
the suction pipes ; 6 suction and 6 delivery valves of the
double beat type ; 3 sluice valves, and 6 charging pipes.
An air vessel, Bft. long and 27£in. in diameter fitted with
water gauge glass and cocks is placed over the main
delivery junction piece which is situated over centre of
working barrels. The manufacturers are the Austral
Otis Engineering Co., of South Melbourne, who have
attained signal success with the construction of these
pumps.* t 3,

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any si<m of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discretion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Mount Drysdale, Oobar, an interesting mine of
old-time richness which the enterprising Henry B. Coles
took under option to J. Bull some months back, moves
on to the inspection stage. Inspection on Bull’s account
will be made by Danvers Power, which means that the
mine is in for a severe overhauling. Drysdale has in its
favor the fact that it has distributed about £70,000 in
dividends, and it isn’t likely that all the rich pockets have
been tapped ; and, apart from probable pockets, there is
a large accumulation of low-grade ores.
* * * *
C.S.A., Cobar, is still probing its interesting
problem of how far the lode extends east and w'est ; and
at time of writing 160 ft. of crosscutting hadn’t thrown
much light on the matter. North drive at 450 ft. level in
the lead ore shows high-grade stuff, rig'ht across the
drive; and as assays give 60 per cent, lead, 3oz. silver,
and 4jdwt. gold, the gross return per ton on present
metal values maybe estimated at £lO 17s. 6d. Of course
it is impossible on present development to estimate ton-
nage, and until the diamond drills throw some light on
the sulphide values it is just as impossible to estimate the
chances of the mine. There certainly is no reserve of ore
to speak of on the top of present crosscut; but if the
drill holes show an uninterrupted rich passage 300 ft.
lower down, and the drive keeps going north in the 60 per
cent, stuff, the C.S.A. will be on the top rung of mines.
• # * *
Decision of Warden C. J. Helm, at Cobar, in
the Hardie-Andrews disputed occupation of 20 acres
mineral land, south of Gardner’s and 0.5. A., opens up a
new phase in the interpretation of N.S.W. Mining Act.
Andrews’ original lease application, which he sold to
Frank Gardner, proved on survey to be in excess 20
acres ; and as Hardie’s Central blocks joined Andrews’
original pegging, the survey left a gap of 20 acres between
Hardie and Andrews. Both Hardie and Andrews re-pegged
(Hardie for the Central Blocks Co., Andrews for himself).
They made application simultaneously. But Warden
Helm decided that until the original lease of Peter
Andrews was granted or refused by the Minister, the land
wasn’t available. So if a man can embrace 100 acres in
his application for 80 acres, and thus exempt the excess
land, he can likewise, while applying for 80 acres, allow
his pegs to wander in a promiscuous sort of way over a
few square miles of country, and lock up the whole en-
closure until such time as the Minister has dealt with the
matter. If Warden Helm is right, the Mining Act is
worse even than people thought it.
• * * *
Boppy Mountain (N.S.W.), which it may be
again necessary to say isn’t Mount Boppy, although with-
in three miles of that top-notch mine, shows surface assays
up to 4oz. silver, and some lead and gold. All of which,
considering the outcrop, points to a big proposition. The
area is 160 acres, held mostly by mining men branded
“ C.S.A." ; and outside capital will shortly be asked for to
sink present shaft to 400 ft. The richness of the C.S.A. at
400 ft. has stamped those figures on the minds of men,
and justly so, for Cobar is essentially a field of depth.
But if surface indications count, Boppy Mountain has
a big score.
• • • *
Royal Oak of Hauraki, a J. Bull concern,
which is spending some of its accumulated shekels on its
North Boppy option (N.S. W.), is likely to hear encourag-
ing remarks from its chairman, George Hardie, who has
just visited the mine. At the 200 ft. the lode is 15ft. wide,
carrying gold in mineral; and in present sinking of main
shaft, which is in country rock, both mineral and galena are
showing. Policy is to sink to 400 ft. and crosscut to the
lode ; and as the rock drills, air compressor and hauling
gear with which the mine is equipped are just about as
neat as you can find, chances are that, by end of
January, manager Harry Smith will know something.
* * • *
Twelve months’ work at Cobar Gold Mines
(N.S.W.)—the Bull-owned show which has been sitting
on a nest-egg for a couple of years, waiting for something
to turn up—resulted in a profit of £2456. This was a
miserable result, seeing that, besides 18,418 tons of ore,
15,191 tons of tailings and 8824 tons of slimes were used
up. There still remained £68,000 cash in hand, which is
practically the whole of the profit made out of the
oxidised ores in the mine. Chairman M'Donald told the
shareholders at the annual meeting that they could get
“ some idea of the immensity of our lode ’ by reflecting
that between 216 ft. (where the sulphides start)
and the 316 ft. level “we are likely to have opened
up 176,000 tons of payable ore, or, say, over half
the total tonnage crushed with the 100-stamp null
since commencing operations. (Hear, hear.)” The fre-
quent occurrence of “ hear, hear,” in the reports of these
speeches of chairmen at London mining meetings is very
touching. The gentleman who is alleged to have said
“ Hear, hear,” might much more sensibly have put a
rule-of-three sum to the directors ; “ If you can’t pay any
dividends at all out of 350,000 tons of ore, how much can
vou pay out of half that much ore?” The sooner the
property gets into the hands of “the larger concern
mysteriously mentioned at the meeting the better both
for the shareholders and for Cobar. The ore could be
comfortably handled in connection with the Great Cobar ;
but, worked alone, there isn’t a process in view which will
give good results on account of the siliceous character of
the stuff. As repeatedly said here, the only way to
economically work these Cobar mines is to work them
together ; and Chesney has gone a long way to prove that
already.
V * * *
J. E. Carne, tlie most experienced man on the
field staff of N.S.W. Mines Department, comes in late with
some remarks on Coppabella. Though most of what he
says has been said already, he supplies certain new figures
on which soipe conclusions may be formed. He makes
the width of the Proprietary lode 10ft. 6in., of which
about a third contains most of the galena ; and the
average value of the lode right through is, he says, 9 2 per
cent, lead and 4joz. silver. Taking these figures, we can
work it out that there is £ll9s. 6d. worth of metal in the
ore at the present very high values of metals. Allow
15s. per ton for mining and 4s. 6d. per ton for
milling—both low figures—and £1 is left over to pay
freight, realisation, and returning charges, management,
and development. Seeing that, it is good concentrating
material, the bulk could possibly be reduced to seven
tons of ore to one ton of concentrates, and that would
make a big saving in freight and returning charges. But
even if reduced seven to one, the concentrates, supposing
they saved all the metal, would only contain £7 worth of
metal after paying mining and milling charges; and
freight and returning charges (smelting, &c.) would
absorb the whole of that. If this is the value of
the ore in bulk, it is impossible to see any profit in it.
Compare that value, by the way (£1 19s. 6d. per ton),
with the £ll 11s. sd. per ton gross realised by the first
10-ton lot sent from the mine. Some people might
almost believe that that stuff was carefully picked and
sent to smelters to help the §hare market along a bit.
Something in a small way may perhaps be done
with Coppabella Prop, by following the richer portion
only of the lode ; this, according to assistant-geologist
Carne, runs about per cent, lead and Boz. silver, and
is, say, 3ft. 6in. wide. At present prices that ore is worth
gross about £5 9s. per ton. Probably it could be reduced
in bulk to about one in three, and after paying all ex-
penses (£1 2s. 6d. mining and dressing, £2 6s. Bd. cartage
and smelting, &c. —that is, £7 per ton of concentrates—
and £1 to cover losses and to pay management and
other charges) might give a profit of £1 per ton. But
present market values are unusually high, and unless
better silver contents can be got (they are at present
exceedingly low), the outlook is extremely gloomy for any-
thing in the way of continuous work.

Perseverance, Iv'ilgoorl io, has estimated its ore
reserves (Sept. 30) at 287,000 tons, worth lOJdwt. This
means 20 months’ supply at the recent rate of treatment.
This is not very gaudy ; but, then, on December 31st
last, the reserves were estimated at 110,700 tons only, and
since then about 130,000 tons have been treated, so that
just over 300,000 tons of ore has been developed meantime,
most of it since Klug took charge. Costs for October
panned out at 365. sd. as against 435. 9d. last year.
Manager Klug expects, to reduce costs still more after
alterations to plant are completed. Valuing ore reserves
at 425., there is a profit of 6s. per ton, or say £76,100, in
sight. In addition to reserves stated, there are also
176,000 tons worth 6dwt., or say 245. per ton gross.
But these latter are worthless by present mode of treat-
ment. The fringe of the lode found at 1245 ft., near the
boundary, by the Associated, has been cut in the cross-
cut from 1100 ft. Discovery of this lode should alter the
future of the mine.
* * • *
Hainault, Kalgoorlie, is in a strong position, as
co. has 205,000 tons of SJdwt. ore in sight, practically four
years’ reserves, and, at present costs, there is Sin apparent
profit of 9s. per ton, or, say, £90,000. Mine is being very
economically managed by Frank Moss, and, although a
low-grade proposition for W.A., is being very successfully
worked. The deepest level is only 750 ft. ; but there is no
great need to push ahead, as mine has a bigger ore reserve
in proportion to treatment plant than any mine on the
fields, bar Vivien, which has merely a 10-head mill.
Karridale, Burtville (W.A.), has just cleaned up
another 156 tons for 11200 z., bringing up the total from
the mine to 1656 tons for 4,9200 z. The Kevenue,
Meekathara, has treated 3icwt. for 8690 z. The total from
this mine is 15 tons scwt. for 22590 z. But in both districts
the reefs are too small for big co.’s to tackle.

Westralia Mt. Morgans has closed, and will be
closed for three or four months at least.- Bad water is
now given as the excuse, but the excuse is worse than
the water. Development was utterly neglected, and
what manager Bob Nicholson, of Ivanhoe, predicted on
his visit has come to pass. . At the end of June Nicholson
estimated that there were 34,800 tons of Bidwt. ore in
sight, and even that moderate estimate proves to have
been exaggerated. The final crushing panned out 3800
tons for 10840 z., or 245. 2d. per ton. This, according to
A. E. Morgans, leaves a profit ,of 4d. per ton, or £6O.
Last year’s costs panned out at about 405., when the full
60-head mill was running full time ; and it is practically
certain that 235. lOd. cannot cover present costs. Some
350 men have been put off, and practically the town of
Morgans is dead.
*■ * * *
As reported on November 30, Lancefield,
Laverton (W. A.), has closed down, the arsenical
ore at 300 ft. not being amenable to ordinary treat-
ment. During October 6080 tons yielded 16s. sd.
per ton. Costs were 225. 5d., leaving a loss of 65., or
£lB2O. Mine will be closed down for some months till a
roasting plant is installed. This will mean a big expen-
diture in new plant. 8., M. and Co. reckon the mine can
be placed on a payable basis, but it seems somewhat
doubtful, as, in addition to the ore being low grade,
timber in the neighborhood is very scarce.
The surface and underground staff of Golden
Pole (VV.A.) has been changed, and D. Nicholson, brother
of Bob of the Ivanhoe, is now in charge. As mine is a
ticklish one to work, such a complete sweep is risky.
.** * *
Pin Gin (W.A.) is a district well known 10 years
ago, but deserted till lately. In the old days a 5-head
mill was erected, but was dismantled before doing any
work. As people’s ideas have since been modified, the
ground has all been re-pegged and a lot of prospecting is
being done. Government has erected a 10-head battery,
and results so far have been satisfactory. The best return
has come from the Anglo-Saxon, which yielded 3190 z.,
from 50 tons. But as prospectors are limited to 50-ton
parcels till all have had a turn, it is safe to presume that
the ore was picked.
Proposed by Westralian Government to lay 30
miles of tramway from Bavensthorpe copper fields to
Hopetoun on the coast, and as a State smelter is at work
on ore purchased by the State, the project is likely to
materialise. The new Assembly is strong on the develop-
ment of the mining industry ; the Estimates for this year
will disclose a big increase in Mines Department expendi-
ture. But a strong feeling prevails that much money is
being wasted. Half-a-dozen worn-out fields are buttered
up at enormous expense ; each has a warden (at £7OO or
so a year), mining registrar, mines inspector, and other
highly-salaried officials. Fifty thousand pounds saved
this way and spent on the encouragement of prospecting
would give the industry a much-needed lift.
* * * *
Bendigo Stock Exchange the other day, being
in contemplative mood, began to see that the centre of
the hub was not in the Golden Square. Therefore, it
agreed to ask the managers of important mines to report
developments at Melbourne and Bendigo simultaneously.
This burst of generosity lasted exactly 48 hours. Then
arose a wail of “ Bendigo for the Bendigo Stock
Exchange ” —a cry as poignant as any lamenta-
tion ever uttered by King Solomon. As the
cry of the timid increased in volume, the courage
of those who saw that Bendigo would benefit if it threw
parochialism on one side frizzled up, and so the Stock
Exchange rescinded the resolution it had passed
a few days earlier. It was a painfully retrograde
action —an action as much against the best in-
terests of Bendigo as would be the return to State
parochialism threatened by Barren joey Carruthers*
The Stock Exchange of Bendigo now simply claims
early information, which may enable its members to
get ahead of the public in distant parts. Yet shareholders
are entitled to equality of news whether they live in Mel-
bourne, Sydney, or Timbuctoo. It is a poor sort of
spirit that would begrudge this, even if is it not a right
established by law. Yet the members of the Bendigo
Stock Exchange have stamped themselves as being
unwilling to share with the Melbourne Stock Exchange
(and through it the public) prompt details of impor-
tant news from mines. The reason may be to keep the
business arising from Bendigo mines in Bendigo. To do
this is impossible if shares can be purchased by outsiders
who prefer to deal in Melbourne or Sydney or Adelaide.
And as the members of the Bendigo Stock Exchange are
well aware of that fact, they have now to show that t hey
can escape the other horn of the dilemma —viz., that they
want early news, so that they may get in ahead of the
other fellow.
* * *
Cathcart Proprietary, Ararat (Vic.), is an
alluvial mine with an electric plant and a fair field of
wash; but its board has still to pad the hoof if it wishes
the co. to enter the select realm of dividend payers. A
great opportunity occurred a few weeks back to put
Spring Hill Leads'Browne in charge. But while he waited
in the ante-chamber the local people stirred up in
the inner chamber. So Browne drifted off to find a
haven at the Great Northern Extended Consols, Ruther-
glen, which show needed fresh blood as much as the
Cathcart board does. Now the Cathcart manager has
gone, and, having missed Browne, the directors have to
scratch about for an alluvial man at a time when good
alluvial miners are not plentiful. The mine itself needs a
lot of handling. The rim of wash is narrow as far as
proved, water is heavy, and there isn’t the gold there
that ardent Araratites would believe.
* ♦ • *
Long Tunnel, Walhalla (Vic.), demands prompt
attention from shareholders if they wish to avert a double
slump in share values and in output. Co. was reorganised
to work the north-west ground, and Grayson’s incline
shaft was sunk to enable that ground to be reached. But
the extension of the shoot of stone from the Extended
mine led the directors to go south. Luck waited on
them, and they cut stone which proved to be suffi-
ciently profitable to stop calls, and to put some
funds in hand. But all progressive work was stopped
to root out this end of the show. Most folk see
the end of the stone within sight, but the Melbourne
directors apparently have a tin film before their eyes, for
they fail to appreciate the gravity of the situation, lhe
pumps ought to be up, the shaft ought to be going down,
and the battery ought to be undergoing re-modelling.
But since the spasm of energy which led to the sinking of
Grayson’s shaft passed, the board has been content to
draw fees and to find an excuse for not publishing the
amalgam yields. As things go, there is little more chance
of divs. than there is of the directors risking their necks
on the Walhalla road in monthly visits to the mine.
• * * *
Another of the Age mining fledglings has made
a feeble flutter. This is a forty or fifty-year old thing
near Kyneton, about which there was a hoary legend that
the old owners left ounce stone at the bottom before they
were beaten out by the water. This was the sort of yarn
that fired the imagination over the New Yorkshire, at
Tarnagulla, and it is not odd that the result
should have been the same in both cases, the
truth being that the old generation of miners
knew quite as well as the moderns how to make ounce
dirt pay. Eliza Hill’s first yield was about 3dwt. per ton.
Now.some underhand stoping has given dirt worth 7dwt.
This is a fair rate of progression, if it could only be main-
tained. Underhand stoping, however, is expensive, and
it looks as though the co. must sink the shaft further and
make calls to pay costs. The Syme crowd can afford to
lose its money, but some of the strong backers of the
mine are less wealthy, and quite as deserving.
* * * *
The tribute question has cropped up again at
Ballarat over the Sebastopol Plateau No. 1. The show
has paid £9600 in divs. at a cost of £70,000 in calls to
shareholders. Western district folk have been good
backers of the mine of late years, but even they, like
Lansell, have sickened of Ballarat. So it comes to this
that if work is to be resumed, the Sebastopol Proprietary,
which owns the lease, must forego rent and cut down the
absurd 5 per cent, royalty, or have the mine thrown on
its hands. This latter would be the best thing that could
happen, for then the owners would have to work or
forfeit. _ «
* * *
The evidence given by miners before the Vic*
Coal Mines Commission makes interesting reading.
Witnesses employed before and after the lockout which
broke up the union, state that before being given employ-
ment under existing conditions they had to sign an under-
taking that they would not join a union. Further, they
said, the manager of one mine told them that,
unless they bought a house and land from the co.,
they would not be given a job. One man swore
that a petition presented to Parliament bearing
his alleged signature was not signed by him at all.
The strangest feature of all is the way weights have fallen
off.' Under union conditions, the men had a weigher of
their own to check the weights of each skip of coal as it
left the mine, and in those days skips went from lOcwt.
to 12cwt. each. Since the union was beaten check-
weighers have been abolished, and the men have .trust
to the honor of the co. for the correctness of the weights,
which have mysteriously declined by 4cwt. a skip.
# * * *
Sydney inventor Frank Cotton s latest experi-
ments with his liquid-fuel furnace have been on low-grade
nickel ore from New Caledonia; and some ingots of metal,
now shown at Sydney Exchange, prove that the process
does the work. And if it can smelt nickel ore, it can
smelt just about anything. No direct nickel-smelting
process has hitherto been known—except, perhaps, an
electrical process, and that is too costly to be used in
a commercial way. The New Caledonian ore is exported
either to Freiberg or France, where it passes through five
processes—practically a series of reactions, all expensive,
and all involving more or less loss of metal. One result is
that, though what is known as “commercial nickel
(about 70 per cent, nickel) is worth about Is. 7d. per lb.,
or £l7B per ton, there is no profit in ore under 7 per cent.,
and none below that grade is exported from the mines.
The Australian inventor, who claims to do the trick m
one act, has been working on refractory 5 per cent, stuff,
and the results of the experiments have been closely
watched by agents of New Caledonian mine-owners,
who have hundreds of thousands of tons of this class of
ore, for which hitherto no market has been open.

Discovery of nitrogenous rock at Paratoo
(S.A.), about 200 miles from Adelaide, on the Broken Hill
line, should ■ mean profits for Somebody, and incidentally
set up another Australian industry, if the thing turns out
as good as first reports represent it. Nitrogen is one of
the most expensive of commercial fertilisers ; and the
manures converted from the nitrogenous rock of Chili
have been filling the pockets of U.S. “Nitrate Kings
for a good while now. Scene of the Paratoo find is a rock
five chains in circumference, rising 60ft. above the plain.
Samples sent to Adelaide revealed ammonia, nitrates of
phosphoric acid, and potash. Finders, Thos. Miller and
R. J. Fawcett, have twelve 40-acre blocks.

Aboriginalities.
“ Wurrym”: Sa-ay, don’t be too hard on
Westralia. 8., 9th Nov., says we can’t grow
enough vegetables for our own use. In Perth and
Fremantle, at any rate, vegetables (barring as-
paragus and celery) are cheaper than and equal to
those I’ve met in Victoria, the cabbage State. Of
course, the outback prospector and distant mining
camps go short of vegetables, but any decent-sized
town has its vegetable supply. Fruit of some
sorts will soon be a drug, and the apple export
trade will start. Grapes—well, I like Roma Black
Mamburgs better, but I’ve never seen finer
muscats, or Princes, or Wortley Halls than one
gets for 3d. and 4d. a lb. in season, while the
Darling Range strawberry is getting a name for
size and flavor, and is sold at fid. to Is. a box in
season.
“ S.S.” : “ Cat ” M‘Cart is the king of a
thousand cats. He is a hatter living on a small
selection at the foot of Ham’s Head, near
Willynilly, Gippsland. M ‘Cart’s hut is a chimney-
less, primitive structure of stringy bark, and his
clearing is just about large enough for himself and
four goats. The thousand cats live anywhere, but
M‘Cart’s hut is their head centre, and they
frequently gather there to jubilate, on which
occasion Pandemonium and a Chinese opera are
peace compared with the vicinity of the home of
“Cat” M‘Cart. M'Cart is an enthusiastic
collector of cats, and while other selectors on and
about the Ram’s Head are organising crusades
against cats, Mac encourages them to increase
and multiply. About a year ago M‘Cart fell ill in
his lonely hut, and before it occurred to anybody to
look him up, the desolate hatter had had a night
and day of picturesque horror such as few men
have enjoyed this side Gehenna. About ten of
his cats were shut up in the hut with him, and
when Mac realised that it would be well to
liberate them he was either too weak or too
frightened to move. His half-wild tabbies had
taken to perching on his bunk and glaring at him
with awful, famished eyes as if he were meat, and
he understood quite well that when he became un-
able to help himself the cats would help themselves.
A fight ensued. Every time Mac brought his head
from under the rug the cats attacked, and twice he
was awakened by feeling their teeth in him. After
that he didn t dare to go to sleep. It was an
emaciated collector of cats that the adjacent
selectors discovered in Mac’s hut when they
called to inquire, and he was in hospital for some
time after. When he returned home he collected
his cats again.
* * * *
“David Richards” : The evil policy of
leasing stock routes is making its consequences
disastrously felt in the out-back of N.S.W. at
present. Whilst all along the route, on each side,
private land (C.P., C.L., station, or other) is yet
well grassed, the routes are practically bare of
anything suggestive of the vegetable kingdom,
except trees ; and, in the ease of buddlia, white-
wood, rosewood, beefwood, myall, belar, and other
edible sorts, even they have been either felled or
lopped within recent times. The consequence is
that hungry hordes of jumbucks and cattle con-
tinually gaze at the arid breast of Mother Earth,
and moan with the emptiness of their Little
Marys, while the bloated individual who has got
good grass and plenty of sheep in these hard
times, and can atford to pay £llOOO to land agents,
smiles up his sleeve, and chuckles at the thought
of how lie has robbed the stock routes. Where is
the sense in setting apart a tract of land as a stock
route, and then letting station sheep eat it as bare
as a gallows ? Ask of the land agents, ye hunger-
stricken animals 1

Queensland’s prickly pear is now esti-
ma ed to have full charge of 4,000,000 acres of
territory. An expert named Tryon has been in-
structed to seek some parasite that will consume
this 6250 solid square miles of pear, but on
appearances he might as well .look for a fungus
that would eat up 6250 square miles of crowbars.
If all the Submerged Tenth in England surrounded
the pear and walloped it with axes, it looks
healthy enough to survive. The rabbit, the pear
and Cohen hold a joint mortgage on the land part
of Queensland, and the water hyacinth promises
to monopolise its streams. These pests will be
there when Tozer is dead and forgotten.
* * * *
“Republican ” : I don’t remember having
seen the following true Palmer River yarn in
print as told me by “ Truthful ” :
I was just back from Cooktown, dead stiff, at the time
when Chows were dying- like sheep on the Palmer, and
the Government was giving a fiver a corpse to anyone
who found and buried one. I sees two Chows sinking a
paddock on the terrace below me camp, one bailing, the
other digging. I goes up to the warden and says, “ Mr.
St. George, there’s two of those yellow coves dead down
there in the crick.” “ Indeed, James,” says he. “Ah,
will you see that they’re buried ?” he says. An’ I goes
down an’ flattens ’em both out with my long-handled
shovel, an’ throws a few shovels of muck on top of ’em,
an’ comes back and gets me ten lovely quid.
THE BODIES, METHOD OF RABBIT EXTERMINATION.
The grassed side of the fence is Mr. Rodier's run where his method has been applied. The
other side represents the normal state of the country.
On one of the stations far west of Towns-
ville (Q.), close to the Edge, the manager about a
week ago found hundreds of snakes dead around a
dry water-hole. A tract where snakes cannot exist
must be even worse than Gehenna, where the
Worm that Dieth Not was looking splendid the
last time he was heard of.

The Swiss village of Zofingen, in the
Canton of Aargau, was lately decorated with flags
in honor of a hen which had laid its thousandth
egg. Five adjacent villages sent deputations,
accompanied by bands, which serenaded the
industrious hen.

Bent’s State is so cursed with socialistic
legislation that even the rabbits are leaving the
land and taking to loafing round Melbourne
Crowds of unemployed rabbits can be seen any
morning or evening in the Eitzroy Gardens and in
vacant patches around the suburbs.
LAST RESORT OF THE IMPECUNIOUS.
HUSBAND : “ Not a bite of bread in the house; not a scrap
of meat; the rent a month overdue, and the grocer threatening to summons! What
ever shall we do?"
WIFE (decisively): “ We'll have to take in boarders."
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : A very interesting
and instructive section of nature-study for country
State school teachers to take up in Australia
would be the propagation and careful cultivation
of native fruits and native flowers. I often
wonder how much improvement intelligent culture
would effect in certain of our native fruits, such as
the quandong, the geebung, the Australian cherry,
the brown or she-pine berry, the black or brush
apple, &c., &c. Probably many Australian
flowers and fruits could be improved almost out
of recognition. Anyhow, it’s time we devoted
greater attention to the study of Australian flora
and fauna. In many Australian schools illustra-
tions of native trees, flowers, fruits, and animals
are conspicuous only by their absence, which is
numerous.
* * * *
“D.S.M.”: Waiting, t’other night, at a
dam for a shot at kangaroos, I saw by the bright
moonlight a cat slip gently into the water and
make for some ducks that were floating a little
distance out. Presently the ducks flew away,
except one that was left struggling with the cat.
In about a minute the cat started back to the
bank with the bird in tow. I shot it as it was
polishing its victim off, and made a sufficiently
careful inspection to be able to guarantee that it
was plain twopenny cat, and a very dead wet cat
at that.
“E.H.H.”: The love of a uniform does
not stop at reviews and social functions. Two
cookies, neighbors of mine, whose sons once
belonged to the Bushrangers’ Riflemen, wear the
left-off uniforms to the creamery. Another, whose
brother is on the railways, comes decked out as a
porter, and an ex-Salvationist still sticks to his
guernsey in the cow-yard. If I had a- brother
who was an aide-de-camp, I would wear his left-
off feathered hat while driving the harvester, but
I haven’t any elevated relatives.
“ Ses ” : I once embarked in the fish in-
dustry. It was at Texas, on the Severn River,
while spelling horses. This Darling feeder teems
with black bream and Murray cod, and they bite
so well that we could have supplied Sydney and
Melbourne with fish had facilities for exportation
permitted. The Texas housewife, if short of meat
for dinner, has only to say to the old man, “Just
trot down the bank and get me six bream and a
cod for supper, while I get the pan ready. 1 ' The
old man goes down with his rod and hand line,
and in 10 minutes returns with the required
number. Texas is a great tobacco-growing dis-
trict, though the industry is mostly in the hands
of Chinese. We regretted this, as the Mongolians
could not be induced to barter with us when we
wanted vegetables. Among the few whites out-
side the town we did better. We exchanged fish
for tobacco at the factory, and for beef at the
station. What we could not dispose of imme-
diately we salted, and stacked under a tree
for the mailman, who, the Texas people told
us, bought all he could get at 3d. a pound,
and took a load every week to Stanthorpe and
Goondiwindi. The lady behind the grocery coun-
ter also informed us that we had a monopoly of
the business, as the local anglers were up to their
eyes in tobacco harvesting just then. We were
pleased, and bought a lot of groceries. Pleasure
now became business, and each day we carried to
camp as much fish on a long pole as the two of us
could stagger under. Even then we had once or
twice to tether a cod in the river till morning. It
was hot weather, and the cod, being exceptionally
fat, were hard to cure ; but by the end of the
week we had a great square, stack as high as our
tent. Then we interviewed tfie mailman at the
local pub. “No, he wasn’t taking any more.
The market had dropped ; and, besides, they went
bad on the road this hot weather.” Next morning
we left Texas very early. We also left the fish.

“ Roma ” : South Gippsland (Vic.) is un
doubtedly the home of the tallest snake yarns at
large. There grow luxuriantly fairy tales of tiger-
snakes “ follerm’ a man ’alf-way up a ladder, and
then goin’ back and waiting for ’im at the foot.”
But the champion curdler thisyearisaboutawoman
who, one autumn, was terribly frightened at dis-
covering a tiger-snake coiled at her feet, and who
was only rescued by the skin of her teeth. Then,
during a padded silence, the narrator goes on to
describe how, every autumn since, the woman
sheds her skin, just like a snake, and is quite
unsafe to live with for a week or more.

“F.H.R.”: The “fust” set had just
finished pretty roughly, owing to two of the lads
(who couldn’t get partners) “bucking” into the
last figure—the stockyards. We were standing in
groups outside the door, wiping the perspiration
from our arms and heads with various kinds of
handkerchief, when the M.C. came out. “Look
’ere, chaps,” he whispered ; “some of thewimmen
are goin’ crook. One’s goin’ ’ome a’cos it’s too
flamin’ rough. Don’t take any notice er me—l’m
goin’ ter rouse.” Then he went inside and yelled
to all concerned : “Look ’ere, yous blokes; this
ere’s a darnce an’ not a cattle-vard. If yer don’t
like ’ter darnce quiet, yer’d better dashed well
go ’ome.” After that things fairly ’ummed.
PRIVILEGES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He had been told that he was not a supporter of the
working class, but nothing gave him greater pleasure
than to see the working man at work. (Laughter.)
Some people would like to hear him sing, but he was too
hoarse. (Laughter.) It vexed him when people who were
treated well in the State left for other States , and foixot
the place where they had so many privileges. (Cheers.)
Bhxt at Leongatha.
In far Japan an agfed man, nigh stricken unto death,
Cried to the brown girls round him with anguish in his
breath:
“ Oh bear me to the balcony that looks upon the West
That I may raise a last long gaze to the land I love the
best.”
They bore him as he bade them and they laid him on a
mat,
They gave him tea, and cigarettes, and sake where he
sat.
And as he blew the smoke-rings from his large and
practised mouth—
He sang a song of Bentland—fair Bentland in the South.
“ Girls, I always was a roamer,
A beachcomber—not a homer —
I liked my pipe, my pint, my ship, my girl, my life so
free:
And I’ve wandered through life gaily,
rm Bill Bailey—asked for daily —
And I won’t go back to Bentland far away across the
sea / ”
The agfed man took up his can and drank a mighty drink.
“ If folks could see Bill Bailey now I wonder what they’d
think?
It’s twenty years, and more, ago since first I sailed
away—
I mind it well—in the Queensland Belle bound for Algoa
Bay.”
“Oh tell us, Mister Bailey—for you’re on your dying
mat—
What made you leave your native land, your station, and
all that i ”
“ Well, listen girls—my poor brain whirls—but if lam
not wrong
It was due to too much privilege, and too much comic
song.
It’s a land of politicians—
Governments and Oppositions—
All the members sing like Tietjens, while there’s money to
be spent
On artistic roads and bridges;
And the people’s privileges
Are uncountable—but still I want to dodge the Land of
Bent.
l< If I were able to recite the privileges high
That swarm about the people I should not have time to
die,
But I may briefly hint a few that lend a special tone
To Bentland and its rulers. First, of course, there is the
Loan.
They’ve the privilege of borrowing whenever they’re
inclined,
And Bent of Bentland spends the beans exactly to his
mind ;
They’ve the privilege of paying the interest to the Jews,
And the privilege of applauding Bent and Co.’s financial
views—
Oh, I tell 3'ou they are happy—
Bent’s a great and wondrous chappie—
He is always gay and cheerful—he’s a second Sunny Jim.
He’s a perfect revelation
When he shouts before the nation—
That ‘of all the lands Australia’s the land of lands for
him !’
The other privileges that just make the place a Heaven
Are absolutely numberless. I’ll give you six or seven ;
There’s the privilege of walking if you’ve got no horse to
ride;
There’s the privilege of talking, though you may be put
outside;
There’s the privilege of applying for a farm you never
get;
There’s the privilege of possessing Sunday thirsts you
may not wet;
There’s the privilege of keeping what you never need to
give;
There’s the privilege of dying if you do not wish to
live 1
Ah, there’s no land quite so pleasant
For the millionaire or peasant;
Even good old Walter Besant would have said so, had he
know r n it 1
When the politicians stump it
You can hear ’em sound the trumpet
Of its praises so distinctly that a Gabriel might have
blown it 1
“ But I’m dying; yes, I’m dying here in Nippon, on a
mat;
And I feel I left my country like a miserable rat—
But the songs were too much for me, and the privileges
used
To annoy me—for I didn’t value them— and so I cruised
Lound the world to find a country where no privileges
were,
And I found the Yoshiwara, and I didn’t want to stir.
It’s my ill-grained, stupid nature, I suppose, but I’m
content,
And this Nippon suits me better than the Paradise of
Bent.
Girls, I always was a ranger—
And a stranger—seeking danger—
And I had no ear for music, and I didn’t like to see
All those privileges splendid
(Which in Bentland are extended
To the people) simply wasted on a thankless cuss like me.

“Elmore” on another sort of “drunks
luck :
Sandy Flat was having'a church bazaar as Jack Cross
and John Snow came in from the sawmill with a camel s
thirst and a seven months’ cheque to quench it with.
After lubricating they struck the bazaar, and started in
to buy whole hooks of tickets from the girls who smiled
on them. That bazaar was a howling success.
A week or so later Jack and John had got from the
best parlor to the wood-heap, and, with swags rolled,
were arguing which of them should tap the sour publican
for a bottle, when a young man came along- and sheepishly
remarked that he and his girl were going to get married,
and how much would Cross take for the bed. Jack care-
fully felt the log he was sitting on, and shivered lie
reckoned he was getting ’em all right. Then a brig it
bush girl hove in sight, and called Bill away. And the
set of toilet ware, if it’ll go cheap,” he said when he came
back. “ I’ve got ’em, I’ve got ’em ! gasped Jack. Oh,
no!” said the maid, “ everything is up at the hall jet,
but they want you to take them away. John gave a
shout of joy, tore the straps off his swag, and thiew
things nine different ways at once. In our good
clobber,” hejyelled ; “ the blanky bazaar tickets.
A quarter of an hour later a hand-cart pulled bj John
and Jack, and pushed behind by the prospective bride and
groom, passed a startled and disappointed publican, it
was loaded with a bed, toilet-ware, four Pictures. and
seven cushions, and it pulled up at the rival pu •>
it realised a wedding, £l6, and two separate attacks
of D.T.’s.

“Larry” : This story from the Murchison
(W A ) illustrates the self -reliant and independent
spirit' of the men of the West. Three mates
were camped for a few days. One evening a
cyclist rode along and described minutely a couple
of horses he had seen many miles back, making
down country. One of the men recognised them
as his. “I reckon I’ll go after them he re-
marked. He knew they were making for the
station where they were bred, 500 mhes away,
vet he just shouldered his swag, and slouched off
into the darkness with a casual s long. Ihe
others smoked in silence for some tune, then one
said “He might have arst fer a horse. Dunno
—w’d vou ? ” “ No.” They didn t offer a horse,
for thev reckoned he was able to look after his
own affairs, yet they would willingly have given
him one, although possibly he might have fallen
a few points in their esteem had he arst lor it.

“ Werrimee ” : Every year
resolutions are made in out-camps
and huts to keep on the strict
Q T ” for 12 months, and then go
down and seethe Melbourne Cup,
the Mecca of the bushman. Ihe
“resolutions” are often well
carried out till the long ride down
begins, and the wayside pubs start
a-callin’. They are hard places
to negotiate. One man tells of
two abortive trips. The first
time he got as far as Browns
pub, ten miles from his starting
point, and went back a week
later with a sore head. The next
time he was fitted out with a pair
of good horses, and was resolved
to camp out every night and
give all the pubs a wide berth.
But his hack lost a shoe when
nearing the first town and as it
was tenderfooted he had to make
a call at the blacksmith s. While
the latter was putting the shoe
on, an old chum strolled m and
insisted on his going to the pub
to have just one—which wouldn t
hurt him. He had it, and
forgot all about the Cup. -two
weeks afterwards he carried his
swag back to the station. An-
other man, before starting the
first time, sent his money down
to the bank. He pulled up at a
pub to have “just one nip, and
there being convivial company
present he had several ; and thereafter the pub-
lican supplied him with blank cheques hll the
limit of his account was reached. On the^nex
occasion he left his money at the station
with instructions to the boss to send it along
when he wrote for it. He wrote for it three
days after, and the only races he saw were run by
blue snakes and green goannas throughthemuiga
He made a third attempt, and again left; humonej
with the boss, with strict injunctions not to send
it* \to him no matter how he asked, until lie
received a’letter direct from Melbourne e
publican had a friend named Jones m Melbourne.
The letter was sent enclosed to Jones, Posted
it back to the station manager, pum when J ones
received the letter containing the cheque he re
turned it to the publican, and—there you are .

LAND PEST.
LiSMORE.-Another hare has been seen on Boorie Creek,
it being the second seen here within the last fortnight.
Which is reminiscent of the panic-stricken citizen
in plague time who reported that he nearly saw a
rat.
“O’F.”: One sees an occasional quaint
name on the title-page of the dusty old theologi-
cal volume that nobody seems inclined to buy.
Noticed in a secondhand book shop the other day
the works of “ the Right Reverend Father in God,
Offspring Blackall, D.D., late Lord Bishop of
Exeter.” Then there is the Rev. Increase
Mather’s “ Remarkable Providences.” How is it
that no mother in Australia now names her child
Offspring, or Increase, or Birthrate ? Instead, she
confers on him some name like Montressor
Vyvyanne, which the other boys soon change to
Skinny or Blinker.
“ Itan ” from Queensland :
The Bananaland Labor party is a disrupted institution.
The Socialism-in-our-Time members—six all told—are the
bitterest foes the Parliamentary Labor party now has.
The whirling- Joe Lesina—mostly an Ishmaelite who is
against everybody and doesn’t mind in the least if every-
body is against him—leads this confused company in no
definite direction, but in a very angry spirit. At next
election at least 15 Labor members who refused to sign
the Convention platform will be opposed by the Trades
Hall, and the fight will be a memorable one, as most
family feuds are. The Morganites and Kidston’s followers
have a good chance all the same of getting back as
numerically strong as they are now, if not stronger.
Their chief plank will be that Queensland must
be honest and pav its way. During the past few weeks
the Socialism-in-our-Time party has been advocating a
renewal of the borrowing policy, in which respect it joins
hands with Philp and Leahy. Therefore, the election
seems likely to be a scrap between Honesty-ln-our-Time
and Socialism-in-our-Time with the remnant of the Philp
party supplying a side show and calling for the Biggest
Possible Drunk in Our Time.
Front Brisbane :
Chief Protector of Q. Aborigines, Both, has flat
tened out his detractors. A few weeks ago a number of
members vigorously attacked the Protector and hurled
serious charges at him in large quantities. The Protector s
categorical reply is refreshing. As for a petition pre-
sented by Hargreaves, M.L.A., Both narrows down the
affair to the point that “the onus of proving that the
member was innocent of substituting: one petition for
another—the one hawked and the other presented to the
House—rests with himself.” Hargreaves read extracts
from a letter purporting to have been written by
“B. J. Bennett,” but inquiry has failed to prove
that there is such a man. So the horrible charges
contained in that document have fallen flat. Inves-
tigations by the Under-Secretary for Lands prove
that the meetings held in the North were mostly en-
gineered by one large firm of boss storekeepers, who had
an axe to grind on behalf of the station managers, hit
hard by the Protector’s interference with their free em-
ployment of the blacks. “It was all because of my
interference with a vested interest in flesh and blood,
says the doctor in defence. Joe Lesina’s attack, to the
effect that Both sold to the Sydney Museum 3000 curios
which were State property, looked the blackest case of
the lot, for Joe read a telegram, purporting to have been
received from the Curator, saying that the sale was
effected. Both replied by reading a letter from the
Museum trustees declaring that there had been no such
sale.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on 3IS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned, unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
