The central obstruction came from the bunch of members who have come to
be known as “Reid’s squealing dozen.” —AGE.
Dr. James Mitchell, father of “Library”
Mitchell, owed most of his wealth to coal lands
which he acquired at Stockton, near Newcastle.
He lived many years in that rocky part of Sydney
known as Cumberland-street, and the present
Mitchell occupied the house for a time after the
old man’s death.
At 68 years of age, mining speculator
Dowel passed out at Herberton (Q.) last week.
In the eighties, Wm. Springthorpe Dowel repre-
sented Tamworth (or New England) in N.S.
Wales Assembly, and did so for three Parlia-
ments. Then alterations of boundaries, single
seats, and reduction in numbers crowded him out.
The Rodfishers’ Association of N.S.W., a
body responsible for a large number of the current
perjuries about “fishes of that size,” has elected
Justice O’Connor and State Judge Fitzhardinge
to top positions in the committee of management.
But that is not enough ; affidavits must be attached
to the reports, and the fish must be sworn to
before a J.P.
Edward VII. recently paid a visit to Edin-
burgh, of which a Scotch paper thus speaks :
_ Although the Sovereign was only six hours in his Scot-
tish capital, he succeeded during that short time in ap-
pearing in three different suits, with all the accompanying
paraphernalia of royalty.
Perhaps he will go down to history as Edward the
Dresser.
“G. 8.” : Can’t understand the boosting of
Victoria’s Swinburne as a Democrat. Except the
Water Bill, Swinburne hasn’t assisted to give Vic.
anything valuable in legislation; in fact, he has
voted against almost every progressive measure
brought forward. Swinburne may look a Demo-
crat alongside Bent, but that is too low a standard
to test a politician by.

L)ied at 70, George Marchant,
"b took Australian merinos to California m 1860,
anfl made , tbe terribly adventurous journey
across the plains to New York. The old gen-

Jerry Simpson, of Kansas, is dead. When
G. R. Dibbs visited the U.S. A, he behaved so
unconventionally that the Yanks christened him
the Jerry Simpson of Australia. The nickname
was a fit, for Kansas Jerry wore no socks and
comported himself in the legislative halls as a
man who wanted to live up to that eccentricity ;
but the term clung for a while. Any allusion to
it used to rouse Dibbs to boiling point; but just
then George Republican D. had repudiated his
Republicanism and become a K.C.M.G “to
oblige a lady,” and was trying to look as little like
Simpson of Kansas as he knew how. Jerry, the
Congressman, died of heart disease.
A staggering story of
hunger in the medical man
comes from a Southern
city. A wealthy patient
was attended by Dr. Blank
for about two or three
months ere Death con-
cluded the “case”, and in
consideration, seemingly,
of the fact that his language
to th e doctor had been of ten
disrespectful, the patient
bequeathed him a big
sum. The legacy merely
whetted Blank’s appetite
for the dead man’sguineas.
He sent in a huge claim
for medical attendance on
his departed benefactor,
and the executors, pro-
testing against the charge,
finally appeased the appli-
cant with a cheque for
four figures.
“Elijah” Dowie, it
seems, had a very unsavory
character at Melbourne
Gaol, some 20 years ago,
when he was several times
committed for obstructing the footpath or refusing
to pay his debts. The old humbug— not then so
hairy as now—-was an aggressive apostle, and the
prison authorities held him in particular contempt.
Zimri Sellar, one of the Labor members
for Adelaide, was an apprentice in a provision
store in London sixty odd years ago, and served
groceries to the Duke of Wellington, though he
doesn’t mention whether Wellington called for
his pound of cheese personally or by proxy.
Sellar is continually giving the Assembly an
interesting little scrap of his autobiography.
Captain James Banks, buried at Waverley
(Sydney) last week, had sailed most of the world’s
seas. He and Clarke Russell, the novelist, served
their apprenticeship together on a vessel called La
Hogue. Banks changed from the Dunbar to the
Duncan Dunbar the day before the former started
on the voyage that ended tragically at The Gap,
Sydney Heads.
A son of the late Graham Berry now
figures in the advt. of a patent medicine as “ Son
of Great Statesman Cured by Blank’s Long Pills
for Short People,” or something to that effect.
This should be an encouragement to all patriotic
Australians to serve their country in a public
capacity, and to rear up a stalwart race in order
that they may, by-and-bye, be cured by somebody’s
electric belt, and keep green the old man’s memory
in the printed testimonial.
“S.S.” : Have seen Willie Kelly, of the
motor, in Parliament, and I want to know what
his electorate sees in him, and how it comes to see
it. Willie, during the time I sat and watched
him, behaved like a simpering, giggling johnnie-
boy, and talked with the loathly “ Bwitish ” affec-
tation which alone should induce an Australian
electorate to chase a candidate over the horizon.
I don t know what electorate Wearisome Willie
sits for, but I trust with all my heart that it is
not as bad as he represents it. If I were an elec-
torate, and Wearisome Willie represented me in
that way, I’d sue him for libel.
The man who caused Adelaide gaol to be
known for over 20 years as “ Howell’s Hotel ” has
just passed out, at the age of 73. John Howell
was a coastal trader between Sydney and New-
castle about 1854, but left the sea on being ship-
wrecked for the second time (the first time was in
the Bay of Biscay). Then he found his real voca-
tion in life as a gaoler, and gave up his spare time
to the writing of quite decent verses.
The will of Mars Buckley, of the half-cen-
tury-old Melbourne firm of Buckley and Nunn,
drapers, has been proved at £450,000. The amount
has been left to the sons and daughters of the
testator, with provision for an annuity and the
use of the Buckley mansion and furniture for the
widow. Mars Buckley’s pile did not all come
from chiffons and shop-walking. A big deal in
Bourke-street land during the sixties helped
materially.
A sight in Melbourne of late is provided
by a sleek, smug-looking Papuan native missionary,
dressed largely in his original skin, parading at
the heels of a church lady, who trots him round
for inspection, and evidently derives much grati-
fication from her ownership of the saintly coon.
Why the missionary has been brought from New
Guinea is not explained, but possibly his mission
is to teach political economy to the Women’s
National League.
William Knox, President of Vic. Chamber
of Commerce, and M.H.R. for Kooyong, on the
Union Label: “Mr Chairman, I—e1 —er —beg to
protest. I—er—for one, object. I—er—wish to

else. He is constant in protesting. Probably
he will be found protesting when the last trumpet
sounds.
“S.T.S.”: The curious record of S.A.
Bench —only three Judges, and those, for just on
20-years till 1904, the same three—has been com-
memorated in a portrait by George Webb. Ade-
laide’s better-known painters run to landscape
mostly, and Webb is the best local portrait
painter, with the possible exception of the Ham-
bridge sisters, who mostly do miniatures. He
shows the three Judges on the Bench, and has
caught the cold white light-from-above effect of
the Supreme Court well. Bundey’s is a splendid
likeness, Boucaut good, Chief Justice Way only
fair.
Edward Albert Stone, Chief Justice of
Westralia, retires from office early in the year.
He is the son of George Frederick Stone, a former
Attorney-General of Westralia. The Chief Jus-
tice was appointed clerk to the Attorney-General
in 1860, and was called to the Bar in 1865, when
he entered into partnership with Septimus Burt.
He was clerk to the Legislative Council in 1870-74,
and a nominee member of same 1880-82. He was
acting Attorney-General in 1879, Crown Solicitor
in 1882, and a Puisne Judge in 1884. He got the
Chief’s seaton the retirement of Alexander Camp-
bell Onslow.
A. young gentleman named Thomas, who
is vicar or.rector or something of a small township
called Guisborough, in the north of England, has
been chosen as Bishop of Adelaide. The young
parson has only had eight or ten years’ practice as
a parson, and his best billet has been in a place
not the size of any of half-a-dozen Sydney or Mel-
bourne suburbs ; and it doesn’t look as though he
had a third of the experience of scores of Aus-
tralian reverends. But he is English; and so he
gets the Australian billet. Either Australian
reverends are an abominably cantankerous lot,
who would start to break things if one of their
own number were selected for promotion, or the
bosses among them have an amazing idea of the
value of half-a-dozen years’ practice in telling the
English Hodge how to hold himself respectfully
towards his betters.

The ‘ ‘ well-paid public servant ” that Ice-
berg Irvine and Bent used to blather about, dis-
appears steadily from Victoria, chiefly because
outside concerns think him such a valuable acqui-
sition that they offer him more wages. Dr. Howell
quitted being agricultural chemist to Bent and
co. to go to the Mt. Lyell co. Viticultural expert
Burney left for a better-paid private billet. And
now W. E. C. B audinet, an assistant analyst, is
gained by the, same Mt. Lyell co. that enticed
his former chief.
Bourke, whose statue is about to be re-
moved to make room for the new Public Library
in Sydney Domain, was one of the few able men
appointed to a Governorship in Australia. He
stamped out free land grants, burst up the Batman
sham land purchases from the Port Phillip blacks,
and founded and superintended the laying out of
Melbourne. He established religious equality and
set aside the produce of land sales for the en-
couragement of immigration. At that time a
Governor possessed real power, and Bourke seems
to have maintained a high and steady purpose
amid shifty and shady surroundings.
Thomas Caradoc Kerry, who is at present
charged with the appropriation of books and
other unconsidered trifles consigned per his
favor to the islanders of Tristan d’Acunha, is
described by the London papers as an “Australian
Explorer.” Now when did Caradoc Kerry become
either an Australian or an explorer ? If Judas
Iscariot appeared in England just now, he would
probably be described by the press as “An
Australian Police Agent” or a “Melbourne
Informer,” or something of that sort. As a matter
of fact, Kerry doesn’t seem to have appropriated
anything. He was going to Tristan D’Acunha,
and a number of old tabbies of both sexes rushed
his yacht with boxes of cast-off clothing and illus-
trated Scripture tale-books, in which exhortations
to the benighted islanders were arranged in
alphabetical order on the “C. is for Cat ” plan.
This rubbish Kerry is alleged to have heaved over-
board in preference to endangering his life by
offering it to the islanders, and when the fact
leaked out the tabbies started to scratch.
An obscure politician, William Consett
Proctor, went under to cancer in Sydney, last
week. The deceased represented New England
for about a dozen years, having for colleagues at
various times Henry Copeland, James Squire
Farnell, and “ Tiger ’ Inglis. He twice made a
ripple on the surface of things. Once was in 1889
when railway-contractor Proudfoot won £5OO from
him in a Supreme Court suit. The revelations
were unpleasant and Proctor resigned, but was
re-elected. Again in 1878 an unsympathetic
jury ordered him to pay a young lady
of Armidale £GOO for breach of promise.
The reading of letters which had passed
between the parties, retailing all manner
of social secrets, set Armidale by the
ears and caused heart-burnings which it took
years to allay. Proctor's father was a bagman
travelling for a Sydney drapery firm. Prior to
that he had been a policeman in Ireland, and he
it was who, in 1848, arrested William Smith
O’Brien at Clonmel railway-station.
Just possible that some of the trouble in
N.S.W. Railway Commission arises out of the
fact that Chairman Oliver isn’t a trained railway
man, and the other two are, and are so much
hide-bound with prejudice against him that
they can’t see when he is right. Mostly,
it seems, he is right. When Parkes was
fixing up the Railway Commission, with E. M. G.
Eddy as Chairman, in 1888, he had the idea that
the State’s public service ought to be represented
on it, and so he offered Oliver, then Under-Sec.
for Lands, a Comrnissionership. Oliver declined
at first, as railways were off his beat; but when
it was represented to him that what was
wanted was an organiser more than a railway
man, he took it. Fehon was appointed at the
same time ; he had been a railway man in Eng-
land, India, and Victoria, where he was chief
traffic manager, but he had been out of the busi-
ness for some time, dropping his money on a
Western N.S.W. station. Kirkcaldie came in
years later from the staff; shortly after he was
appointed Victoria tried to get him as a Commis-
sioner, and N.S.W. put up his salary to £2OOO to
keep him. The Chairman gets £2500; the other
Commissioner £l5OO.
“ T.D.” : Am only just
beginning to find out that
Australian brush-wielders are
probably just as numerous in
London, proportionately, as
the followers of musical art.
Happened on a nest of studios
in St. John’s Wood, the
other day, and found that
Joe'Wolinski, whose notable
picture on Luke Fild< s lines
created much discussion in
Sydney five or six years back,
was working in one of them.
Next door was young Arthur
Burgess, whose paintings of
battleships, and nautical
things generally, have found
their way into the Royal
Academy. Longstaff was
temporarily in occupation of
Solomon J. Solomon’s studio
just alongside, although his
own is about two minutes’
walk away. Also B. E.
Minns has a cosy little place
a couple of stones’-throw
from Longstaff’s. The pros-
perous Jack is at work on
so large a canvas that it
necessitated a studio bigger
than his own. Wolinski has
also put in a lot of time in
painting a subject which
ought to give him promin-
ence. Minns has been doing
some work to the order of
Colonel Rhodes, the just-
dead brother of the great
S African schemer, who died
leaving so many people not
yet done, and Burgess has
been putting excellent color-
ing into his seas and skies,
and making them an offset against the dull
grey tint with which Britain smears the leviathans
of its first fighting line.
Randolph Bedford has been shaking up
the dry bones of the Queensland Marine Depart-
ment in his recent descriptive articles, and
now various Northern pressmen are foaming at
the pocket because Randolph has been paid £25
by the Morgan Government to do an article
on Queensland coastal scenery. The other
scribes avow that they could write something half
as good for half the fee paid to Randolph the
Reckless, and one man threatens to write his
article on spec, just to show what he is capable
of, and to sign it “ Adolphus Bedstead.”
TO RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The old familiar voice we hear
That rises in the city bar.
Above his glass of ginger beer
His latest stories sound afar.
They echo and reverberate
Apace along the Inky Track,
Whose travellers arise elate
To hail the news that “ Bedford’s back”.
We listen to the yarns of gold
Collected on Papua’s shore ;
We revel in the others told
Of crime and cannibals and gore.
The crowd can never get enough,
That fills each inky bivouac,
Of tales of bluster, blood, and bluff,
That circulate when Bedford’s back.
This week he talks Australia to
Some swagger Piccadilly club ;
The next he’s chasing copy new
Before some Thursday Island pub.
More points than you’ll discover by
The compass or the zodiac,
He’s covered, and we’ll gather why
Now that we know that Bedford’s back.
One day a tragedy he’ll show,
Blank-versed, with Scripture for its text,
Float half-a-dozen mines or so
And turn a novel out the next.
He lunches in some brigand’s lair,
Dines on the Pontiff’s choicest tack,
And how lie sold them each a share
We’ll ascertain now Bedford’s back.
vie.
A.B.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 5, 1905.
My Dear Arini, —
Shakos and swords and jingling spurs at the
military concert on Saturday night. Brigadier-
Deneral Gordon—not half so chestv as a lot of his
officers and privates —was there, and Mr.
Bertie 5 Kelly, who is a fine musician, con-
tributed several violin solos. Proceeds go
towards a fund to found a military library for
the garrison artillery. Wonder if they’ll buy
Ouida s and Mrs. John Strange Winter’s novels for
the sake of the military heroes who strut and
whirr and clank like crates of hardware through
the pages. One of the best items was the sword
duel between Dr. Roth and M. Lucciardi, the
Vlce-Consul for Franee. Remember that thrilling
description of swordsmanship in Marion Craw-
ford’s “ Saracinesca ” ? Really, the long, long
doctor and the agile little Frenchman fought like
these two Italians—minus tiie gore that was shed
in the romance. I believe, by the way, that for
real gore a duel scene in Dumas’ “Chicot the
Jester ” is considered to about reach the summit.
Musicians meet at the Australia Hotel Friday
afternoon to make arrangements for receiving A.
K. and Mrs. Virgil, who arrived by the Sierra’
Mr. Orlando S. Baker, U.S. Consul, will preside,
and Mr. J. A. Delaney will support him if he
feels himself sinking down at one end. The
visitors are widely known in the States as ex-
ponents of a royal road to piano-playing. Mrs V
is credited with being an accomplished pianiste,’
and knowing how to wear her hat just the right
way.
Miss May Peach—a Mosman girl of 17—with a
Voice, blue eyes, and a downpour of golden hair,
left for London and an engagement by the Orontes
last Saturday. Mr. Alfred Courtney cabled for
her after hearing a tinned song by her. That’s a
great idea, singing into a gramophone and for-
warding the record to the impresario man in
London. It suggests how far we have advanced.
If the gram’ had been invented about 27 centuries
ago, the old prophet could have prophesied into it,
and then set the instrument at the door of the
palace to denounce the wicked king till further
orders, while the prophet himself bounded like a
kangaroo till he reached a safe place at Carmel or
Lebanon. Miss May’s uprising justifies Ada
Crossley s prophecy of two years ago, that the
sweet I each had a lyric future. Impresario
Courtney is the young singer’s uncle, and as it is
only in melodrama that uncles are wicked—in real
life they are only bankrupt through helping unde-
serving nephews—her ascent in the musical scale
will no doubt be made as easy as possible.
The deplorable Dailey case, which came to a
halt m the Sydney Divorce Court last week,
didn t show Australia’s gilded youth to much
advantage. Of four young men prominently
mentioned not one seemed to have done
any useful work. They had mostly been to
Oxford where the Rhodes scholars go, to learn to
be Imperial statesmen—and then devoted them-
selves to spending money more or less creditably.
Of only one can it be said that he had shown any
desire at all to become a useful Australian.
Ihe others seemed to regard the spending of
money they hadn’t earned, a La mode of the London
Smart Set, as the highest object in life. Quite out-
side of the merits of the main case—on which
comment is inadvisable until a definite conclusion
has been reached—Ld have felt inclined, had I
been on the Bench, to have sentenced all the young
men who came forward as witnesses to work-
hard work, not necessarily in gaol, but hard work.
Of the ladies who were witnesses, or were pro-
minently mentioned, Mrs. Dailey is rather tall
and very attractive rather than handsome
or pretty. Fair hair, red-brown eyes, a refined
narrow face, a figure as slender as a growing
girls, feet that can well afford the publicity
of the ankle-length skirt, and a manner that
even to women spells fascination. She has
the repute of being a “good dresser”—one of
the rare ones who can get a good effect out of ex-
treme simplicity. Her smart handling of G. H.
Reid when he cross-examined her was one of the
sensations of the hearing. Mr. Reid was com-
pletely nonplussed. Miss Kitty Cowell is of the
charming and fluffy sort—very typical of a certain
London set, just the Gaiety notion of a fasci-
nating Girl from Kay’s ” with power to
draw a Hoggenheimer to her will. Another ladv
who figured with the pretty pet-name of “Dreary-’’
was a Coogee (Sydney) barmaid. She’s a girl
with no special beauty except in her eyes, but has
inspired quite a number of passions which, if not
grand, are at least cottage-grand size.
Out at Bondi Beach there is as much agitation
going on as to whether men and women should
bathe in the surf as there is in the self-same
bay when a southerly blows. For years a
few early morning bathing cranks had that beauti-
tul beach all to themselves. They undressed on
the rocks hard by, rushed into the deep, emerged
dripping brine, and hung themselves out to dry
on the sand without so much covering as a strand
of sea-weed. But woman came along and
troubled the waters and the male bather. The
latter had to increase his covering in consequence.
At first one lady in skimpy skirt used to drift
away to the beach centre, have her bath, and drift
home in her soaked skirt. Then two younger
ladies appeared with a piece of canvas which was
set up on the sand as a topless tent, and with head
and shoulders quite visible the nymphs adjusted
the necessary neck-to-knee costume. That was a
year or so ago. To-day the beach is lined with
hundreds of both sexes, who bathe together with
ivs little concern as Willis has about returning to
Sydney. A casual glance at the aquatic revels
proves how necessary the dressmaker and tailor
are in this life.
Paquin, the inspired Parisian dressmaker man,
has been telling an interviewer things. Such as
“Americans set the fashions.” The world was
under the impression that Frenchwomen did so
Well they did, but now they don’t :
We dressmakers certainly do the work of modelling
dresses ; but it is the American buyers, the representa-
tives of great American houses, who come here by the
score several times a year, who decide on the final shape
and color scheme. These representatives come to my
house to inspect the hundreds of dresses which I and my
assistants have prepared. The buyers visit all the great
firms and make comparisons. Choosing the best models,
they ask for certain dresses, but with such-and-such
changes made. Once suited, however, they buy hundreds
and hundreds of the kind they require, and their selection
fixes the mode for that season.
Money has bred taste, and the cult of clothes is
almost a religion with American women. An
American paper has just published the names of
the hundred richest men on earth—the ones who
can buy most clothes for their womenkind. Fifty
of them are of the United States. Rockefeller
heads the list with a billion dollars. Beit, the
S African diamond man, comes next; the Czar is
fourth. One Australian only is down among the
fifty-million-dollar-men, and he is called “Sir
Jarvin Clark.” Surely our Rupert is the man.
But out of America come the richest people, and
they the women at least—go to Paris to be
clothed. The money that Old Man Graham
makes out of the hog at Chicago, what time he
isn t giving good advice to his chesty son, goes in
dresses at Paquin’s—so many hundred hogs to a
dress.
Blue is to be the reigning color, says a fashion
prophet. “ Pastel blue in all the rich variety of
curious tints that the word stands for. It fades to
green and often to grey, it reflects a tint of gold,
but it is always blue. Peacock blue has come
back, marine blue holds its own, and all blues are
admittedly good by reason of the pastel’s pre-
dominance.” At first night of “ Leah Kleschna”,
there were 64 blue-clad women in the reserved
stalls.
’Frisco papers by last mail say that Melba will
give a few concerts there. Also that “she will
take her long rest in California.” Sounds like the
repose one gets in eternity. It was announced
here some time ago that Melba is due for a
vacation (but no concerts—just straight-out, rest-
ful loafing) in the Commonwealth. And that
plan, so far as I know, is unchanged.
Talking of singers, Miss Nora Long, the Maori-
land soprano, has been making the Austral
Salonites split their elbow gloves of best suede,
with enthusiasm over her singing of “ In the Days
of Roses.”
, Collins Brothers and Co., of Clarence-street, are
issuing a series of Australian Christmas cards.
Our own birds and animals, bits of Australian
scenery, fronds of Commonwealth foliage and
scenes of station life embellish the covers, and
there’s plenty of room inside for you to write the
midsummer greetings.
Miss Beatrice Gritnshaw, who wrote the clever
little booklet on Medlow Hydro for Mr. Mark
Foy, is doing special commissioner work for local
S.M.H. After a sparkling description of the
New Hebrides paradise she doubts if any Aus-
tralian traveller could be roused to enthusiasm by
its picturesque jungle :
No—to get the full flavor out of tropical life in wild
islands, one must have been born and bred in the cold,
grey, decorous North, where the policeman is always out-
side the window-pane, and the window-pane is mostly
wet; where the next-door neighbor and his opinions sit
like a nightmare on the chest of the community ; where
travel means a cushioned railway-carriage with a foot-
warmer and a novel, and adventure the sight of a bull
running unaccompanied down a macadamised road.
Miss Gritnshaw was on an Irish cycling paper,
I'm told, before she came to Australia.
The genial, beaming Mr. Murray White, always
a bosom friend of the late Mr. “Jack” Want, has
retired from practice. His nichein Selborne Cham-
bers will be occupied by Mr. Salenger, from
Bourke. Mr. White's friends say he will now
grow tall roses and cultivate small dogs to fill in
his time, but I should think that even the smallest
dog may pall at times.
Mrs. John Frazer, one of our foremost Some-
bodies, is giving a big at-home this week. Men-
tion is made because the lady is one of the very
few hostesses who pay local professional talent
for its services at her frisks. Since the bridge
mania has become acute, the poor singer or player
has even less chance than of yore to earn a guinea.
Hostesses are greedy gamblers instead of enter-
tainers.
Last night the Allen Taylors added another
deed to the pile that shall be counted unto
them for righteousness. They entertained the
Boys’ Brigade, some hundreds strong, with
harmony, conjuring, and supper at the Town
Hall. Lord Jersey spoke to the boys, and from the
simple chummy-like way he talked, one would think
he’d risen from “ flying papers ” himself instead
of having his peerage reached down to him from
the shelf of his ancestors. For the rest of the time
he and the Mayoress were engrossed in earnest
chat.. Mrs. G. H. Reid and Mrs. Carruthers—the
latter lady has distinguished good looks—wore the
smartest hats, and Miss Margaret Harris, her
niece (who now does her hair like Cleo de Merode)
and Mrs. J. C. Williamson were amongst the
invited guests.
Lady Jersey was so “fetched” by the half-
mourning bouquet of sweet pea presented to her
on arrival in Sydney by Mayoress Taylor that she
asked for seeds of the flower—a chastened mauve
—to take back to her own peeratical gardens. As
“ Wallington ” Layton had “ invented ” and
grown the blooms, he had to shell out (or seed up).
Mr. John Young, the veteran bowler, is enter-
taining Lord Jersey this afternoon at his pictur-
esque place at Annandale.
The engagement of the young King pf Spain to
Princess Ena is announced. She.is very pretty—
for a royal girl. Her father Battenberg was
one of the handsomest of men. But he
must have been glad to die, just to
be rid of the monotony of living in
the same house as his mother-in-law, having
breakfast morning after morning with her
in a bell-tent on the lawn, and putting the cat
out and locking up last thing at night, and know-
ing that his royal brothers-in-law called him “ the
stalled ox.” When he married Beatrice he was a
lieutenant or something in the German army, and
had an income of quite £2B a year.
Two Sydney governesses left within the last
month to take teaching billets in Japan—private
Jap. families. And from England, at the close of
the war, there went a whole squadron of Girtonians
and degree’d women, engaged by the Jap. Govern-
ment for girls’ high schools. Miss Plumblossom
and Mdlle. Butterfly intend to qualify for some-
thing more than man-hunting and hair-dressing.
Mrs. Ebenezer Vickery is working hard on the
rather wooden planks of the Women’s Reform
League. Sydney folk won’t soon forget their first
introduction to Mrs. Ebenezer after her marriage,
when they were invited to meet her at the
converted Lyceum Theatre and talk over good-of-
our-race questions. From Point Piper, and Rose
Bay, Woollahraand Darling Point they poured,
expecting something" superb and millionairish in
the way of entertainment. Instead of caviare
sandwiches and Irroy, Society got prayers and the
singing of hymns. So it doesn’t rush the draw-
ing-room meetings for the advancement of the
Reform League. Last Sunday a lady on the staff
of a semi-sectarian weekly rang up the Vickery
mansion for some particulars of the League, as
she wished to make some perfectly respectful ob-
servations on the same. “We cannot discuss
business matters on the Sabbath,” was the
reply. The “ conversion ” of the Lyceum is
a very commercial spec, indeed ; £l5 a night is
what mummers have to pay for it, and they are
restricted to mum within strictly ring-a-ring-a-
rosy programmes.
Miss Selvvyn, daughter of a bishop, and grand-
daughter of the Bishop Selwyn, who directed the
soular system of Maorilanders, was married
recently in London to a gentleman in holy
orders. Portraits of her abound in ladies’ papers.
Holy-order damsels all look much alike—intellec-
tual and modest as to forehead and expression ;
no straying hair or pin curls, but high-necked plain
frocks, and a locket. On the other hand, the
holy-order men they marry look like Captain
Wilson or Captain Balmain—if those field-mar-
shals wore their collars back to front.
Such handfuls and handfuls of gems there are
in the Sylvaine family caskets that Leah
Kleschna tries to rob these nights at Her
Majesty’s; but the house doesn’t get half a look
at them. They are still in the big box when
Sylvaine turns up the light and startles the rob-
beress by presenting her with a lecture instead of
a pair of handcuffs. Brown, it seems, is the right
tone to go a-burgling in ; afterwards, when you’re
sorry and not in gaol, you picture your grief in a
black skirt and Russian blouse over a white shirt-
waist, and a plain hat of sunburnt straw, with a
meek quill stuck horizontally through the crown.
Note that the quill lies down. But what
a chance “ Leah Kleschna” gives to the
marriage-reformers-—the sort who are always
telling us of the wickedness of the woman
who sells herself for a home with nice soft
cushions, and a seat at every first night, and
something dazzling to wear in it! Leah’s wicked-
ness, they would point out, is so obvious, though,
after all, she only wants the before-mentioned
jewels—and even then she’s going to buy some-
thing to hang in the family meat-safe with them.
And the Berton girl, whom Sylvaine is to marry,
obviously only wants the jewels too; in half-a-
dozen words you get quite a large enough hint
that Sylvaine isn’t much use without them. And
nobody notices the Berton girl’s wickedness ; no
good, kind philanthropist thinks she needs
“saving.” Yet the only essential difference be-
tween the two seems to be that one went after the
jewels with a skeleton key, and the other crooking
an engagement-ring finger and wearing a winning
smile.
The girl who threw herself over the cliffs at
Coogee, last week, should have lived to ornament
the daily press. There is plenty of room in the
journalistic world for the person capable of such a
masterpiece of condensation as her last letter,
“ Suicide—Coogee.” The majority of people who
set out to take their lives leave verbose epistles
based on the literary style of Home Slops or the
Sydney Morning Herald’s anti-socialistic lead-
ers.
The death at Townsville (Q.) of Christo-
pher O’Connell French, grandson of Liberator
Daniel O’Connell, recalls the fact that Sydney
harbored two relatives—a cousin and a cousin’s
son—of the great Dan. Both were Maurice
O’Connells. The father married a daughter of
Governor Bligh, and himself filled the office of
Acting-Governor for a time. The son, born in
Wynyard Square Barracks, did some stirring
army work, but quitted that line for politics, and
entered the old N.S.W. Legislative Council. He
went afterwards to Queensland, where he became
President of the Council, and several times acted
as Governor.
Mrs. Helen Jerome is back in Sydney, after
far-world wanderings, about which she wrote for
Melbourne Age. In Melbourne, on the way back,
she was entertained at dinner at Federal Parlia-
ment House by Dr. Maloney, and she can pride
herself on the fact that she is the first woman to
be entertained there.
A Queensland correspondent writes me :
A blinding shower of rain greeted the arrival at
Brisbane of Queensland’s new Governor. The
elements wept to see the result of a general ran-
sacking of old wardrobes and decayed liat-boxes.
The Windsor uniforms wore many creases and
bore testimony to the corroding power of moth
and rust. Mayor Proe Bonopublicanus was so
unidentifiable in his C.M.G.-ship emblems that
the crowd cheered him, thinking, at first glance,
he was Chelmsford. The soaked soldiers who
lined the streets spoke bitterly—both of them—
about the Federal authorities because they could not
sport overcoats instead of the gaudy review dress.
A little girl dashed through the rain while the
procession passed the G.P.O. and handed His Ex.
a bouquet. Then a crowd of boys raced in the
mud alongside the Vice-regal carriage and
whooped, much to the mortification of the police,
who looked inclined to shoot but for the awk-
wardness of the line of fire. All the women
who lined the pavement thought “ Chummy ” was
just too handsome. So the procession passed
along to Government House, under festoons of
flags, close to row's of feathered palms, and through
an archway enriched by sample products to prove
the great natural resources of the country ; and
something was signed, sealed and delivered. Next
morning the Sydney swimmers arrived, and look-
ing at the flags and greenery said proudly that the
Brisbane crowd were a decent lot, and knew what
was due to an aquatic push when it visited them.
Count Eustach Kierski, a Pole of aristocratic
descent, and known in Brisbane as Charles
Parsons, died like any other man when the end
of the coil is reached, last week. His family
reside in Posen. He came to Australia 20 years
ago to try his luck at work, and turned out to b«
a pretty shrewd dealer in stock. With others he
entered pastoral pursuits at Mount Cornish, but
sold out a few years later, and then bought
Camoola Park. In 1899 he again sold out, and
was a rich man. In 1902 he went back to Queens-
land to look after some station interests at
Winton, but the drought years hit him hard, and
he broke down at last under the strain. Mrs.
Parsons is a daughter of a former partner of her
husband’s, R. Newton.
Henry George Russell Phipps, who committed
suicide in Brisbane Botanical Gardens by cyanide,
was the second son of the Marquis of Normanby.
He settled in the Beaudesert district as a grazier
a good many years ago. A wonderful number of
British and other aristocrats are spread over Aus-
tralia, and quite a large proportion of them seem
to be trying to grow the bullock in some dry
region, where the only sure crop is the curse that
comes home to roost, and where there is nothing
but wire fence for it to roost on.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 4, 1905.
My Dear Yarrie, —
A good many empty houses gape on the Toorak
view just now, and there’s more to follow, judging
by the early shipping lists of 1906. All the frilly
charmers are collecting the paraphernalia generally
marked “ wanted on the voyage.” This includes
a complete angler’s kit, being the appointments
the young she-Australian mostly wants on the
voyage abroad— i.e., powder, cold cream,
transform—but I mustn’t give too much away.
The absentee land proprietor, by the way, is an
epidemic calling for drastic treatment.
There must be a special allowance on the V.R.
expenditure list for caddies’ lids. The other day,
at various times of a sunny afternoon and in
various parts of the city, I counted nine hat-lift-
ings on the part of Aide Buchanan, each time the
Grey Grace ascended or descended from her ve-
hicle. Towards the last, the lid was looking
something like the snail-promenade that generally
distinguished the lid of the shabby Micawber.
And I wondered sympathetically if the V.R.
caddy were allowed a fresh lid every other day.
Great rejoicings at “Mandalay”—the O’Hara
home—that the martial son of the house is qualify-
ing for bloodshed rapidly and passing every test
with promptitude in Fogtown. At latest his
dancing step was perfect, his “ haw ” irreproach-
able, he had taken honors in ragging, and was
in readiness for a commission in the British ranks.
Judge Box, the one bachelor of the Vic. Bench,
fegjfjgh at length his duty to take a little notice of
the sex he has disregarded for so long. At
Stonington the other night the new Solomon made
himself extremely pleasant to the fair of the party.
Invitations are in consequence flowing in, and his
bachelor state may any day be showing signs of
capitulation. In an under-manned capital like
this, an amiable bachelor rising 50 should not be
allowed to peisist in his singleness.
Aide Victor Hood goes to pick up his
strength for the exhausting Stonington duties to
the nearest port of the spiced Empire. He is a
nervous young man with a disposition to lisp,,
and on the Le Hunte staff appreciated the great
amount of leisure he had to get through. Fearing
a break-down in the brisker atmosphere of this.
Bent capital, the Hon. Victor goes off to gather-
muscle, strength and an appetite.
The G.-G. and his dame have their considering-
caps on at this minute, the question of a fancy
fling for kiddies having been mooted. In view of.
the fact that there are no branches to the North-
cote tree, the idea of treating the Victorian off-
shoots is magnanimous. Perhaps it’s their way
of stamping approval on an industry that comes
in nowadays for a good deal of official encourage-
ment.
Love has a varying effect on its female victims.
The tale goes that when a certain little Australian
mummer found her Fate on the Big Smoke stage,
she altered in her dress and ways to a degree that
simply staggered all the ’Roo-landers visiting the
hub. She gave up her pert, giddy manner and
her philanderings, and settled down into quiet,
plain living and dull garb. That’s how real Love
affected this little siren. It generally sends the
sex into wasteful and ridiculous excess so far as
clothes and mode of living go.
The light cure is the latest thing for suffering
femininity. A medical man’s chick is one of
many trying the play of powerful lights on her
anaemic phiz. Some progress and improvement
are reported. In the Cold Country there are
thousands pinning their belief to this light cure.
Remedies are with every decade becoming nicer.
Next we shall be given sugared roses for indiges-
tion, and violet saute for chest complaints.
Macedon is to be the headquarters of a whole
host of tired fashionables. This not so much
because of the bracing air and glorious
sunsets, as being the locale of a Vice-
regal roost. The eye of Toorak has not
been trained to the glories of the scene.
It is merely interested in the clothes of its stylish
neighbor and the goings and comings of the smart
fry in its vicinity. Because of the rush of appli-
cants for the Macedon cottages, local Todgers are
giving themselves airs and picking and choosing
their tenants. So far word has not come along of
any extra curl on the part of the gums or addi-
tional frou-frou of the fern trees.
The Talbots are keeping the chef busy. Every
second night a small batch of officials or useful
Toorakites are bidden to a Stonington dinner.
The meal is always a cool one. The figure of the
aristocratic Tal bottle at the head of the table, says
a recent guest, is, as Daudet would say, a very fish-
bone in the throat. How can one ladle up the
soup heartily or plunge into the peas zealously
with a frozen Sphinx looming a few yards oft?
One can but crumble the bread in one’s best
manner and take a helping of salt as if it were a
pearl from the shell. The fat official who requires

A COMMON MISUNDERSTANDING.
Many people think that the use of salt is merely a
matter of taste inst jad of being a necessity for health and
digestion. As our food is prepared and refined in modern
life the natural salts are removed, and we add common
salt to take its place, although this is by no means an
equal benefit. The natural salts contain phosphates which
nourish the bones, nerves, blood, and the brain. Com'mon
salt has none of these. The modern invention of
“ Cerebos ” Salt exactly replaces the natural salts lost in
preparing- and cooking the food. There are still many
people who think that this “ Cerebos ” Salt is an article
of luxury or perhaps a medicine. It is neither of these,
however, but an invaluable food which doubles the
nutritive value of everything to which it is added. It has
no taste but that of ordinary salt. It is used in the same
way as common salt by putting it in all articles of food in
the kitchen and at table. The growth of children is
stimulated by it, the powers of the body are strengthened,
and long life made more secure.*

A divorce listed for the near future promises to
be interesting. The marm billed for the role of
respondent is smart and youngish. The spouse
petitioner has a difficulty in fixing on the third
party. There are said to be several eligible.
Capt. Collins —the band-box beau of the De-
fence Department—is on his way back to his
Commonwealth perch. He is said to have ac-
quired much information on defence matters while
holidaying abroad.
Master Willie Kelly lent his motor-car —the
one that boasts such a number of casualties—to
the Princess management for its recent drama, in
which the bishop’s carriage plays so prominent
a part.
Miss Florence Young has some interesting
things to say of the land and the fraternity of
dollars. One thing she noticed was the scarcity
of good male voices throughout Uncle Sam’s
country. “There’s scarcely half-a-dozen tenors
of any account in all the theatres.” In
the Cold Country managers are similarly stuck
for the he singer. None, or next to none, are to
be found in the singing quarters. The songstress,
on the other hand, abounds.
A jolly squatter from the Murray borders pro-
vides manicure entertainments for his fluffy
friends. He unearthed the daintiest nail-ca pen-
teress in the city, and escorts flocks of his
she-chums to her little apple-green parlor for
treatment.
All the Buckley clan who were scattered in the
Cold Country at the time of their progenitor’s
death, are sailing back to these parts shortly. The
announcement of the bad tidings was a shock to
the revellers, for, up to quite recently, the health
of the ex-draper had been a subject of congratu-
lation.
Miss Rita Kiddle once more dashes about
I’oorak parts after a longer spell of country
life than she generally indulges in. Her papa
looms on the Athenaeum Club steps when the
Block gets busy, and looks more than ever an
elderly Othello of the dramatic boards.
Miss Gyton, the agile tennis player, now stands
for the third time champion shield-holder of all
Australian courts. Away from the nets, this
racketty person looks as mild as green peas, but,
once on the court, she dashes into the game with
marvellous springiness, and lasts out ail the
heavyweights. The champion is most often the
little lean sort. Same with men. Take Hon.
Michael Scott, f’rinstance—a pink-cheeked
Johnnie, the kind one would expect to be too
tired to crumble a maccaroon at tea, and to call,
therefore, for sponge-cake. On the links he is a
demon. Cricketer McLeod is ai. example.
Stalking the Block he looks a blast and tired
lounger whose strenuousness wouldn’t run beyond
rolling his own cigarettes. In the cricketing field
lie’s a fighter of the first rank.
Annette Kellermann writes in a skittish vein of
gay and rather glorious doings when not pushing
her way with deft stroke in foreign waters. She
tilksof “that fetching Miss McGillicuddy look-
ing charming in ‘ Lady Madcap ’”. Annette also
expresses disgust at the Big Smoke choruses.
“ The girls in the comic opera haven’t the voices,
n ir the figures, nor the dancing legs of William-
son’s crowd.” The Parisians gave the Yarra girl
a great time, and at the latest were turning out
mechanical swimming toys emulative of Annette
in the wet, and dubbed “la Miss Kellermann.”
A Fogtown pianist was inspired to a composi-
tion by the champion brown nymph, and has
named it “The Kellermann March,” instead of
the Kellermann Swim. So many pianists have
done marches and cake-walks and valses, that a
Swim arranged for the pianoforte would be a
refreshing novelty for hot concert rooms. It
could be set to crawl-time, with occasional sinuous
passages suggestive of an eel going by, and here
and there music reminiscent of a bloater in the
distance.
The Talbott]es have been planting trees in the
Botanical Gardens. The idea of the lengthy Ex.
delving deep into the sod with beads of perspira-
tion dripping from his high-born brow, and his
t rey spouse putting her hand to the plough, or
whatever it is that pushes the earth about, is dis-
concerting. One could more easily imagine the
Bent Premier penning odes to the sky-lark or
measuring out feet of mournful elegy.
Some of the Staughton fashionable stock are
being unshipped at Melbourne’s port within a
fortnight, which will enable them to get their
land-legs and their chiffons out of ciea.e in time
for the final spree of the year, to be given by the
head of the clan on the 20th.
The motoriste as she whirrs now sits in state
within a glass house like a pot of asparagus fern
or any rarer green shoot. Toot-tooting along the
road, leaving a trail of massacred domestic pets
along the thoroughfare, the motoriste strikes one
in a droll light. But when she is drawn up in the
neighborhood of a shop, and one or other of the
party emerges from the petroleum conservatory
to traffic in goods, and one or two remain shut up
in the impressive show case, the effect is still
droller. A dame who looks upon the wine when
it is golden more often than necessary was en-
closed in the case the other day in Collins-street,
and a crowd gathered. Her countenance was
rather purple, and the man in the street guessed
she was “ being done to a turn ” in an oven heated
by petroleum.
A swagwoman, supposed to be insane, has been
arrested at Oakleigh. She carried with her an
orthodox swag and billy, but, on the strength of a
golf club, contrived to command the choicest
viands at every house she visited. The awe-
stricken cookies took her to be an indigent
member of the Government House party, or a
leading official of the Women’s National League.
Nowadays there, is more virtue in a golf club than
a tennis racket, and a person who can talk about
cleeks and bunkers with the proper enunciation ig
received into a sassiety that would shy off people
with fourteen hindquarters of meat on their
escutcheons.
Pasteboards were hurriedly circulated last week
for an afternoon spree at the Federal Government
House. The cards mention briefly, “Lady North-
cote at Home, 4.30 to 7.” This little postscript
spree will keep lots of the early migratory folk in
Melbourne a while longer.
Miss Alison Fairbairn’s latest accomplishment
is driving a motor. Not content to lounge in the
petroleum brougham behind a prodigiously fine
snorter, she whistles up a car, and, with the
motor-man by her side, steers the machine along
Tooralc road into the hot, pulsing heart of Collins-
street.
Mr. Sydney Smith, the successful plaintiff in
the claim for commission against the Tyson estate,
is a dandy widower with a taste for straw-deckers
and a weakness for fancy vests. A daughter is
married to a son of Federalist Allan McLean.
Sydney Smith himself is a son of the departed
veteran, who was the early Dick Whittington of
Melbourne. The late Peter Tyson and the
widower in question were inseparables.
Tooralc is wondering hard if anything is to come
of a flirtation that has been carried on furiously
by a local girl of money and an eligible person
who had a rather interesting career in London in
association with a financial Pecksniff whose end
was dramatic. A wealthy mama looks on from
the background and breathes the silence that
represents consent.
The late Charles Arnold affords an example of
the provident mummer—the strutter with a turn
for happy mining speculation. His widow, Dot
Frederics, has the consolation of a fat income.
A recently-deceased patentee bequeathed a
great dream fortune to the family, the next of
kin, and several public institutions. The be-
queathed dream-sums were set out on five pages
of foolscap. The actual value of the patentee’s
estate amounted to a little over £2OO.
Miss Queenie Cadden raises her travelled voice
in song at a concert this week (Thursday). On
the strength of a Fogville record, she had her
box-plan taken up with enthusiastic promptness.
Miss Scott is busily pursuing the study of vari-
ous band instruments, with a view to joining the
Jackson Quartet, into which she is to wed one of
these days. This just demonstrates a familiar
argument to the effect that a girl will do anything
when the masculine encouragement is good and
strong enough. Fancy the saucy Evelyn, after
her frisky* term of comic opera flings, tickling the
drum and scraping the fiddle for a vaudeville
family quintet.
An indignant correspondent writes from Ade-
laide about a garden fete held in aid of the Home
for Incurables. The organisers had a laudable
desire to make the tea and cool drinks tent pay,
so they securely plugged up every water tap on
the grounds. The nearest hotel was about two
miles off, and the temperature was holding its
centenary celebration—just like the death of Nel-
son. Some of the language is said to have been
incurable as well as the patients.
An enterprising Adelaide drapery firm has a
realistic representation of Robinson Crusoe in its
toy department. Half the room is fenced off and
fitted up with rocks and caves and a desert island,
and here Crusoe and man Friday disport them-
selves before the gaze of an admiring public.
Also a cockatoo, a penguin, a parrot, a seagull,
and a cat wander at large and do not devour each
other. Said that Crusoe and Friday get £lO a
week to mooch about, and look through telescopes
at nothing, and generally conduct themselves like
the stranded lunatics they represent.
Yours Affectionately,
TRYPHENA.
The Foreign-trade members in the Rep
resentatives began the debate on the Union Label
last week with the obvious desire to be closured,
in order that they might raise a newspaper howl
concerning “the gag.” Lonsdale, who bellows
frantic questions and roars crushing answers at
himself with the voice of a football barracker, was
followed by Johnson, dreariest of ineffective
talkers. Somebody called out: “ Is this another
stonewall?” The top of Joe Cook’s head glistened
with excited anticipation. “If it is, you know
what to do,” he yelled. Everybody laughed, and
Joe foamed at the mouth. “You know what to
do ; you know what to do,” he screamed again.
But the Government didn’t move, and Johnson
finished his speech, a disappointed man.
The Melbourne Divorce Court has come to
a very undecided decision in the case of the lady
who wanted two divorces simultaneously. The
position was that she married Walker and lost the
run of him. After a while a man who is now dead
told her that Walker was defunct, but later a man
who still survives told her that he was yet ■'live.
Anyhow, she married Brown. Brown also 1 ded
away, and married Amelia Clapp. The case as
put before Chief Justice Madden was this
(1) If Walker was: dead when the lady married Brown,
then she was Mrs. Brown, and Brown had committed
bigamy in marrying Amelia Clapp.
(2) If Walker wasn’t dead then she wasn’t Mrs. Brown,
but Amelia Clapp was.
The lady asked for two divorces. She obviously
only wanted one, but the trouble Was that she
didn’t know which one. But Chief Justice
Madden gave her a divorce from Walker on spec,
whether he was dead or alive, and refused to ex-
press any opinion at all about Brown. This
leaves three people very much where they
were, for neither the lady, nor Brown, nor
Amelia Clapp knows whether any one of
them is married or not. If Walker died
before a certain date—the date of Brown’s first
marriage—then apparently Emily Walker is Mrs.
Brown. If he died after that date, or hasn’t died
at all, then Amelia Clapp seems to be Mrs.
Brown. Chief Justice Madden says (with costs)
that he doesn’t know which of them is Brown,
and they were as far advanced as that before
(without costs). Brown is left to be torn by two
wives and conflicting emotions, but presumably he
also has some costs to comfort him. Even if a
man is only tenth cousin to a lawsuit the wide-
spreading branches of costs generally manage to
throw a bit of grateful shade over him.

POINTS
A daughter of the British royal house is
announced as being about to wed the Catholic
King of Spain. Dill Macky and Woolls Rut-
ledge must intervene.
Said that the man for the post of secre-
tary to the N.S.W. Intelligence Bureau will be
found by Joseph Carruthers in Sydney Tele-
graph office. Come to think of it, Sydney Tele-
graph hasn’t been scoring Government billets for
quite a long time. Now, when Lyne was in
power
Close on £30,000 has been spent on
Government House, Sydney, since 1901, with a
view of beguiling the Governor-General to put in
a substantial part of his time there. The tempta-
tion, however, has not drawn to any great extent,
and the renewal of chairs only once or twice sat
■on fills Carruthers with anguish.
The Carruthers Administration in N.S.W.
ishould be known as the Quarrel regime. Joseph
imakes most of his limelight out of rows with the
Federal Government. The Railway Commissioners
irow among themselves, the Water and Sewerage
Board rows with the Cabinet, and the whole
Junction of Government seems to be in a state of
shindy.
Deputy-Speaker Salmon gave a most wise
:and commendable ruling during the mudwall in
ithe Representatives. He laid it down that when
<one member after another uses the same weary
■ old argument the man in the chair may refuse to
j listen to that argument any longer, and may
• order the orator of the moment to find a new
argument or else leave off.
Melb. Argus published a leading article
regarding the actions of Carty Salmon, Chairman
.of Committees in the House of Representatives.
'Quoth Salmon that afternoon: “I find, in 19
finches of the paper, 16 lies, five misrepresenta-
tions, some half-truths which eminent authority
!has said are worse than lies, one illogical conclu-
sion, and one grammatical error.”
A recent Melbourne item :
The construction of the Flemington tramway has been
temporarily owing to rails and other perma-
ment way material ordered from Great Britain not having
[arrived when expected.
'.This weariful hanging up of everything till soine-
ithing is imported is calculated to get on the
merves of a good Australian, who wants to see his
(country moving.
In 1901 it was reckoned that Australia
mould put 61,695 armed and more or less trained
mien into the field ; the number is now down to
:50,718. The permanent force has only decreased
Iby 292, but as there were only 1568 permanent
nnen to begin with, there wasn’t room for a very
ilarge reduction. The citizen soldiery, which
isliould be growing all the time, has decreased by
110,685. Again the Fools’ Paradise.
The N.S.W. Assembly is truly a House of
!Small Things. Recently Nobbs, M.L.A., took
mp the time of the august gathering by inquiring
whether a German named Casimir Gangioff, a
jperson with one arm and one eye, was begging in
.Sydney. And the Premier himself replied that
'Casimir Gangioff, a person with one arm and one
<eye, was working at present, so there was no
ireason to interfere with him.
In selling leases of 99 years’ duration on
!the Rocks resumed area, the N.S.W. Government
lias decided not to include a building covenant.
Excepting as t 6 the alignment of the streets, the
building conditions will be the same as when
jpeople scrambled on the Rocks and built where
;and how and what they pleased half a century ago.
Apparently the old bag and the kerosene-tin rem-
mant will be legitimate building materials if any-
body wants to use them.
An important matter was carefully evaded
last week in N.S.W. Assembly. Ex-Minister
•Owe’Sullivan asked, along with five other ques-
tions, if it were true that policemen were taken
away from street duty to look after unregistered
and stray dogs. And Minister Hogue replied
coldly, along with five other answers, that police-
men captured diseased dogs while on duty. Con-
sequently the question as to when the healthy but
vagrant dog is arrested remains unanswered to
this day.
During the passage through Queensland
Assembly of the Franchise Bill, some of Philp s
followers and little Jenkinson made pathetic
appeals for the bad old system of copious adver-
tising in the country press. “ Only £l2OO a year,”
they moaned, “was thus expended nowadays”;
but when Morgan pointed out that one paper
with a possible 300 circulation had, under the
Philp regime, drawn £9 per week for many years
from the patient Treasury, a cold chill fell upon
“the Remnant.”
Part at least of the daily press opposition
to the union label is based on fiscal apprehension.
The union label is a “ Made in Australia ’
brand, and will secure that the great bulk of
the unionists and sympathisers will abandon
imported goods in favor of those made in the
country in which we live. In other words, it will
make the theoretical Freetrade Laborite a prac-
tical Protectionist; therefore the press organs of
the importers curse it with a full head of steam on
and the safety valve tied clown.
Federal Opposition-leader Cook was bad
enough when he was a private member ; but since
his appointment as deputy-leader he has burst all
bounds, and his voice is a flood, sweeping all
before it. Cook every conceivable subject,
and he is constantly interjecting. Webster, who
can talk himself a bit, measured Cook’s speeches
for the present session, and found that they ran
into 2367 inches, or 91 solid yards of alleged
oratory. During three receflt sittings of the
House, Cook made 13 speeches and 78 inter-
jections. This was for the week ended Nov. 9th.
It was a plain, ordinary week, with nothing to
stir the Opposition to any particular effort. And
with brassy impudence Cook squeals about Deakm
and Isaacs “stifling free speech.” That is how
Cook goes on when he is “stifled. If lie were
given air, probably the whole House would be
asphyxiated through Joe taking up all the
breathing space.
The Queensland House of Nominees ap-
pointed a committee to consider the Land Mono-
poly Tax Bill, and the committee reports that
“ the conditions which might possibly, under some
circumstances, justify legislation for the so-called
bursting-up of large estates, do not at present
exist in Queensland.” If it sticks to this view,
there are two noble possibilities open to Premier
Morgan. One is, during the recess, to send up
sufficient new nominees to make the Council
change its so-called mind on this point. A still
better one would be to send up enough new and
firmly-pledged nominees to carry a Bill for the
abolition of the Upper House, and to set to work
next session to burst up that very large and
barren estate for good and all.
The municipal council of Caulfield (Vic.)
seems to be the champion fool corporation of that
State. Years ago it delegated to a tram company
the right to run trams over certain roads, and also
gave the co. sole possession of the centres of the
roads, but, with amazing folly, it omitted to make
any stipulation as to the return of the roads
should the co. fail to run any trams. The co. failed
to do so—just as so many other Private Enterprise
concerns have failed. The roads have gone to
ruin. The co. is nothing but a brass plate, out of
which it is impossible to extract any satisfaction ;
and the council can’t do anything till it has placed
the co. in liquidation, and got its assets—the
roads —made over in legal form. That process
has already cost £2OO, and is likely to cost as
much more, and the council is considering whether
a high conical cap with some bells on top of it
would be a suitable costume to wear when ex-
plaining things to the ratepayers.
Last week the gaud mayor of Sydney was
informed that there were 5000 unemployed in the
city, and he staggered back and said that it was a
disgrace that such a thing should be in a “ young
country.” What an amount of satisfaction
some people seem to get out of the dull wheeze
about a “young country!” There are only 12
IN HEAVEN AS IT IS ON EARTH.
SPIRIT: “ What! What's that ? You exclude ME! This is the result of your pernicious Immigration Restriction Act. I give you
warning, sir—you are driving Capital from the country! ”
pence in a shilling in a young country, the
same as in an old one. If a young country
chooses to import things instead of manufacturing
them its artisans will be unemployed—the same as
in an old one. For every £ a young country
borrows it owes 20s.—same as an old one. We
can’t eat the youngness of the country, nor drink
it. It is a disgrace to any city of 500,000 people
to have one-twentieth of its adult male population
unemployed, but what on earth has the youngness
of the country to do with it ?
“ Ailath ” : In view of the damage done to
the dignity of Webster, M.H.R., when he arrived
at Redfern without much time to spare, and
couldn’t be supplied with a sleeping compartment
because he hadn t ordered one, and in view of the
dust, language and turmoil that ensued, I beg to
propose that in all future cases of the kind : (1)
All the other passengers should be fired out and
the member should have the whole dashed train.
(2) That the entire blessed refreshment room
should be put in the hat-rack of the member’s
carriage. (3) That the general manager of the
railways should himself get on the engine and
drive like . (4) That there should be a wreath
of flowers round the engine funnel. (5) That the
whistle should applaud violently, and (6) that the
people should cheer as the vehicle goes by. I think
this should meet the case.
The not usually bloodthirsty Adelaide
pressman, W. J. Sowden, writing from China to
his own Register (23/11/5): —
The ill-luck which had attended us on a previous visit
to the Place of Execution was with us still. There had
been executions the day before—we saw the blood of
eight beheaded victims slowly drying and caking in the
sun—and there were to be some on the following morn-
ing, but to-day decapitations were off the list. The cross
on which the punishment of the 1000 cuts is administered
to women, who are not struck on a vital spot till after all
the protuberant flesh has been removed, lay near to the
congealed blood, and it had been used within a week ;
but it was idle now.
Hard luck, indeed. The gentle, desirable Chow
whom Bruce Smith calls “a splendid worker,”
was loafing that day instead of industriously
cutting 11L0 pieces off a live woman.

The Tory yell about “the boycott ” re the
Union Label was silenced in a few words by
Spence, M.H.R., in the House of Reps, the other
night. ’ Spence said he patronised bakers who
paid 525. a week for an eight-hours day, not bakers
who paid 365. a week for a 12 hours day, and that he
followed the same rule with his tailor, his boot-
maker, and so on. “If Mr. Kelly or Mr. Lons-
dale want to eat bread made in a bakehouse full
of plague rats, or wear clothes that will bring fever
into their homes, they may,” he said. “But if
this sort of preference is hoycotting, I intend to
keep on boycotting. It is what every honest man
ouglit to do.”
Some new regulations have come into force
in reference to tl?e payment of engine-men on the
Victorian rail wavs The eight hours system has
allegedly been in force for two or three years. In
reality the day, even under the best conditions,
was from 8f to 91 hours. The eight hours pre-
tence has now been abandoned, and nine hours
are calculated as a day’s work. The time spent
in preparing engines for the trip and putting them
away after the trip is over (three-quarters of an
hour or an hour) is now paid for. That is to say,
the men used to prepare and put away the engines
in their own time and were not paid for the work,
Now they do it in the State’s time, and are paid
for the work with the money that is deducted
from the amount paid them for the actual driving,
so being paid means the same amount of money
as not being paid.
“ D.H.R.” complains :
The N.S.W. Advances to Settlers Board is a delusion
and a snare, and a thing deserving of many adjectives.
Its wheels and other parts want lubricating with an axe,
and its drivers want remarks about “Awake! arise”
made to them in a voice like Baker abusing a poet. There
are too many flies on it, and it doesn’t revolve fast enough
to blow them off. If a settler who sees a good business
possibility ahead, and wants money, applies to the Board,'
it is six months, or thereabouts, before he gets a reply ;
and by that time Che deal is “ off,” and the money useless.
I have known two or three such cases. The applicants
were all deserving characters, who had good grass, and
plenty of improvements on their places to warrant any
money-lending office granting them a loan, but through
red-tapeism they missed the tide that leads on to fortune
and sheep. This is not the way for a country to go ahead.
A mortgage is not a mummy to which 1000 years or so
don’t matter.
Lloyds Weekly (London) tells the story
of a Battersea tradesman who went to the country
which, according to our wildly ignorant Bruce
Smith, welcomes all immigrants with open arms
and asks no questions at all: —
For reasons of his own the gentleman in question took
a steerage passage to New York. Except that the great
majority of his companions were Russians and Germans,
the voyage out was fairly comfortable. His troubles
commenced when he arrived under the shadow of the
famous statue of Liberty. He went ashore on Ellis Island,
confident in the possession of sufficient money to satisfy
the inquisitive officials that he was not an undesirable
alien. He was asked his age. Like a true-born English
man he told the truth, and the figures “ 51 ” were imme-
diately chalked on his left shoulder. Having passed the
doctor, to whom he bad to present himself almost in a
state of nature, our friend thought he was safe, but to his
great disgust he was informed that his 51 years were a bar
to his admission to New York. This was on a Sunday
morning, and until the following Tuesday he was kept
inside a wire-protected enclosure, through which he
talked to the friends he had gone over to see. Not until
one of these, having proved to the satisfaction of a certain
official that he was a man of means, became security for
him, was he released. One of his companions—a Welsh-
man__Was less fortunate. Although he put down £5O in
front of the officials, he was sent back because he had a
slight malformation of the shoulder.
Australia at all events didn’t make.chalk marks
on the backs of the Six Hatters or whitewash
lar 0 e figures oli the spine of the Petriana myth.

Remarkable Testimony.
A Newcastle Lady’s Experience. Twice Given
up by the Doctors, and Twice Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root .Pills Prove Sucessful.
Mrs. Elizabeth Lightfoot, of Lambton Heights,
Newcastle, N.S.W., relates a story which is often
told by women ; of suffering for years from habitual
constipation, dreadful headaches, general debility,
and subsequently from a bad leg. How Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills restored her to health,
and healed the running sores when doctors’ efforts
failed, is best told in her letter which bears her
signature, attested to by a Justice of the Peace.
“ For over four years,” she states, “I was in bad
health and suffered dreadfully from constipation,
severe headaches, and general debility. I took all
kinds of medicine, and
my doctor prescribed
for many months. He
could practically do
nothing for me, and
finally advised me to
take an opening medi-
cine regularly. I tried
many pills and reme-
dies, but I failed to
gain any benefit beyond
the temporary move-
ment of the bowels.
The local storekeeper
one day advised me to
try llr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. I had no
faith in pills, but I tried
them, and the first
couple of doses seemed
to have a wonderful
effect, I continued with
Mrs. Ligiitfoot,
(from a photo.)
them, and I can truly say they gave me every
encouragement. I soon regained my strength
and activity, and was free from headaches and
dizziness, and my system became regular in every
way. This all happened about four years ago.
Some time since, having discontinued your pills
altogether, I had a breaking out on my leg, which
extended from the ankle to the knee. I used
various ointments, but finding they had no
deterring effect I consulted the doctors, who
treated me for some months, but they failed to do
me any good, and advised me to lay up or go to
the hospital for treatment. It was only as a last
resource that I thought of your pills (having
previously been under the impression pills were
only purgatives). I commenced taking them
regularly, and gradually my leg got better, and in
a few months entirely healed. This wonderful
cure entitles them to every credit for their blood
purifying and healing properties. I have since
used them regularly once or twice a week, and I
have much pleasure in recommending your pills as
a household medicine for children and adults, and
especially for mothers under all conditions. lam
giving this unsolicited testimonial as a debt of
gratitude to you, and you may use it in any way
you wish.—Mrs. Elizabeth" Lightfoot.” Sub-
scribed and declared at Lambton, N.S.W., this
third day of June, one thousand nine hundred and
five, before me, George E. Hardy, J.P.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are not simply
purgatives. They contain more important
properties which act directly on the liver, kidneys,
and blood : correcting, purifying, and restoring
the whole system to health and strength. The
many wonderful cures effected by these pills
have awakened the interest of all clases to the
curative, cleansing, and healing properties, and to-
day Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are the family
remedy, not only of the miner and the artisan,
but of the niore wealthy, professional, and
independent section of the community.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Gehrs was responsible for a great upheaval at
his first appeal-shoe at the sticks after his return from
England. He played for the Port against Adelaide. The
latter declared its innings closed with four down for 309.
Then Gehrs took the willow, slogged three sixes, 23
fourers and many other things, carried his bat for 200,
and brought about the defeat of the metropolitan club.
* * * *
Playing against Waverley on the latter’s wicket
on Saturday the crack North Sydney team found itself up
against some of the smartest fielding seen this season.
People who are under the impression that O. Gregor3 7 is
played out should have seen him picking up the leather,
even when he had to hang himself over the fence side-
ways to reach it with his left; and they would have under-
stood that such a fielder is a good man to have in any
team. The wicket was certainly soft, but it appears to
have been the fielding which broke up the Northmen and
got them all out for 147. Five of them were caught, one
stumped, one run out, and only three bowled. Waverley
got to the sticks on a pitch that was slowly dr3 7 ing, and
bumped up 111 for the loss of three wickets. North’s
prospects look bluer than the miller’s daughter. Out at
St. Luke’s Park it certainly was boggy, and when the
Paddington captain won the toss he graciously waved
Burwood to the wickets, where Noble and Collins got
loose on them. Noble took five wickets for 26 runs, and
Collins five for 17. The remaining 13 were scored by a
player called Sundries—in fact, Sundries was the third
best scorer on the side. When Paddington got to the
wickets, in spite of the fact that it had a crowd of the
XI. playing, it didn’t shape a great deal better than Bur-
wood, and the mighty Trumper himself scored a duck.
Noble made 22, and the side vanished for 78, 22 more
than Burwood’s record. In Burwood’s second innings
Noble got four wickets for three runs, and Collins
one for 15, and the day ended with five wickets down
for 22. Evidently Gordon was a very considerable joint
in North Sydney’s late tail, for on Saturday it smashed
up Central Cumberland for 99, and then made 134 for the
loss of five wickets. The other late N.S. limb, Middle
Harbor, did not do so well against Redfern. The Syrian
suburb made 294, the biggest score of the day, and when
play stopped, Middle Harbor had replied with 17 runs for
one wicket. Considering the pitch Glebe made a fair
stand against Balmain, notching 120, but the Wilks
suburb responded gamely with 55 for five wickets,
although Glebe had the benefit of Cotter’s bowling.
Penman did some ferocious bowling for the University
against Sydney District, taking five wickets for 20, but
Sydney managed to make 180 notwithstanding. Present
‘prospects are that next Saturday will see the positions on
the premiership competition list evened up considerably.
* * * *
A feature of the Balmain-Glehe cricket match
on Saturday was the breaking out of Gorr3 7 . Of the five
wickets Balmain lost, four fell to Gorry, who stumped
three and caught one !

Adelaide pressmen gave away their annual
cricket match with Parliament by 7 sheer good nature.
Last year most of the runs for the talkers were made by
E. H. Coombe, who is actually an editor, and ought to
have pla3 7 ed for both sides, or neither. This time he
was bowled for 0; but he took three of the pressmen’s
best wickets. Also, Parliament played a doorkeeper to
make up its side. He caught out the best bat among the
writers —a terrific chance at square-leg which no real
Parliamentarian would have been liked 7 to hold ; and
batting last (as a sub.) he made 13 himself, and helped
M‘Donald, M.H. A., to carry his bat for 64. And the Press
lost bv 27.

The obstacle which has been hindering the
advancement of the Australian Cricket Board of Control
has been partly unveiled, and it leaks out that the oppo-
s tion comes from the section which is liked'to have its
wings clipped. The newly-formed Board is credited with
a desire to do away with the bigdiv. which the Australian
Eleven player has for a long time tied in the corner of
his handkerchief ; and, after paying the members of the
team a guaranteed sum, to distribute the remainder for
the benefit of cricket throughout the various States. The
idea is a good one, but it doesn’t arouse the mad en-
thusiasm of the blessed Eleven.

Australasian Amateur Swimming Champion-
ships meeting commenced at Brisbane Dry Dock, last
Saturday afternoon. The weather was cool and fine, and
Brisbane poured out its shekels and filled the available
space comfortably. Tbe Quarter Mile attracted seven
starters, who made a beautifully simultaneous dive off,
When they came up Kieran streaked away to the front
with Queenslander Springfield second and R. Healey
third. Kieran kept his lead with perfect ease and won by
2 (yd. from Springfield, who beat R. Heale3 7 by syd. The
champion made no effort and took smin. 37sec., which is
a lot slower than he usually 7 travels over the distance,
both the world and the Australian records being held by
him. The former is smin. 19sec. and the latter smin.
27sec. In the 2‘2oyd. Breast Stroke, Queenslander
Morrison stuck to Kieran gamely 7 till at three-quarters of
the distance the champion spurted and won by 7 12yd. in
the record time of 3min. 32.3-ssec.
* * * *
Australian cycle sports promoters are at their
wits’ ends to attract the people. The big Sydney meeting
last month was remarkable for its fine racing, its exciting
finishes, and absolutely perfect weather. Nevertheless,'
the “ hill” was a desert, and the empty benches of the
paying pavilions gibbered at the Summer Nights Syndi-
cate hke teeth in the naked skull of Impecuniosity. Then
the East Melbourne Bicymle Club’s meeting at Exhibition
Oval last w.eek also proved a frost, and the club is won-
dering why. In Sydney, tbe people who think nothing
can be a success without the foreigner said it was because
Yankee Ivor Lawson wasn’t racing. Well, he raced in
Melbourne, and he and MacFarland together didn’t seem
to draw a cent better than. MacFarland did on his
solitary in Sydney. A visible effect the Yankees
produced was that one of them roused some hoot-
ing ; but hooting didn’t fill the club’s coffers.
Evidently something will have to be done. In the event
of the promoters being unable to secure George Reid to
perform the Yes-No act on a unicycle driven by elec-
tricity, or Joe Cook to ride his own talking-machine, it
looks'as though a lot of the short mile sprints, which are
mostly loitering matches for the first 1000 yards, and a
wild rush over the last 760, will have to give way to longer
races with good lap prizes to make them fast. Writer
noticed that during the Sydney 7 carnival the interest used
.to rise to fever pitch during the Five Mile. There’s only
one gamble in a mile-run. A five mile race means changing
positions, men dropping out one by one,, desperate
struggles for the lap-prizes to pay drinking expenses
afterwards, and Chance and Accident cropping up like
the joker in a game of euchre. And it all makes for ex-
citement,, which is the thing that draws.
« * * *
Amateur cycling in Maoriland is now almost
extinct, smothered by the game wnich fills the pocket
instead of decorating the sideboard with pots of pewter
and other brummagem. The League of Wheelman has a
rule that amateurs wishing to join the cash section must
stand down for a year, and nine Canterbury amateurs—
said to be the last of the Mohican* —are now doing this
with enthusiasm in order to qualify for the higher cycling
life, so to speak.
* < * *■
S. A. League of Wheelmen has recovered by law,
for a city rider, £8 odd, won by him at a country sports
meeting last June. The sports committee had withheld
the prize because it suspected confederacy. At its first
inquiry it found that nothing could actually be proved,
but at its second it got an attack of suddenness, dis-
qualified all the riders, and held on to the prize money.
The decision for plaintiffs was given with admitted
reluctance by the local court Judge. Evidence, he said,
“ certainly pointed strongly to a concerted attempt to
obtain the prize money improperly ; it was a pity that
the committee muddled its inquir3 7 .”
- * * *
By the way, Mayer protests against his Bulletin
description as “Meyer, the Flying Jew.” The “a” has
been in the paternal name for generations, and the
Jewishness is repudiated with emphasis. He’s Mayer the
Flying German. Nevertheless he was christened Henri,
which is French.
