The cycling fete promoters’ combine has finally
decided to stick to its offer of £6 ss. per programme to
visiting “ stars,” so pie-biters Lawson and MacFarland will
either have to climb down or ride for the stakes only.
The combine runs 34 programmes during the season, which
pans out at nearly £220 to each rider, providing he is fit
enough to start every time ; besides this they have a
mortgage on the scratch prizes and a chance for the han-
dicap ditto. Lawson sulked in Sydney. At the E. Melb.
Club’s meeting they got their own terms, so that club
stands out of the combine ; but they w 7 ill have to make
lip their minds one wa3' or the other for the Austral
meeting. Rutt and Ma3'er are stated to have agreed, in
writing, to the combine’s terms. Meanwhile the local
stars, Farley, P3 7 e, and co., get no offer of even ss. appear-
ance money, let alone £6 ss.

“ F.” : After all the recent talk of motor-racing,
this year’s Austral meeting—spread over five days at
Melbourne Exhibition oval—is bound to draw big crowds.
In the matter of genuine sporting interest, cycling is as
far ahead of motoring as boat-pulling is ahead of
3'achting. The man does the w 7 ork, not the machine
that carries him, and although, unhappily, the man is not
always a noble sport, he has to put in some good work at
one time or another if he wants to earn his corn at a
gathering of “ all the champions.” Since the early days
of Plugger Billmartin in Melbourne the crowd has never
had such a strong infusion of foreign competition in
an M. B. C. meeting as it is getting this year.
Mayer, Rutt, Lawson, MacFarland, and Samuelson
are a strong assortment of invaders, and if they don’t
have things too much their own v 7 a,v in competition with
the locals, they will be well worth their takings—to the
management. Seemingly they are expensive people,
these pedallers from foreign parts. And besides these
there are the Arnst brothers, of Maoriland.
* * * *
The Stinking Fish party has something to think
over in the fact that of the five motors which finished the
recent reliability run—under conditions unknown in
European tests—one car and engine were made, barring
tw 7 o insignificant details, from beginning to end in Mel-
bourne. Of all forms of modern engineering, a motor car
requires perhaps the greatest care and accuracy and
precision of workmanship. Yet it is impossible to attend
any public function without hearing stout persons arise
and contend that some such complicated article as a
clothes peg or a firelig-hter should be dragged across
14,000 miles of sea because Australia does not possess
workmen capable of turning it out.

In the person of F. Hardens, N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen recently registered probably the lengthiest
pedal-pusher now in active revolution on this globe.
Hardens goes a bit over 6ft. 4in. in his socks, and it takes
41in. of tape to circle his chest. D. Ellis, a whilom
N.S.W. amateur champion, now retired, was even a
lengthier bikist. Ellis was a 6ft. 54in. streak of flying
humanit3', and was built in proportion.
It would be a most carefully built law that
Father Robinson would not find a way through, and the
Victorian Act forbidding lotteries seems to have been
slung together anyhow. When the snowy-haired pro-
moter first advertised an art union with a first prize of
£5OO he said nothing about drawing it at his bazaar.
Then the Attorney-General was asked how it was that
the genial father had got permission to run a lottery 7
when the law expressly forbade them. The Attorney-
General replied that he would not allow any lottery to be
run ; but that an art union in connection with a bazaar
was legal. Father Robinson rose to the occasion and
replied that his lottery was an art union. It was to be
drawn two or three weeks after the bazaar was over, but
he proposed to re-erect the bazaar for that night. So
last week a few stalls were run up in Melbourne Town
Hall, and a little bazaar was run while the art union was
drawn. It was supposed by most people that the art-
union tickets would admit holders to the drawing; but
they didn’t. Everybody 7 who entered the Hall had to buy
two more art-union tickets. So Father Robinson filled
his list up to the brim. The man who can give points to
a clever white-haired cleric knows enough to run a hotel.
* * * *
Some good sport is promised from the side-shows
incidental to the Weber-Buttan Singh wrestle at Fitz-
geralds’ Building (Melbourne), next Monday week. The
Olympic Club, which undertakes the management, is
young and full of enthusiasm. Pupils of rival teachers
are to have a go at wrestling, boxing, &c., prior to the big
struggle, and the ymungsters are training as though their
lives depended on the issue. One pair of bantam catch-
as-catch-can-ers are thought to be so evenly matched that
they will probably wrestle themselves to a simultaneous
stand-still or, rather, lie-still.
* ■* * *
. Wirth Bros, were more than generous in the
matter of the wrestling match fiasco which concluded
their Melbourne season. By the terms of the agreement
Weber and Gunga Brahm were to wrestle two or three
falls— i.e., either man had to win or lose two falls —for a
certain percentage of the takings, the winner to get
a lot more than the loser. Gunga Brahm broke the
agreement by refusing to wrestle any more after Weber
had downed him once. Clearly he was entitled to nothing
at all, but when the trouble was all over the slippery
Hindu received £l5 under the contract, just as though
he had fulfilled his share of it. The Wirths must be
heartily sick of the colored wrestlers by this time. Some
months ago Buttan Singh caused an uproar at the circus
by half-strangling y 7 oung Weher, in order to save the £lO
challenge money which he was bound to cash up if he
couldn’t turn the white man over within 15 minutes. The
other night Buttan’s old colleague Gunga disgusted the
audience by “ turning dog ” on Weber and the manage-
ment as aforesaid.
The wood-choppers’ Australian Cup meeting at
Latrobe (Tas.) produced some fine displays with the axe,
and Mangan (Tas.), although defeated in the first heat,
hung out long enough in the final to defeat Challis and
the rest of the opposition. It was a swift contest, and
the finish between six axemen was only a question of a
hit or two.
“Ed.”: My friend Abraham Noses says the
city flats reckon they’re smart, hut they’re not in the
same street with the country punters ; and he tells this
bitter experience : Mrs. Haystack owned a horse. She
said the horse was a sure thing, but that she’d stiffen him
for a consideration. This was agreed to by the book from
the city. Diana Haystack said that, as her friends were
all on her horse, she would take a £2O bet about it, for
the look of the thing, and to avert the calamitous finger
of suspicion. This was agreed to. The country crowd
avalanched on to the dead ’un, which started at three’s.
He didn’t get too good a start, and at the distance was
apparently out of it. The crowd was getting read3 7 to
jump on itself, when Mrs. Ha3 7 stack’s horse came with a
rush and cleared out from the field, finishing first, the
rest nowhere. The bookmaker went off to have a solo-fit
when he saw the first ticket rushed in was that handed
up by Mrs. Diana Haystack herself.

Wren, the King of Collingwood, announces that
he has taken over the sole control and management of
Melbourne's pony racecourses, with a view to elevating
the malodorous “sport” of pony-racing to “a height
hitherto unattainable ” in Victoria. He promises to do
this “ by the energetic application and exercise of experi-
enced watchfulness and rigorous action,” which is much
the same thing as saying that his vigilance will be of the
same brand as is used in safeguarding the Collingwood
tote.
* * * *
The heavy downpour of rain in Melbourne,
which brought about the postponement of the opening of
thb Austral meeting, did not prevent the rowing of the
pair-oar championship. The Mercantile crew finished
first, with Hawthorn second, and Seymour, away to
glory, third. It was a tough struggle, and the boats,
being filled with rain water, were very crazy.
* * * *
Sydney’s Prince Alfred Yacht Club “did the
thing properly ” on Saturday, and carried out two pro-
grammes simultaneously. The yachts contested the first
of the three races which decide the club championship
for the year over a course which meant practically twice
round the Harbor. The cruisers had a handicap for the
usual spoils. Of the remaining two championship races
one will be an ocean race. Fleetwing went to the
front, but at Bradley’s Head Petrel forged ahead, and
went right across the Harbor. When they came together
at the lightship Petrel was first, with Sunbeam, Magic,
Fleetwing, Culwulla and three others in that order. At
the buoy 7 there were changes of position in the rear-
guard, but the first three kept their sequence. Then
Sunbeam ran past Petrel, and led round Shark Island.
At the alleged fort, Magic was a second in front of Petrel,
and Culwulla had slipped into fourth place. The
nor’-easter had turned black by the time the second thresh
down the Harbor commenced, and the yachts folded
their extras like wings. Petrel kept the topsail aloft and
went plunging through it, passing Magic and gaining on
Sunbeam, which tore through the water in front. Bound
the Pile Light and back to Shark Island they 7 flew,
Sunbeam still in front, and away to the finishing line,
Sunbeam was Still ahead. But when the times were
worked out it was found that Petrel had won the race,
with Scotia second and Sunbeam third. It was a lovely
race, and on board the following steamer the club had
provided music and refreshments which lent a glory and
a sheen to the race through the gray seething water.
* » * *
The Sydney Amateur, the Dingy, and the Skiff
Clubs alTheld races on Saturday afternoon on Port Jack-
son. At the first a pleasant nor’-easter made for ideal
conditions, but as the afternoon wore on it increased in
intensity till it turned “ black,” and then capsizes became
the order of the going, and there was joy to those who
laid the odds.
* * * IT
It is now proposed by members of the Motor
Boat Club of N.S.W. to hold a “ reliability test ” from
Sydney to Newcastle towards the end of the season. The
idea is a splendid one, as there are quiteenough sea-worthy
craft on the. Club’s register to make a good “ field,” and
the actual contest should be about the most thrilling
event in Australasian sporting history up to date. The
big French club at Marseilles held one of these ocean
reliability tests last season, but the boats ran into a
terrific gale, and the torpedo craft which followed the
test did good work rescuing the testers.
American champion sprinter Arthur Duffey,
who was beaten at Sydney Cricket Ground by 7 Nigel
Barker, has definitely retired from the track and joined
the sporting staff of a U.S.A. magazine. He was an-
nounced to begin his new career-by showing in cold type
how he managed to travel round the world and live on
the fat of the land wherever he went as a lily-white
amateur. All of wiiich will be interesting ; but if Arthur
Duffe.v wants to make a real scoop he should write a book
explaining how he managed to avoid being found out till
his hind leg's lost their cunning and he realised that the
time had come to retire. There is not likely to be any-
thing new in the exposure—merely the old tale of secret
appearance money and a double-extra stretching of the
term “ expenses.”
Judging by the meagre telegraphic reports, the
Kalgoorlie “Hundred” meeting was a huge success.
Four thousand people were present the opening night,
and a limited number of bookmakers were licensed to
operate. Betting was colossal for an athletic meeting,
and a whole library of books was opened on the
“ Hundred.” E. R. Terry and Arthur Postle were
favorites at 3 and 5 to 1 against them. Jarvis came next
at 6 to 1, and as much as 20 and 33 to 1 were on offt-r
against the outsiders. Six thousand people rolled up to
see the final. Arthur Postle never gained a place, and A.
S. Jarvis, on the 10yd,, finished first, with E. R. Terry
second. R. Mitchell, the third man, was, as far as the
betting was concerned, an outsider, while 16 to 1 was
offered against M‘Martin, who ran fourth.
No, we don’t really appreciate golf in this
country. In the recent international match for £4OO,
Taylor (Englishman) landed a ball on a grass slope, and it
began to roll away towards a sand-pit. There were 10,000
people watching, and, says the London Daily Express,
“ there was a minute of agony for the Englishmen pre-
sent, but the Scots all the time were screaming in frantic
g'lee, stopping suddenly with a half-stifled sigh of disap-
pointment as the ball came to a standstill on the very
edge.” You couldn’t find 10,000 people in the whole
Commonwealth who would scream over a golf ball if it
were dropping into Hades.

Maoriland Boxing Association proposes to
borrow three first-class professional boxers froth Aus-
tralia next season to give exhibitions and generally
instruct the y 7 oung Maorilander how to stoush. With the
fire and general lust of butchery that infects the Maori-
land blood, the venture will no doubt prove a paying one
for the instructing pugs, if they live to enjoy the profits.
The Whitelaw-Rollo scrap at Sydney National
Sporting Club was brief and humorous. Rollo was evi-
dently under the impression that the people were there to
be entertained, and he did his best to amuse them accord-
ingly. He fought his battle as George Reid would have
fought-it in his mind, and burlesqued the whole theory
and practice of stoush, duck, and side-step. Whitelaw,
evidently annoyed by this disrespectful behavior, was
unusually cautious and hit away steadily. Once he landed
Rollo on the jaw. Rollo threw up his arms, dropped to
the floor, turned a somersault or something, and bound-
ing up like an india-rubber ball uppercut Whitelaw on the
chin with the right, and swung the left round
to the jaw. It was a neat bit of business. White-
law grew more dour, and met a series of rushes
with straight pokes which sent the dancing dervish to
the floor. Every time Rollo went down Whitelaw
moved rapidly away, afraid of the rebounding trick. In
the third act Whitelaw managed to bang Rollo on the
jaw just as he was in mid-air doing a balloon act. The
effect was electrical. Rollo rolled over and lay still till
he was safely counted out. The aerial act is not a success.
* * -* *
The retirement of champion Jim Jeffries has
brought out some strange and wonderful aspirants into
the Yankee ring. The latest is a giant Red Indian, weigh-
ing 25 stone. This incredible mass of meat has acted as a
sort of policeman in one of the Indian reservations. His
claim for fistic honors appears to rest on the fact that he
once knocked out and captured eight of his fellow-coun-
trymen who had painted themselves red with yellow
stripes, and uttered a strange arrangement of sounds,
and started to make thing's willing. But the men that
Lo, the poor Indian, is likely to meet in the ring are not
going to wait to be fallen on by half a ton of man. The
noble but elephantine savage will probably last about one
round when he meets a real boxer.
* * * *
A recent fight at Queen’s Hall, Melbourne, was
one of the most sudden things in the history of the busi-
ness. Fred Preston, the local heavyweight, whose left
punch is like a stroke of lightning, but who is a performer
of aldermanic proportions, met William Green, a coal-
puncher from Newcastle (N.S. W.), with a local reputation
of long standing. Green looked hard enough to knead
his tubby adversary up like dough in about half a round,
but the opposite thing happened, and Preston wiped Mr.
Green out in a most dramatic way in 2min lOsec. It was
science up against the other thing, and science visited
Mr. Green so often and so hard that the man from the
place where the coals come from was down five times in
the brief space mentioned, and wore the troubled and
hopeless expression of a man kicked by nine mules simul-
taneously. The police stopped the fight to prevent
further unnecessary bloodshed, and Preston was declared
the winner, while the gallery loudly execrated the judg-
ment of those who brought together the Unassorted pair.

News of Kid M‘Coy comes from America.
Kid’s true name is an aristocratic one—Norman Selby.
On Oct. 19 he was married at the Algonquin Hotel, New
York, to Lillian E. Ellis (formerly the actress Lillian Earl),
widow of Edward C. Ellis, once proprietor of locomotive
works at Schenectady. “Mr. Selby ” announced after his
wedding that he had dropped the name of Kid M’Ooy for
ever. Quoth he (according to an American paper) : “ I
promised m3 7 wife that, when we were married, the
sporting life I had led would be left behind. I
have seen everything. Nothing in the long experience
I have had with sporting people appeals to me. With
that kind of life I am done. I intend to drop the name
M‘Cov for ever. I want m3 7 friends to know that it will
offend me if they so address me.” Yankee papers add
that M‘Coy (or Selby) has been a frequently married man.
He married his first wife (Julia Woodruff) three times, and
divorced her twice before she died. The new Mrs.
M’Selby is said to be richly gilded. She became a widow
in 1904.
“ Hamer” : Lord Jersey’s passage through
the streets of Sydney was done in quite a casual
way, but the reception from those who saw him
was cordial. The most enthusiastic person I came
across was an excited individual who grasped my
arm with one hand, wildly waving his hat witn
the other, and speaking volubly : “ What s the
matter? Hooray! Hooray! Who is it? Hooray!
Lord Jersey ? ’Oo the h—l is he ? Hooray !
Governor of Noo South Wales? When? Hooray !
Hooray !” I left him screaming after the retreat-
ing carriage—“ Hooray ! God save the King !
Alleluia ! Rule Britannia ! ”
A discussion in Vic. Assembly the other
day disclosed the figures and the real facts of
Bent’s Closer Settlement farce. The system has
been in operation almost a year, yet, so far, not
90 applicants for land have been settled thereon.
Ninety ! And there are 250 applicants calling at
the Board’s offices every week ! Out of 174 estates
offered only eight have been sufficiently cheap to
be worth buying, and they were mostly bought at
exorbitant prices, but then the other 1(36 landlords
were so much more exorbitant that the
eight looked cheap. Of these eight estates
half the blocks are still on the Board’s hands.
There exist compulsory purchase provisions—by a
resolution of both Houses —but the Board knows so
well that the Upper House won’t “ resolve ” that it
hasn’t attempted to use this alleged power.
Minister Murray practically admitted the failure
of the system —“Under it no provision is made
for the intelligent and industrious settler of small
means ; . . . the scheme merely enables a few
private owners to dispose of their estates to a sub-
stantial purchaser at remarkably good prices. ’ But
there is no promise of any move in the direction
of real settlement, a necessary predecessor of
which must be a cumulative land-tax.

With the object of assisting persons whom
most of them termed “adulterers”, the aitchless
Tory section of the Vic. Leg. Council succeeded in
carrying a motion to call business men to give
expert evidence regarding the gross injustice of
the Pure Food Bill, which aimed at crippling the
flourishing poisoning industry. It fell in badly.
All the witnesses, including the great F. T.
Derham, refused to support the Council’s views.
They announced that the principles of the Bill
were good and that they regarded poisoning the
public as rather reprehensible than otherwise. One
member has argued that all jam was made largely
from pumpkins and rightly so; but a jam-maker
who was called seemed to almost regard the
pumpkins as a personal reflection, and demanded a
Pure F'ood Bill so that his pure and unquestion-
able jam might not have to compete against other
men’s pumpkins. As a matter of fact, only a very
dull lot of Rich Landlordswould haveexpected any
manufacturer to declare publicly against an Anti-
Adulteration Bill, and thereby pour suspicion over
himself like tar. The proceedings left the House
with a gloomy doubt that Derham is in the pajr of
the Trades Hall, and it went home breathing
heavily about the Six Hatters.
A S.M. Herald leader on the Home Rule
motion in the Representatives makes it appear
that Gladstone’s Home Rule Bills were rejected by
Parliament and by people, and most of the Yellow
Pup orators hold forth in the same absurd strain.
Gladstone’s Bill of 1886 was rejected by the House
of Commons on the second reading. Gladstone
went to the country on it and was defeated. But
on returning to power he introduced his second
Home Rule Bill in 1893. The second reading of
that Bill in the House of Commons wa,s carried
by a majority of 43, and the third reading by a
majority of 41. The Bill went to the House of
Lords, and was there thrown , out by 419 votes to
41, all the paralysed old peers being brought down
in bath chairs, and the deranged peers being
brought along by their keepers, and the newly-
dead peers who hadn’t been buried yet being
carried in by their executors to vote against Ireland’s
freedom. Soon afterwards Gladstone retired
from the post of Prime Minister, and Rosebery
succeeded. It was therefore the Lords, not the
Commons or the people, who destroyed the Home
Rule Bill with which Gladstone’s memory is
identified.

SOME INCONTROVERTIBLE TESTIMONY.
The following very much condensed remarks are taken at random from our Visitors’ Book at Annandale,
and will suffice to convince anyone as to the excellent methods of manufacture and the quality of the
article turned out:—
Hon. ALFRED DEAKIN, K. 0., M.P., Prime Minister. —
“ The pianos themselves contain many trophies of inven-
tive skill of a high order, and are finished in an artistic
style, which reflects every credit upon the firm and its
employees. lam not acquainted with any local industry
of higher class, more encouraging prospects, or which
better deserves the marked success it has won.”
Rt,. Hon. O. O. KINGSTON, P. 0., K. 0., then Minister
of Customs.—“ Very pleased with all I have seen.”
Hon. Sir WM. J. LYNE, Minister of Customs.—“ Can-
not speak too highly as to their excellent quality.”
Rt. Hon. Sir E. BARTON, P.C., K.C., G.0.M.C., Senior
Puisne Judge Federal High Court.—“ The most modern
appliances, well-paid skill and unremitting care.”
Hon. Sir JOHN SEE, K.C.M.G., ex-Premier of N.S.W.—
“ An establishment that reflects the greatest credit upon
the firm.”
Hon. WALTER JAMES, Agent-General for W.A. —
" The most convincing protectionist argument in the
Commonwealth. On my visit to Sydney I had no more
pleasant experience than that afforded by an inspection
of the works and factory. I was astonished to see the
class of work done.”
Hon. Sir ARTHUR RUTLEDGE, K.C.M.G., Q.— “An
industry rich in promise for the prosperity of New South
Wales. The perfect method of uniform extraction of the
sap from the timber without injuring the fibres will
enable the wood to resist all climatic influences. It is
the only perfect system I have met with in Australia.”
Hon. J. G. JENKINS, ex-Premier of S.A.—“Them’s my
sentiments, too.” ,
Hon. E. W. O’SULLIVAN, ex-Mmister of Works,
Kf.S.W.—“ The pianos are superier to any imported.”
Hon. T. WADDELL, ex-State Treasurer, N.S.W.—“A
great manufacturing enterprise.”
Hon. JOHN PERRY, ex-Minister for Public Instruction,
N.S. W. —“ More than surprised.”
Hon. JOHN KIDD, ex-Ministerof Mines and Agriculture,
N.S.W. —“ Greatly impressed with the magnitude of the
works.” ■ T „
Hon. Sir F. B. SUTTOR, ex-President Legislative Coun-
cil.— 11 Every success to those who have established'such
works.”
Hon. JAS. HAYES, N.S.W.—“ Superior to anything of
the kind I have ever seen.”
The late Senator ROBERT REID, President Chamber
Commerce, Melbourne. —“ The splendid patent iron
frames are a great success.”
His Eminence CARDINAL MORAN, Archbishop of
Svdney.— “ Had no idea that in the musical department
such perfection had been attained.”
Rev. RAINSFORD BAVIN, ex-president N.S. W. Confer-
ence Methodist Church.—“ A splendid proof of Australian
spirit, ability and enterprise.”
Hon. J. B. NASH, M.D., M.L.C., N.S.W.—“Musical
instruments of excellent quality and workmanship.”
Hon. A. W. MEEKS, M.L.A., N.S.W.—“ Surprised at
the magnitude of the undertaking."
Rev. WM. G. TAYLOR, Superintendent Central Metho-
dist Mission, Sydney.—“ Why import pianos when we
have amongst us so up-to-date an establishment?”
Rev. GEO. CAMPBELL, Chairman Congregational
Union of N.S.W., 1902. —“ A delightful surprise. Worthy
all commendation and encouragement.”
Rev. Dr. MARDEN, M.A., Principal Presbyterian
Ladies’ College, Croydon, N.S.W.—“ Thoroughness and
attention to detail throughout.”
Colonel ORLANDO H. BAKER, American Consul.—
“ Surprised to find such extensive and complete works in
Sydney.”
J. S. LARKE, Esq., Commissioner for Canada.—
“ Surprised at the efficient organisation of every depart-
ment.”
Rt. Hon. the ex-Lord Mayor of Sydney, Aid. THOMAS
HUGHES.—“Beyond all praise.”
F. B. FREEHILL, Esq., Consul for Spain, Sydney.—
“ Astonished and delighted to find in our midst an estab-
lishment affording such evidence of great enterprise.”
J. BARRE JOHNSTON, Esq., President Chamber of
Commerce, Sydney.—“ The expert knowledge displayed
is not surpassed in any part of the world.”
J. P. WRIGHT, Esq., J.P., President Chamber of
Manufactures of N.S.W.—“ Have never seen in Europe or
America such a combination for the production of pianos
of the highest class.”
A. W. SCOTT, Esq. (Messrs. Parsons Bros.), Sydney.—
“ Most up-to-date and complete ; an object lesson to all.”
EDWARD RILEY, Esq., President of Sydney Labor
Council.—“ Thoroughness and high-class workmanship
through every branch.”
WM. MARTIN, Esq., President Australian Natives’
Association, N.S.W.—“ Having seen large establishments
of the kind in other parts of the world, was much struck
with the complete system and organisation.”
GEO. T. CLARKE, Esq., President United Friendly
Societies’ Association, N.S.W.—“ First-class pianos, rich
in tone, and specially durable for our climate.”
Ex-ALDERMAN J. D. FITZGERALD, Sydney City Coun-
cil.—“ The firm turns out a ‘ native’ instrument which
will take its place in the world’s concert rooms.”
ALDERMAN T. H. BARLOW, Sydney City Count'd.—
“ I had little idea of the magnitude of the works.”
LEWIS DE GROEN, Esq., Conductor of Music to his
Excellency the Governor-General.—“ No question as to
high quality of materials, and extreme care shown
throughout.”
PROFESSOR ENNIS, Mus. D. (London), of the Ade-
laide Conservatorium of Music.—“ The tone of the instru-
ments I have seen is excellent, possessing no wiry or
metallic quality.”
Hon. J. HUME COOK, M.H.R., who was presented
with a Beale Piano by his colleagues on the occasion of
his marriage.—“ One could not wish for a better instru-
ment. After some 14 months’ wear it is still in perfect
tune, and its tone as rich and mellow as the most fas-
tidious could desire. All are unanimous in its praise."

The Beale=Vader Patent
Steel Tuning System.

rom O

•Cftcwt x
Showing SOLID IRON ERAME, with steel
screws and tuning pins. A glance will tell you
that this construction will endure for generations.
The pins are lubricated, and so can always be
turned with delicacy. Patented throughout the
world. The pins are firm, cannot move, nor thus
five out false vibrations, as in the old system.
The Tuning System
of Imported Pianos.
Pins driven into holes in a WOODEN PEANK
which sooner or later must give way. In a space
of 1J square feet, 216 pins, 1J inch by \ inch,
are driven. Timber cannot resist such honeys
combing under a strain of 15 tons. The holes
become oval, as shown above, by the crushing of
the wood fibres.
Improvements in Piano Woodwork.
n r
Retire I
B c
igure u..
figure 3.
IMPROVEMENTS IN PIANO DOORS.—Patents taken out in Australia by BEALE & CO., Ltd. The doors of
imported pianos from any countrj' are liable to shrink and warp, especially the key-board fall or cover. We avoid
these misfortunes and increase the streng th thus :
Fig. I — A is the centre or core of door, basswood.
B, Bl.—Outer and inner cross-layers of bass-
wood or walnut.
O, 01. —Outer and inner veneer of walnut
again crossed.
I).—Outer frame-piece of American redwood.
Dl.—Ornamental moulding enclosing the panels.
E. —Binding piece of Australian cedar.
F. Walnut veneer on outer face or front frame.
FI. —Strengthening cross-layer of white wood.
By means of our construction, consisting: of three or more internal layers of timber and two externa', irames all
glued together under pressure, a door is obtained that cannot under any circumstances warp or twist, no matter to
what climatic conditions it may be subjected. Figure 3 represents a section of part of the upper lid, and Figure 2of
the fall or key-board lid, constructed on the same principle. It will be seen that the various jointed plies of timber
with the fibres opposed tend to secure straightness in the wood, and thus prevent warping. It is known as the com-
pensatory princ'ple. All our mouldings are tongued on, as shown in Figure %. whicn German makers do not follow,
hence the mouldings of imported pianos fall or are easily knocked off. You cannot obtain Pianos upon this systefn
from any other makers. The advantage to the buyer and user is most manifest.

HEALTH
VITALITY
KUTNOW’S POWDER
APPETITE
KUTNOW’S POWDER is one of the most beneficial remedies for the improvement of health and the prevention of disease ; in fact, it is the one remedy
which enjoys the support and approval of the general body of medical practitioners. The reason for the recognition of its value by such distinguished
physicians as PROF. LAWSON TAIT, M.D., SIR MORELL MACKENZIE, M.D., SIR SPENCER WELLS, M.D., and SIR ANDREW CLARK,
M.D., is not difficult of comprehension. Kutnow’s Powder is appreciated by doctors because they know that it contains nothing which is injurious, no
mercurial poison, nothing painful or drastic, and nothing but what is beneficial. The most delicate person can take it, even very young children and
invalids find it agreeable ; moreover, its action is so gentle and soothing that insensibly to the patient it efficiently does its work. It clears and flushes the
entire system of all noxious debris and poisonous liquids, which action is invaluable to both sick and well. It gradually changes certain morbid conditions
of the system, and establishes healthy tissue, which alone is able to bring about a sound constitution. It stimulates the vital processes to renewed
activity, and enables the kidneys and bowels to remove waste matter which ought to be eliminated, one of the most important of which is URIC ACID.
In this way Kutnow’s Powder alters, corrects and purifies the fluids, gives tone to, and re-establishes the healthy function of, all the digestive and other
organs of the body. It cleanses and lubricates the human machinery. Kutnow’s Powder is an alterative which possesses tonic, laxative, stimulant,
and diuretic properties. It is a mild aperient which flushes the whole alimentary canal. It is a stimulant which has the power of increasing the vital
activity of the body. It is a cholagogue which never fails to make the liver act properly. Its alkaline action neutralises excessive acidity in the stomach,
and bowels. It is an antiseptic which prevents putrefaction. These are the qualifications of Kutnow’s Powder, and, needless to say, they never fail
to assist in the maintenance and re-establishment of Health. ( 1 •

Mississippi (U.S.A.) _ State law makes
inter-marriage between whites and blacks a penal
offence. Recently, at the town of Magnolia, a
white woman was sentenced to ten years im-
prisonment for marrying a negro, and the Court
regretted that it could not make the sentence
heavier.
The “Australian Women’s Liberal Union ”
has passed a resolution to the effect that Asiatics
should be admitted to Australia “ under adequate
restrictions.” In South Africa “ adequate restric-
tions ” have proved to be chiefly flogging. Is this
what the “Australian Women’s Liberal Union”
means? And if so, will it take the responsibility
of its own theories ? And supposing it takes tile
responsibility, does it think that the spectacle of
a fashionably-dressed lady, with a picture hat on
her head and a cat-of-nine-tails flying loose in her
jewelled hand as she flogged the naked yellow
backbone of a screeching Chinaman, woul be a
nice thing for this country to look upon ?
The N.S.W. Temperance Alliance seems
to be another political alias of the Carruthers-
Stinson push. It professes to be a non-party
organisation, and collects funds from Cold Tea
folk of all parties, but it pays ex-Salvationist
Bruntnell a copious salary to go round “ organ-
ising,” and as far as can be seen, he organises
chiefly to keep Carruthers in his job. At last
election Arthur Griffith was a candidate, and he
was also a subscribing member of the blessed
Alliance. Nevertheless the Alliance spent good
teetotal money on circularising the electors to
vote against Griffith and for another man. The
other wasn’t a better Alliance man than Griffith,
but he was a Joe-ite and Griffith wasn’t.
As showing the difference between Aus-
tralia’s treatment of its ornamental Governor-
General (who is allowed to get all his goods in
duty free), and the U.S.A.’s treatment of its
working President (both get the same screw), the
YanlceeUustoms authorities have decided that full
duty will have to be paid on all the presents
(mostly dutiable goods) received by Miss Alice
Roosevelt during her recent tour. These are
valued at about £20,000, and the duty payable by
the President is computed at about £12,000. Ihe
trip was a semi-diplomatic one, too !
Apropos “the great revival in trade" in
S’Africa, which, according to the Tory bapers,
was to follow on the heels of peace and the intro-
duction of “ intelligent Coolie labor at the mines.
Thus a S.M. Herald Capetown correspondent
Unless a sharp revival of trade takes place before the
end of the year, next January must inevitably witness a
mercantile cataclysm in Capetown. As for the export
trade, it has almost ceased to be. The war knocked the
sheep-farmer on the head, and one does not see a bale of
wool in a week’s march in Capetown. A little argol, a
few ostrich feathers, a paltry lading of fruit and wine,
represent the exports of Capetown Port Elizabeth and
East London still ship some wool and hides, but only a
fraction of what they handled 10 or 20 years ago. The
wool-washing industry, which used to he represented by
some dozen establishments, employing many hundreds of
natives at Uitenage has gone. Of other colonial industries,
the jam and confectionery manufacturers are about the only
ones that mav be said to be keeping their end up. Business
on the Capetown Stock Exchange is confined to petti-
fogging deals, contemptuously termed “ ticky-snatching
bv Johannesburg brokers, and more money changes hands
at Tattersall’s Rooms on the night before a Kenilworth
meeting than in a week’s transactions on the Stock Ex-
change. Most of the Capetown hotels are empty half the
time, and, though the theatre and music-hall continue to
keep their doors open, nobody can explain how.

The High Court is to be appealed to on a ques-
tion of fly-paper. A thriving business has been built up
by a Yankee firm which supplies a means of catching the
Australian fly. The device consists of paper smeared
with a sweet gummy mixture which detains the fly by
main force, and the Customs department is trying to
collect duty on the paper. The Yankee firm claims that
the stuff is an “ insecticide,” on the ground that it kills
mes, which are insects, and insecticide comes in under a
lower duty than paper. Some humorous cuss asks
whether diamonds could come in cheaply as insecticide
supposing they were fastened on paper and the fly
damaged himself by sitting down on the sharp edge of
xj- e u l noor * The Bulletin doesn’t know, but if the
High Court brushed a fty off its wig—supposing it wore
one ~ an d killed the fowl in the process, the High Court
would clearly be insecticide.

“ Tim ”: My friend, the German count, went out
to the Kichmond pony races the other week, and came
home minus his hat and coat, and generally looking as if
he had been soaked over night, then ironed out with a
steam roller. Said he : “ Himmel! I onderstand not
you Australian vays. I go out to ze Gup to back ze dead
pird. Ach ! you vas queer beeble. He vas dead every
tune, and I gets vat you gall heem? Ah, yes ! ze stone
broke. Zen ze man vith ze dree gards gomes up. 1 know
heem. ‘Here you are, baron/ he say, ‘live bob to a
bound ze vinner.’ He zinks he vas glever, so I take out
ze half zoverigu vith the zixpence rubbed out and say,
done. ‘ Ildil a zov to two bound?’ he ask. ‘No, five
bob change, mein frient/ I reply, and he gif me dwo half
growns. Of course I lift ze wrong gard, but no matter. I
kick myself vith delight, zen I go off to blunge and regover.
Ze bookmaker write ze ticket, zen tear it up, and hand
back my money and say, ‘ Gottam ! you make number*
vour. Go to ’ell! Clear out!’ ‘ Sare! ’ I reply
enraged, ‘ soch gonduct is onpardonable,’ and I raise my
hat and gif heem my gard, and tell heem that I would be
glad to meet his frient zat he should send. Zen Igo
?nd drink ze lager, and ven I pay ze vaiter gall oudt,
.Bolice . Bolice ! ’ Donner and blitzen ! I gomprehend
zen and I run. Ze growd schream, ‘ Velsher !—Velsher ! ’
and I get killed half and lose my watch and chain before
I reach ze gates ; but I quickly drop zat ozzer half-grown
into ze river, zen ze zavages drop me in. Gott in himmel !
I know not ze vord bad enough for you Australians.”

When Johnagiotis Smitliopolous is not engaged
in selling stale fish and fruit, and working his employees
19 hours a day, he is usually to be found coaxing a more
or less honest penny from the soldiers of any nation that
happens to be at war. Reuter’s correspondent in Man-
churia reports that one of the earliest indications that
there was to be trouble in that part of the world was
“the presence of the übiquitous Greek, that keenest of
traders, and persistent gleaner in the wake of armies in
the field. 1 recognised Greeks whose figures had been
familiar to the British during the Boer war.”

Adelaide cabinet makers complain of a new trick
by the Heathen Chinee. The stuff is not sold in the usual
manner, but houses are furnished with it, and
clearing sales of same held, the goods being in some cases
sold as those made by a manufacturer of world-wide
lame.

A French resident of the New Hebrides, in
need of a horse and cart for general work on his young
plantation, sent along the following' order to a well-known
Sydney firm:—“l command you to send to me with
much quickness and despatch one woman horse, also a
chariot. I await anxiously. Yours greatfully, .”
“Burrawong”: in an Australian town two
tradesmen went into bankruptcy. Brown’s estate gave a
dividend of 3fd. in the £, whilst White’s little quota was
18s. 7d. for every £. Some time after, both started busi-
ness again, and gave the commercial travellers their
orders. Brown’s was executed on “ tick,” but White was
refused credit. White wanted to know why. “ Well,”
said the commercial, “ the firm thought that when Brown
went up he kept a bit up his s.eeve, whereas you paid up
all you had. The boss doesn’t like dealing with a man
who’s paid away all his money.”

“ What with the warehouses, Ohow competi-
tion, tick, coupons and Christmas boxes, the game’s not
fair,” said the grocer. “Some of the customers ’ud beat
a land-agent. They run up a bill, and when they have
the ‘ready’ spend it and make their names good some-
where else. If they do pay for half o’ butter they expect
a few ounces over, and reckon you’re ‘mingy’ if you
don’t chuck in a few for the baby. But last
Saturday was the ‘ stretcher. ’ She was only a nipper,
and came for change of a bob, and when I gave it seemed
a bit slow at going, ‘Anything else 1 can do?’I says,
pleasant-like, and when sue Says, ‘ Please, the other
grocer always guves liis cublumevts lollies on Saturday,’
well, only that the missus fanned me with a wasiun’-
board I believe I’d ’a’ fainted.”

“Tim”: Last Cup year I clerked for a bookie
at Flemington, and was nearly driven mad by the
number oi tickets presented for payment by women
bacKtrs. After almost every race a nightmare of frenzied
skirt and weird head gear would come rushing up and
hand in a squashed ticket on some hopelessly beaten
noddy that was still finishing. The first customer nearly
d.opped dead With shock on learning that she was on the
wrong horse, but quickly recovered, and cleared for
action as il she had a contract on to bump the earth out
of its orbit. We paid. After the Cup four of these
errat.c she-ttends struck us all in a heap. On it being
pointed out that their tickets belonged to other book-
makers, two did a quiet weep, and declared broken-
lieartediy that they were positive we had booked their
bees, anu that we had swindled them out of their money.
The others hysterically denounced us for several minutes.
Kvery bet on each winner was collected, and these women
couldn’t have mixed the tickets. Either they had become
confused by the babble, and asked for the wrong horse, or
the excitement of a close finish had so upset them that
they could imagine anything. Inquired afterwards from
other clerks, and learned that their experiences were the
same, also that one crazy creature had been raising
feheoi all along the line by endeavoring to obtain payment
of a t.cket that had been issued by a welsher two years
ago !

“Yarrum”: Other afternoon, near a lonely
spot called the Devil’s Eibow, I met a commercial travel-
ler making for Newcastle, and got into conversation. He
wore a scared expression under his panama, and I won-
, dered what it was all about. After a bit he said : “ Must
be pushin’ on, as I’ve been collecting, and have money
about me.” lie wanted to reach town before dark.
Asked him about how much he usually carried. “ Well,”
said he, after much hes.tation, “ I have one seventeen-and-
six on me now', but 1 have had as much as £10.” Told
him he should be armed, and he produced a revolver
loaded in every chamber. “ You see,” he remarked, “if
I w ere robbed, and didn’t put up a fight, the police would
be round to tiie missus wantin’ to know just how we
stood, and what money we owed. Then the newspapers
would have an account of an 'alleged stickin’ up.’ No
alleged biz for me ! If a man bails me up I’m going to
hurt him so that he’ll not be hard to find.” Imagine a
man hawking a gun to guard thirty-seven and six! It’s
an insult to the district !
* * * *
By using barium as an adulterant hides can be
increased largely in weight (and they are sold by weight).
But the leather is inferior, for it sweats when worn, will
not easily polish, and is less durable than when un-
adulterated. The Queensland Government thought to
legislate against the evil, but if it made its ow'n tanners
honest by law, there is nothing to stop the southern
manufacturer from sending in boots made from barium-
laden leather. The Queensland Government might order
confiscation, but who would pay for the seized goods? So
the matter, with a copy of the Bill which Morgan had
prepared, has been sent on to Deakin, who would do good
work by getting a measure through the Federal House.
The evil of this adulteration travels, of course, where the
leather goes, and the British buyer is now so chary of the
Australian article that he demands a certificate before he
will buy.
* * * *
An Australian quack now practising claims “ to
possess the X-rays in his eyes” The cure of cancer ai.d
h.vdatids is his strong point. He fixes his eye on tl.e
sufferer, and quickly diagnoses the case. Then he makes
a clutch at the patient in the region where the trouble is
supposed to be located. The patient suffers no pain, but
it all passes into the operator’s body. This tommy-rot is
believed by “intelligent” people, who pay the qua,k
generously for medicine which is to conclude the cure
that his X-eye started.

Benqer’s r'ooD is sold in Tins 3
by Chemists, &c. everywhere. J
G. R. Sims tells his Hinglish readers
what’s the matter with them :—
It is this hide-bound conservatism. We declaim against
the Germans and Americans for taking our trade and for
becoming- every day more prosperous while we sink
deeper into the slough of adversity, but we refuse to
accept the policy by which our rivals have achieved
success. There can be but one climax to our crass
stupidity. We shall perish in our pride.
The Pride of Race is pretty well gone now that
Hoggenheimer runs the national show. The
Pride of Wealth is going after it. But the Pride
of Stupidity retains its ancient vigor.
This is about a parson’s half-crown. Carl
Kayser called the other day to see Rev. J.
Spooner, of Brisbane, and arrange his wedding.
After the particulars were given, Carl said a local
solicitor had some cash of his in hand, but the
legal person was out of town, and meantime his
landlord was pushing him for his night’s board.
The parson, no doubt having the marriage fee in
view, nearly fell over himself to advance 2s. Gd.,
Carl promising to turn up with the bride and the
cash next day at 4 o’clock. Next day 4.15 saw a
parson at the police station looking for a German
and one-eighth of a “quid.” Carl was found and
brought before the court, but he pleaded that the
money was a loan, and that borrowing 2s. 6d. was
not obtaining cash under false pretences. He
swaggered out of court a dismissed man, and the
parson followed half-crownless. Yet he should
have had something said to him for bringing into
disrepute an honorable social proceeding which
leads to the much-needed birth-rate.

To You.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HESITANT yet striving
To grasp this prize unwon,
? I, Icarus, came driving
Too near, too near the sun ;
The sun of gold locks twining,
The flame of eyes a-fire,
The glow of lips declining
Yet trembling with desire.
Love’s sirens sit a-singing.
Love’s wreckers light their piles, •
And men like moths go winging
Around the lamp of smiles.
Now hopeful, now despairing,
Fearful, now fain to prove—
How had I found such daring
Had I not found such love ?
God’s cleap blue sky above you,
God’s clean earth at your feet,
And all good men to love you—
How dared I love you, sweet ?
And yet —my fond heart fancies
You keep for none but me
Those burning, wishing glances
That no man else shall see.
The wide room rings with laughter.
I, silent, stand afar
And watch you, looking after*
As moths may watch a star 5
And When the guests have freed you
I know that we shall meet
Where the soft night-winds need you,
And I so need you, sweet I
Sweeps up from the dim garden
The scent of lilac flower ;
This is the outlaw’s pardon,
And this the rebel’s hour.
One touch of warm hearts meeting,
One whisper sweet with fears —
Oh ! That mad moment’s greeting
Is worth life’s empty years !
Why have you picked this pebble,
When all your faith gives gold ?
Your hand comes, a white rebel,
My rebel hand to hold !
But I’ll not ask the reason,
Dear love ; enough to know
Our hearts have their guest season
When all your dull guests go !
U.S.A.
WILL H. OGILYIE.
A Song of the Swamps.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I SIT on the boundary fence that divides
McThree’s cattle run from McHour’s, and
with a pencil that smells of smoke, on paper
cribbed from the book-keeper’s office, I write a
song of the swamps, that in this land of unexpected-
ness are here, there, and on either hand, gaping
scars, or beds of brittle, whispering reeds in the
long drought, but now, since the rains have cpme,
and autumn is upon us, stains of damp verdant
green, and rifts of gleaming water.
Miles away, beyond the hectic green of the
plains, run the zig-zag hills of blue, little Ghains of
irregular heights, and up almost to their feet—a
dark shadow on the horizon —creeps the quaking
swamp. It is a dreary marshland, covered with
wild iris and flat rapier-pointed rushes that shake
and quiver in every breath of wind. I have seen
it in a spring-time, many years ago, blossoming in
wanton glory, truly “ a cloth of gold,” pure gold—
not the dross of cultivated flowers, but color spun
from the sun. Then a wind, slipping suddenly in
from nowhere, would bend back the gleaming
reeds and heavy-flowering irises till the whole
marshland quivered, and a sea of gold seemed to
surge and break tempestuously round the feet of
the hills. But that was long ago, and for many
springs the irises have not blossomed. Before me
lies the black swamp, through which McThree’s
fences run in a drunken line, and from which great
gums, with drought-seared limbs and flood-scarred
boles, crouch against the skies. A road —a cattle
track—twists through the swamp like a hesitating
snake, turning here and doubling there to escape
the treacherous gilgis and yawning holes that
honeycomb the ground.
Once I saw a great flood rise in it, and shoulder
high among the gums. The rains had come sud-
denly after a drought of two summers, and in a
night a great wall of tawny water racing from the
hills had lifted the river and creeks bank high
with eddying currents and masses of yellow foam.
For miles the river could be heard mutilating its
banks, and all night long came the fret of the
flood through the sodden branches of trees that
had fallen athwart the creek. Just before dawn,
when the foam had piled high behind the drifting
logs, and the stream, like a maddened dog, was
tearing at its sides, a tiny trickle of water slid
cautiously through the broken bank, and, like a
dirty brown snake, wormed out towards the swamp,
pattering in the dry leaves like all the mice in
creation skurrying on a bark roof. Behind, the
creek widened and broadened to a lake, and still
the little brown snake wriggled onward through
the trees. Have you ever heard the whisper of
miles of rising water yawning through millions
and millions of dead leaves ? It is the same sound
that the thirsty tide makes crawling on beaches of
dry sand. Higher and higher rose tne flood with
its many voices—the trampling of water round the
boles of trees ; the roar as it leaped suddenly upon a
barren gilgi, the purr of settling earth, and the
frightened crickets shrilling from the higher
branches in deafening chorus. Before night a
sullen sheet of water swept from east to west, and
as the clouds broke at sunset, bursting into flames
of red, the gaunt black gums for miles waded in a
sea of rippling blood. But now there are only
grey, leprous scars to show where the water
pillaged long 8 go.
Once, hunting for emu eggs at the back of the
run, in a valley between two hills, I rode suddenly
from a hillside path of moss upon the dead
swamp. It was high noon I remember, and on
either side the hills swam up into a hot blue sky,
and the red mountain creepers leaped daringly
from rock to rock ; but in between lay desolation
itself. A shrunken depression, the flats covered
with grey sedge, and the centre a dreary sheet of
greyer water, sodden as wool, and blotted
with white uncanny reeds. There was never
a note of bird, and never a hint of flower.
Every tree that grew about it was dead, and
stark, and silvery with leprosy, and one great
gum, riven by a thunderbolt ages back,
stretched black, menacing hands to either hill.
A withered scar on the valley s breast, 1 left it,
riding into the sunset. , ,
But it is the little green flats that lie below the
homestead I love, falling in cascades of verdure
to the river. So green and lush they are now,
and clamorous with birds. The blue Sof water
that winds in and out of the vivid reeds is white
and brown and black with birds, and all day long
comes the skurry of their wings. White cranes
step gingerly through the tender rushes and rifts
of gleaming water that the dawns flush with gold
and the sunset with ripples of crimson—little
marshes that the summer stamps out with burning
feet, but which form again, if only for a brief life,
after the first early rain 3. My pencil jolts
jerkily—it is almost done, and already the sUti is
below the gums, and the long, long shadows stalk
like walking towers across the plain, A breeze
blows strong from the black swamp—pungent
with gum leaves ; a flock of white cookatoes flies
overhead, shrieking and screeching homewards.
and—
CARO.
The Man in the Beef.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Conducting a byewfei*y id the Gulf
Country is more interesting thaii pearlirig
Or scraping the sea bottom for shell. A
Eanaka May own a fleet of liiggers, blit it takes a
white man to build a malt-room and a vat. The
Tropic Brewery was a galvanized iron building
with a 10,003- gallon tub in the centre. A navi-
gable inlet and a small pier were its principal
assets, and most of the beer went down to the sea
in ships.
The Japanese sheller is not a great drinker, but
the sight of a perpendicular drink has been known
to interest him seriously.
Years ago the Tropic Brewery was the centre
of a great thirst. The captains of luggers and
other small craft ran their dingies alongside the
pier and ordered hogsheads between tides. The
thirst of a sailor who has been wiping salt off his
face for three months is not to be measured by
pints. A man with a mere two-pint thirst was
looked upon up there in those days as a human
derelict drifting to a bad end. But the hard-
voiced mariner with a camel in his throat was
publicly esteemed; he could hit a policeman
without getting locked up, or tread on a dog
without being abused.
The Tropic Brewery was managed by a German
named Schultz. Lighters and cargo tramps
loaded from his jetty, and it looked at one time
as though his beer would knock the Queensland
rum trade off its feet.
Schultz had a hard time with the Gulf battlers.
Each morning disclosed two or three beachcombers
lying in helpless attitudes about the brewery floor.
Locks and bars failed to keep them out; they
crawled in under the iron roof thin and weary,
but morning found them stout and full of song,
Schultz said that a Gulf dead-beat would climb
over the rim of a volcano to be in at the death of
a homeless beer.
Wong Ghat, the Chinese butcher, brought a
young crocodile from one of the back creeks and
sold it to the German. Schultz put his dog in a
wire cage and allowed the reptile free run of the
brewery after dark. The brute seemed to like its
new quarters, and, to the German s relief, it
yapped and spluttered horribly the first time its
snout dropped accidentally into 3ft. of beer.
“My goot mans ” —he looked hard at the snort-
ing croc.—“you keep outside de peer in future.
After it had caught its first goat and cleared out
the dogs from the butcher s blood-hole it became
thoroughly domesticated.
The beachcombers now gave the brewery a wide
berth after dark, A committee of goats from the
township collected on the sand hill outside the
brewery and watched the crocodile basking in the
hot mud of the creek-bed. They finally decided
that it was a land-agent, and fled.
Schultz denied that the crocodile used to climb
round the vat platform and lie in the beer.
On the first Sunday after the opening of the
brewery every church in the township was empty.
The Rev. Father Murdoch came down upon the
beerworks with a kindling eye, and delivered his
sermon from the top of three casks.
One sweltering night Schultz invited a number
of German sailors to spend the evening in the
cool shadow of the big vat. The brewery doors
were locked, while the sunburnt Teutons sat on
tubs and sang songs of the Fatherland. Schultz
begged them to “ shake things oop.” He rolled a
cask of drink into position, and urged them to
make noises in their throats. One big-chested
sailor with a yellow beard ran round the big vat
singing a drinking song. The manager cheered,
while the others drenched him with beer drops to
keep him cool. Climbing suddenly to an overhead
beam, Yellowbeard cast aside his clothes and
poised himself stiffly over the 10,000 gallon vat.
“ Ach Gott! you must not! shouted the
terrified manager. “De peer vas no goot after-
vards. It liaf been coolin’ for Chreestmas veek.
Ooom down, Carl Schmidt.” , ~ ,
Carl waved his arms, drew a long breath and
dived into the vat. Rollers of froth heaved over
the rim of the giant tub ; the beer gurgled m its
agony. Then a large muffled silence spread itself
over the beer. Slowly, very slowly, Carl Sclnnidt
rose somewhere under the barm ; it heaved above
him like a snowtield. With beer-drenched hair and
eyes he struggled desperately to reach the side ot
the vat, but the deep froth blinded and choked him.
It took a 12ft. ladle to reach him, and when they
hauled him out he squelched like a half-drowned
lion. He remained semi-unconscious for halt-an-
hour, and when he recovered they gave him more
beer. Nine of them had to surround the manager
to keep him from strangling their betr-washed
countryman. , , £ .
No one knew definitely what becnme of that
10,000-gallon brew. A month or two later the
people further north admitted that Schultz s beer
was imoroving—it had acquired a fine German
Carl Schmidt never quite shook off the effects of
his bath. He returned to Frankfort, and was
appointed manager of a wurst factory, but his
strange habit of diving into the sausage mash
brought about his dismissal.

The Destroyers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE are the Arch-Destroyers ! white-
handed, soft and fair,
Who kneel at the shrine of Fashion,
whose watchword is “Never Spare ! ”
We are the Arch-Destroyers! timid-souled, frail,
and slim,
Who li ive rava; 1 earth’s war-worn borders to
their faintest, furthermost rim.
Only the great dumb sorrow that never was
clothed in words;
Only the unwrit anguish of an army of bright-
winged birds,
Measures our meed of triumph—echoes it every-
where —
We are the Arch-Destroyers whose watchword is
“ Never Spare 1 ”
We are the Arch-Destroyers ! for us have the
ships gone forth
And wrestled with dark and danger and the frost
fiend of the North !
We are the Arch-Destroyers ! for us the ice
gleams red
Where, on the Arctic ice-floes, the seal calf mourns
her dead.
We, who have never shivered ’neath the shreds of
a wretched clout,
Send for a splendid caprice our toiling bond-
slaves out!
And never a sea so sullen, and never a sky so
gray,
That they flinch from the face of danger and
nerve them to disobey.
We are the Arch-Destroyers ! woes of the world
attest !
For us are the reeking altars of sacrifice daily
drest !
From the poles to the burnt equator our minions
have riven and torn
From the shuddering brute creation the glories
that we have worn.
The jewels that blazon bravely on bosom and
haughty head
Flash forth with a baleful lustre grim messages
from the dead 1
The gold that is girt about us in bracelet and
brooch and stud
Is heavy with guilt of murder—is tarnished with
mire and mud.
We are the Arch-Destroyers !—timid-souled, soft,
and fair—
Queens of the world of Fashion, whose queen-
doms are everywhere.
Quick !—to your posts, ye liallions! wielders of
fire and steel,
Ours is the right pre-emptive to harry, and not to
heal!
There is treasure yet for the winning,-where the
bones of the dead men lie !
There is treasure yet for the buying, no ransom
but blood will buy !
Spare not ! —an ’ye would serve us. Serve us not,
an’ ye dare !
For we are the Arch-Destroyers, whose watch-
word is “Never Spare.’.’
VIC.
M. E. J. PITT.
Young Herb.’s Philosophy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ - th’ ole man got th’ word
w y about th’ row at school, how did
he perform ! My oath, didn’t he do
a tumble,” remarked young Herb, in continuation
of a deed of.derring-do, which he had just related
to the frequenters of Bill Murphy’s hairdressing
.emporium. “ I knoo he w.ouldn’t belt me in front
o’ all the chaps at the school, but I got home
quiek-an-lively, an’ I thinks ter meself, ‘This is
no place fer me.’ ,
“So I busts open me aunt’s drawer and gets
out me Savin’s Bank book that she kept ter
instil habits o’ thrift inter me. , An’ off
I skips an’ lifts the lot—two poun’ eight
an’ fourpence. I made fer the station, I
promise yer. Shrubb an’ Duffey ’d ha’ bin lef’
standin’. I screws along the time-table ter see
what train goes first, but there wasn’t anything
ter suit me style o’ beauty till I strikes Gisborne.
Arty Smith, a feller that used ter go ter school an’
lef’ last Chris’mas, lives at Gisborne ; so I ups ter
the ticket office and slung down me four an’ a kick
in style. ‘Here’ I says ter the feller behin’—
‘ here,’ I says, y’ know the way, jest ’sif I was
Rupert Clarke, ‘gimme a secon’ single ter
Gisborne.’ Then I did the Julius Knight act up
an’ down tli’ platform. I promise yer there
weren’t no flies on me then. I felt I looked the
real thing every time. But hones’, I ivas scared
the old mun’d reveal his pleasin’ personality every
minute, an’, when th’ train went off, I wasn’t a
bit glad.
“ I got ter Gisborne all right, an’ strolled outer
the station ’sif I knoo the place well. I didn’t
like ter stick up anyone ter fin’ out where Arty
lived for fear they’d sniff. I was jest goin’ ter
once, but I looked up an’ saw a trooper watchin’
me, so I jest walked up ter one o’ the hotels an’
put on me'lordly air again. * ‘ I’m stayin’ here
ter-night,- missus” I says ter th’ ole lady. She looks
at me a bit hard, an’ I tell yer I felt a nasty one on
th’ solar plexus. But I took it like a little man,
an’ she never saw how it hurt. ‘ Is anyone with
yer?’ she asked. ‘No,’ I says, in a casual sorter
way, ‘ I ain’t married.’ Th’ ole lady, she laughed.
‘I didn’t mean that,’ she says. ‘Then what did
yer mean ? ’ I says, with a fierce sorter look. ‘ Oh,
nothin’,’ she says, ‘but yer look so young. Yer
don’t look more’n sixteen.’ ‘Go on,’ I says,
laughin’ ’sif it was ch’ best I ever heard. ‘ That’s
th’ worst o’ lookin’ a lot younger than yer are.
Sixteen,’ I says, suddenly growin’ sad, ‘I wished
I was. ’ ‘ An’ where’s yer luggage ? ’ she says., ‘ I
never carry luggage,’ I says. ‘ I’m travellin’ fer
me health.’
“T kidded meself I’d got the ole party ori toast,
b it I foun’ out aftir, she’d tumbled f rom the start.
Yer c m t beat the deffeit o women. \\ e.l. b.rneoy
I whips out inter the billiard-room an there s a
push o’ sports playin’. Sol Marks was standin at
the door an’ as soon as he spotted me, he says,
‘Hullo; you’re ole Winter’s son, am t jei .
near dropped. I never knoo Sol knoo me, but I
saw it was no use tellin’ a tale, so I owns up an
turns meself inside out for him. lhats aw
right,’ says Sol, ‘ I won’t put the game away. By
the way,’ he says, ‘ you’ve bin among horses a. bit.
Can yer ride ? ’ ‘ Can I ride ? I says why I
on’y get off a horse now an’ then to go ter bed.
“Well, Sol thought for a minute. Then he
says, ‘Well,’ he says, ‘there’s races on here this
afternoon. If yer like ter stand in with mean
foller me instructions, there might be a bit m it
for both o’ us.’ ‘Righto,’ I says, an without a
word of a lie there I was registered that afternoon
with the racin’ club as young Grogan, a Sydney
jockey. , , c
“ Sol went roun’ among th’ owners an got a tew
o’ them ter listen ter reason. I didn t feel a bit
proud when I come out in a pink silk jacket an
tartan cap. It was aw right I promise yer.
There was a fairish crowd at the races, an
after he’d buzzed roun’ a bit, Sol comes
up an’ says, ‘ Yer favorite for this, an
yer got ter go down. He s supposed ter
be a prime finisher, so yer got ter fall back in the
first mile.’ ‘Go on,’ I says, ‘ lose the race? i
thought I had ter win, an’ I jest put two quid on
him.’ ‘Never min’,’ says Sol, ‘ I’ll give yer them
back an’ two more when yer lose.’ This was jest
what 1 was after. Course I’d never backed him,
but I thought it was a good line ter keep Sol in
front. Yer never know, yer know. But Sol was
an alright describer. 1 kep’ the horse well back
on the far side, got him pocketed at the corner,
an’ got out jest in time ter make a run.
Blow me if I didn’t think he was goin’
ter win, after all. The crowd yelled like
hell, an’ Sol was scared to death that I’d
turned him down. He had a white face when he
came up after. ‘My Gawd,’ he says, ‘yer cut
that too close for my health. Another couple o
yards an’ I’d ha’ bin goin’ faster than th’ horse.’
I waves pie hand at him. ‘Donch’er think I
know how ter ride? ’ Isays. ‘ That’s judgment,’ I
says. He weighs out the four quid. ‘Well,’ he
says, ‘ don’t cut it so fine again, or yer’ll have me
droppin’ dead o’ heart disease.’ The owner o’ the
horse he’d backed him for a lot, an’ he gives me
ten bob for the mount, an’ shook hands with me.
‘Yer did well, me boy,’ he says, ‘ I never thought
he had that finish in him the way he went at the
“ There I was with four poun’ ten awready. I
was gettin’ on, I tell yer. Sol gives me the word
that we have ter win the nex’ race. The horse
has bin run stiff for months, an’ the on’y one to
be fright’ned of was a big bay. I saw the way
the bay could shift as we went out, so I edges up
ter the boy on him —little chubby-faced feller
about thirteen he was. ‘I say, sonny,’ I says,
‘ I’m gointer win this race, an’ don’t yer forget it.
Yer a nice-lookin’ little boy, an’ yer love yer
mother, an’ I wouldn’t like ter see anything
happen ter yer. But,’ I says, fierce as could
be, ‘if yer try ter beat me, I’ll break yer
bloomin’ neck sure’s yer born.’ The little
boy turned pale, an’ neirly toppled outer his
saddle, an’ soon after the start I was able to cut
close ter him so’s ter make him think I meant ter
murder him. That settled the little boy. I got
home with a foot ter spare because they didn’t
want the weight piled on in other races. But the
little boy was nowhere, an’ th’ stewards made him
st md down the rest o’ the meeting. I made ten
quid outer that eVent for I bit on meself.
“ Then come the on’y other race I could start
in, an’ Sol tells me I’ve got ter go dead again. I
starts thinking ter meself that I won’t always have
Sol with me, an’ it wouldn’t be a bad thing
if I considered me own self-respect a bit.
I says ter meself that it’s wrong ter ride stiff, an’
I'll never do it agin. So I slips roun’the ring
quietly an’ plumps the whole o’ me winnin’s,
excep’ two quid, on me mount for this race. It
was a dead cop, from what I c’d pick up, an’ I
reckoned on bein’ a millionaire, ’cause I’d got
sevens for most o’ me money. I makes for a good
persition, an’ half-way roun’ I was floggin’ acause
I’d kep’ back the couple o’ quid when I might ha’
gotanotherfourteen. Then they started ter quicken
for the post. I was on the inside, movin’ as
yer like, with a feller in a green jacket on me
right. He jest stayed level with me, till about
a hundred yards from home. Then I gives me
mount the spur an’ starts ter shift. But, blime
if the bloke in the green jacket didn’t shift
too. I slung in my spurs an’ flogged somethin’
cruel, but that feller drew on me an’, s’help me,
without a word of a lie, he shoved past me an’
pipped me on the post. My word, I tell yer, I
was all out. I thought me heart was broken,
when I trotted back ter th’ stand. There was Sol
waitin’. ‘ Blime,’ he says, ‘ but you’re an artistic
finisher an’ no error. You’ve took ten years off
me life ter day,’ he says. ‘For a moment I
thought ye’d turned dawg on me.’ I was just goin’
ter speak ter him when up comes the owner o’ th’
horse with his fightin’ face cleared for action.
‘ Yer young whelp,’ ’e says, ‘yer turned me down.
Yer ruined me,’ he says, ‘but I’ll break yer
damned neck for yer.’ An’ with that he yanks
me off the horse, an’ in two twos there lie was
floggin’ me hell for leather with me own whip. I
promise yer it was a fair cow. It seemed nex’
year before they got in between him and me an’
saved me life, but I got the marks on me still.
Then they hauls me up before the stewards, and—
I’m telling yer the truth—they rubbed me out for
12 months for suspicious practices. What d’yer
think o’ that. When I had done me best ter win
an’ lost ten o’ the best o’ me own good red gold, I
gets stood down for a year. It was the last race
o’ the day, and the worst o’ the lot was when I
come ter'look for Sol an’ get my cut, he’d taken
shirrocker.
“ I sneaked back ter the pub. an’ tried ter look
calm an’ dignified, but who do yer think was in
the bar parlor when I strolls by an’ orders a long
whisky ? There was the copper I’d passed onthe
road talkin’ earnestly with me mother. That
broke me up. I wouldn’t ha’ minded if the ole
man had bin there, but there was the ole lady
cryin’ fit ter break her heart. Without a word of
a lie, it fair broke me up an’ I went back home
with her that night an’ ate mud. It wasn’t the
copper as put me away. It was the land-
lady o’ the pub. She’d dropped ter me in
one an’ wired down ter town. No, no more
jockeyin’ fer me. It’s a fine adventurous life, but
I’m lettin’ ’em have it. Th’ ole man got me inter
an office after that, an’ I’m goin’ strong. I’m
goin’ ter be an agent. There’s more in that in the
long run Yer just stand between th’ feller that
wants somethin’ an’ th’ feller that wants to sell
him somethin’, an’ yer let ’em have what s left
after yer got yer. whack. So long, chaps. I’ve
got an appointment with a bit o’ skirt at eight,
an’ this new suit oughter about paralyse her.”
C.N.
